The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Analyzing Conditional Puzzles: The False Conclusion Problem
Author presents a logical puzzle and its solution regarding conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a conditional syllogism yields a false conclusion due to an accidental major premise, and apply the distinction between luzumiyya and ittifaqiyya.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism is valid only if both premises are necessary entailments.

Conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i) are common in Islamic logic, but not every combination of conditionals yields a true conclusion. The author presents a puzzling case where two true conditionals produce a false conclusion. By analyzing this puzzle, you will learn to scrutinize the nature of the conditional relation—whether it is necessary (luzumiyya) or merely accidental (ittifaqiyya).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
The problem gives: (1) 'Whenever two is odd, it is a number.' (2) 'Whenever it is a number, it is even.' Write them as conditional statements.
These are both true statements: an odd number is a number; a number can be even or odd.
2
Derive the apparent conclusion
By transitivity of conditionals, one might conclude: 'Whenever two is odd, it is even.' But this conclusion is false because two cannot be odd.
The conclusion is false in reality, yet the premises seem true.
3
Check the necessity of each premise
Determine whether each conditional is a necessary entailment (luzumiyya) or accidental (ittifaqiyya). The first premise is necessary: being odd entails being a number. The second premise is accidental: being a number does not entail being even (since numbers can be odd).
The puzzle's solution is that the major premise is ittifaqiyya, not luzumiyya.
4
Apply the logical rule
For a valid conclusion in a conditional syllogism, both premises must be luzumiyya. If the major is ittifaqiyya, the conclusion does not follow necessarily.
This is why the puzzle arises—only necessary conditionals guarantee truth-preservation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على تفرده في النظر، واستقلاله في التحقيق ما ورد في هذا الكتاب، وينقله عنه الباحثون، وهو يقول: (... إنتاج اللُّزُومِيَّتَيْنِ لُزُومِيَّةٌ فِي الْأَوَّلِ بَيِّنٌ، وَهَهُنَا شَلٌّ ، وَهُوَ أَنَّهُ يَصْدُقُ كُلَّمَا كَانَ الْإِثْنَانِ فَرْدًا كَانَ عَدَدًا، وَكُلَّمَا كَانَ عَدَدًا كَانَ زَوْجًا، مَعَ كَذِبِ النَّتِيجَةِ. وَحَلُّهُ ـ كَمَا قِيلَ: مَنْعُ كَوْنِ الْكُبْرَى لُزُومِيَّةٌ ، وَإِنَّمَا هِيَ اتِّفَاقِيَّةٌ. وَيُجَابُ بِأَنَّ قَوْلَنَا كُلَّمَا كَانَ عَدَدًا كَانَ مَوْجُودًا لُزُومِيَّةٌ ، فَإِنَّ الْعَدَدِيَّةَ مُتَوَقِّفَةٌ عَلَى الْوُجُودِ، وَكَذَا كُلَّمَا كَانَ مَوْجُودًا
Among what indicates his uniqueness in inquiry and his independence in investigation is what is mentioned in this book, and researchers transmit from him, as he says: 'The production of the two conditional propositions: the first conditional is clear, and here there is a problem, namely that it is true that whenever two is odd, it is a number, and whenever it is a number, it is even, together with the conclusion being false. And its solution, as has been said: denying that the major is a conditional (luzumiyya), but rather it is accidental (ittifaqiyya). And it is responded that our saying 'whenever it is a number, it is existent' is a conditional, for number-ness depends on existence, and likewise whenever it is existent...'
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents a logical puzzle about conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i). The author transmits a statement from a researcher: 'The production of two conditionals' – meaning the conclusion derived from two conditional premises. The first conditional is clear, but here there is a problem. The example given: 'Whenever two is odd, it is a number; and whenever it is a number, it is even.' The conclusion would be 'Whenever two is odd, it is even,' which is false. The initial solution is to deny that the major premise 'Whenever a number, it is even' is a necessary conditional (luzumiyya); instead, it is an accidental conditional (ittifaqiyya) – i.e., not every number is even. However, a counter-response argues that the statement 'Whenever a number, it is existent' is indeed a necessary conditional because number-ness depends on existence; similarly, 'Whenever it is existent...' The passage breaks off, but the discussion illustrates the logical scrutiny of conditional entailments.

Key Terms
لزومية (luzumiyya)
اتفاقية (ittifaqiyya)
إنتاج اللزوميتين (intaj al-luzumiyyatayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the logical puzzle, why is the conclusion 'Whenever two is odd, it is even' false despite the premises being true?
A) The major premise is a necessary conditional but the minor is false
B) The major premise is only accidental, not necessary
C) The syllogism is in the wrong figure
D) The conclusion is actually true

Key Takeaway
A conditional syllogism is valid only when both premises are necessary conditionals (luzumiyya); accidental conditionals break the entailment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the conditional: 'If a contract includes interest, it is forbidden.' Is this a luzumiyya or ittifaqiyya? Explain using the definition of interest in Islamic law.
?
Analyze the following argument: 'If the prayer is invalid, then the ablution is null. But the prayer is valid. Therefore the ablution is not null.' Is this a valid conditional syllogism? Identify the fallacy if any.

The Syllogism

The Problem of Two Conditional Premises and Its Solutions
Presents a challenge to the principle that two necessary conditionals yield a necessary conclusion, with multiple solutions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical problem when two conditional premises appear to yield a false conclusion, and evaluate the proposed solutions.

Core Idea
Not all true conditionals are necessary; accidental concomitance can break syllogistic validity.

Sometimes, from two universal conditionals, a conclusion seems to follow, but the conclusion turns out false. This lesson examines such a puzzle from the Islamic logical tradition, where the premises 'Whenever two is odd, it is a number' and 'Whenever two is a number, it is even' lead to the false claim 'Whenever two is odd, it is even.' The passage discusses several solutions, including denying the necessity of one premise, denying the minor premise, and even denying the falsehood of the conclusion. By working through this puzzle, you will sharpen your understanding of necessary versus accidental implication (luzumiyya vs. ittifaqiyya), a key distinction in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Locate the two conditional premises in the Arabic text: 'Whenever two is odd, it is a number' (الصغرى) and 'Whenever it is a number, it is even' (الكبرى). The apparent conclusion is 'Whenever two is odd, it is even.' Write them out clearly in if-then form.
The premises are universal conditionals (كلما).
2
Recognize the problem
The conclusion is false because two being odd does not entail it is even; in fact, the antecedent 'two is odd' is impossible, but the conditional 'if two is odd then it is even' is true vacuously if the antecedent is impossible. However, the logicians here treat the conclusion as false, so something is wrong with the reasoning. Understand that the puzzle assumes the conditionals are true and necessary, yet the conclusion is false, which violates the principle that two necessary implications yield a necessary implication.
The puzzle challenges the principle that دو لزومية تنتج لزومية.
3
Examine the first solution (denying necessity of major)
The first solution is to deny that the major premise (الكبرى) is a necessary implication (لزومية); it is only an accidental coincidence (اتفاقية). The response is that 'Whenever it is a number, it exists' is necessary, and 'Whenever it exists, it is even' is necessary, so the conclusion would still follow, disproving the denial.
This solution attempts to break the necessity of the major premise.
4
Analyze the author's alternative solutions
The author offers two ways: (1) deny the minor premise (الصغرى) because impossible things are uncaused, so the implication from 'two is odd' to 'it is a number' is not necessary; (2) deny the major premise on the ground that the universal does not entail the particular (العام لا يستلزم الخاص). The author also notes that if one insists the major is necessary, the conclusion would be true, contradicting the assumption of falsehood.
These are deeper logical moves involving causation and the relationship between universal and particular.
5
Understand the final correct answer: denying the falsehood of the conclusion
The author concludes that the best solution is to deny that the conclusion is false, based on allowing entailment between two contradictories. In other words, if the premises are truly necessary, the conclusion must be true, even if it seems counterintuitive. This solution pushes the boundaries of what kind of entailment is acceptable.
This is a radical but technically consistent answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على تفرده في النظر، واستقلاله في التحقيق ما ورد في هذا الكتاب، وينقله عنه الباحثون، وهو يقول: (... إنتاج اللُّزُومِيَّتَيْنِ لُزُومِيَّةٌ فِي الْأَوَّلِ بَيِّنٌ، وَهَهُنَا شَلٌّ ، وَهُوَ أَنَّهُ يَصْدُقُ كُلَّمَا كَانَ الْإِثْنَانِ فَرْدًا كَانَ عَدَدًا، وَكُلَّمَا كَانَ عَدَدًا كَانَ زَوْجًا، مَعَ كَذِبِ النَّتِيجَةِ. وَحَلُّهُ ـ كَمَا قِيلَ: مَنْعُ كَوْنِ الْكُبْرَى لُزُومِيَّةٌ ، وَإِنَّمَا هِيَ اتَّفَاقِيَّةٌ. وَيُجَابُ بِأَنَّ قَوْلَنَا كُلَّمَا كَانَ عَدَدًا كَانَ مَوْجُودًا لُزُومِيَّةٌ ، فَإِنَّ الْعَدَدِيَّةَ مُتَوَقِّفَةٌ عَلَى الْوُجُودِ، وَكَذَا كُلَّمَا كَانَ مَوْجُودًا كَانَ زَوْجًا ، وَهُوَ مُنْتِجُ بِزَعْمِكُمْ لِمَا مَنَعْتُمْ. أَقُولُ: وَلَكَ أَنْ تَمْتَعَ الصُّغْرَى؛ فَإِنَّا لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ عَدَدِيَّةَ الإِثْنَيْنِ الْفَرْدِ مَعْلُولُ الوُجُود؛ لأَنَّ الْمُمْتَنِعَاتِ غَيْرُ مُعَلَّلَةٍ. وَأَنْ تَمْنَعَ الْكُبْرَى؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّ الْعَامَّ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ الْخَاصَّ ; لِأَنَّ وُجُودَ الاثْنَيْنِ الْفَرْدِ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ وُجُودِ الاِثْنَيْنِ. نَعَمْ، تَصْدُقُ اتَّفَاقِيَّةٌ ، وَلَوْ تَشَبَّتَ بِكَوْنِهَا مِنْ لَوَازِمِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ لَلَزِمَ صِدْقُ النَّتِيجَةِ الْمَفْرُوضِ كَذِبُهَا فِي هَذَا الْجَوَابِ ، فَتَأَمَّلْ . وَاخْتَارَ الرَّئيسُ فِي الْحَلِّ ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى رَأْيِهِ أَنَّ الصُّغْرَى كَاذِبَةٌ. أَقُولُ : قَوْلُنَا كُلَّمَا لَمْ يَكُن الإثْنَانِ عَدَدًا لَمْ يَكُنْ فَرْدًا يَصْدُقُ لُزُومِيَّةٌ ; فَإِنَّ انْتِفَاءَ الْعَامُ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِانْتِفَاءِ الْخَاصٌ ، وَهُوَ يَنْعَكِسُ بِعَكس النَّقِيض إِلَى تِلْكَ الصُّغْرَى ، وَمِنْهُ يَتَبَيَّنُ ضُعْفُ مَذْهَبِهِ، وَالْحَقُّ فِي الْجَوَابِ مَنْعُ كَذِبِ النَّتِيجَةِ، بِنَاءً عَلَى تَجْوِيزِ الِاسْتِلْزَامِ بَيْنَ الْمُتَنَافِيَيْنِ )).
Among what indicates his uniqueness in speculation and independence in investigation is what is contained in this book, which researchers quote from him, where he says: 'The production of two necessary implications (luzumiyyat) is itself a necessary implication in the first case, but here there is a problem: namely that it is true: "Whenever two is odd it is a number, and whenever it is a number it is even," while the conclusion is false. Its solution, as has been said, is to deny that the major premise is a necessary implication; rather it is an accidental coincidence. It is responded to by saying: Our statement "Whenever it is a number, it exists" is a necessary implication, for numberhood is dependent on existence, and likewise "Whenever it exists, it is even" is also a necessary implication, and this produces (the conclusion) according to your claim, since you denied (the first solution). I say: And you have the option to deny the minor premise; for we do not accept that the numberhood of the odd two is caused by existence, because impossible things are not caused. And you may deny the major premise, based on the principle that the universal does not entail the particular; for the existence of the odd two is part of the existence of two. Yes, the accidental premise is true, and if one clings to its being among the necessities of the essence, then the truth of the conclusion, which was assumed false, would be necessary in this answer, so reflect. And al-Ra'is (Ibn Sina) chose in the solution, based on his opinion, that the minor premise is false. I say: Our statement "Whenever two is not a number, it is not odd" is a true necessary implication, for the negation of the universal entails the negation of the particular, and it is converted by contraposition to that minor premise, and from this the weakness of his doctrine becomes clear. The truth in the answer is to deny the falsehood of the conclusion, based on allowing entailment between two contradictories.'
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents a logical puzzle. The author states that usually from two necessary conditional premises a necessary conclusion follows, but here there is a counterexample. The premises are: (1) Whenever two is odd, it is a number. (2) Whenever it is a number, it is even. The conclusion 'Whenever two is odd, it is even' would then follow, but that is false (since two is not odd? Actually two is even, so the antecedent 'whenever two is odd' is false for two, but the conditional is true vacuously? Wait, the issue is that 'two is odd' is impossible, but the conditionals are treated as universal generalizations. The problem is that from these two true conditionals, we get the false conclusion that whenever two is odd it is even, but that conclusion is false because if two is odd, it cannot be even; but the conditional would be vacuously true if the antecedent is impossible? The logicians here assume the conclusion is false. So something is wrong. The passage then discusses several solutions. First solution: deny that the major premise is a necessary implication (luzumiyya); it is merely accidental (ittifaqiyya). But the rebuttal says: 'whenever it is a number, it exists' is necessary, and 'whenever it exists, it is even' is also necessary, so they do produce the conclusion. Then the author offers two ways out: either deny the minor premise (that two being odd necessitates number) because impossible things are not caused, so the implication is not necessary, or deny the major premise based on the rule that the universal does not entail the particular (the existence of two is universal, but the odd two is a particular, so existence does not entail oddness). The author notes that if one insists on the major being necessary, the conclusion would be true, contradicting the assumed falsehood. Then Ibn Sina's solution is mentioned: the minor premise is false. The author then gives a counter: the contrapositive of the minor is true, which by conversion yields the minor, so Ibn Sina's position is weak. The final correct answer, according to the author, is to deny that the conclusion is false, i.e., allow that the conclusion is actually true if we accept entailment between contradictories.

Key Terms
لزومية (luzumiyya): necessary implication
اتفاقية (ittifaqiyya): accidental coincidence
عكس النقيض (aks al-naqid): contraposition

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first solution proposed in the passage, what was denied about the major premise?
A) That it is true
B) That it is a necessary implication (luzumiyya)
C) That it is a conditional
D) That it is a universal proposition

Key Takeaway
The puzzle reveals that not all true conditionals are necessary implications; accidental universals can lead to false conclusions if taken as necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar logical puzzle using Islamic concepts. For example, 'Whenever a thing is wine, it is intoxicating. Whenever it is intoxicating, it is forbidden.' Does the conclusion 'Whenever a thing is wine, it is forbidden' follow necessarily? Why or why not? Consider the possibility of the second premise being accidental.
?

But the world is not necessary in itself; therefore, the world is not eternal.' Identify the conditional form and evaluate whether the implications are necessary or accidental. Would the logicians of this lesson accept it?

The Syllogism

Refuting a Syllogism by Denying Premises and Contraposition
Author refutes an opponent's argument about a pair and universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to deny a syllogism's minor premise on causal grounds and use contraposition to derive a true implicative statement from a rejected conclusion.

Core Idea
Denying a premise or applying contraposition can dismantle a syllogism and expose hidden implications.

This passage presents a sophisticated logical debate about whether the existence of a pair necessarily implies the existence of an individual pair. The author, responding to an opponent, shows two ways to attack the syllogism: deny the minor premise because impossibilities are uncaused, or deny the major premise because the general does not entail the specific. Then, using contraposition, he demonstrates that a true implicative statement actually supports the opposite view, thereby weakening Ibn Sina's position. This lesson will guide you step by step through these logical moves.

Guided Path
1
Reconstruct the Opponent's Argument
The opponent likely argued: Every pair is a number (major); this thing is a pair (minor); therefore it is a number (conclusion). The author denies the conclusion.
The exact argument is abstract, but the structure is standard.
2
Identify the Two Ways to Deny Premises
First, deny the minor premise: reject that the 'twoness' of the individual pair is an effect of existence because impossibilities are not caused. Second, deny the major premise: the general (pair) does not entail the specific (this particular pair).
The author says 'You may deny the minor premise' and 'You may deny the major premise.'
3
Understand the Principle 'General Does Not Entail Specific'
The existence of a pair is a general fact; the existence of an individual pair is a specific instance. The specific is only part of the general, so the general does not guarantee the specific.
This principle is used to deny the major premise.
4
Apply Contraposition to Derive a True Implicative
Take the statement: 'If the two is not a number, then it is not a pair.' This is true because negation of the general entails negation of the specific. By contraposition, this converts to: 'If it is a pair, then the two is a number' – which is the minor premise the author rejected. This shows that if the conclusion were false, the minor premise would be false; but if we allow implication between contradictories, the conclusion may not be false.
The author uses 'aks al-naqid' (contraposition) to derive the minor premise.
5
Evaluate the Final Conclusion
The author concludes that the correct response is to deny the falsity of the conclusion, by allowing that two contradictory things can imply each other. This is a deep point about necessary concomitance.
The author says: 'The truth in the answer is to deny the falsity of the conclusion, based on allowing implication between two contradictories.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كَانَ زَوْجًا ، وَهُوَ مُنْتِجُ بِزَعْمِكُمْ لِمَا مَنَعْتُمْ. أَقُولُ: وَلَكَ أَنْ تَمْتَعَ الصُّغْرَى؛ فَإِنَّا لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ عَدَدِيَّةَ الإِثْنَيْنِ الْفَرْدِ مَعْلُولُ الوُجُود؛ لأَنَّ الْمُمْتَنِعَاتِ غَيْرُ مُعَلَّلَةٍ. وَأَنْ تَمْنَعَ الْكُبْرَى؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّ الْعَامَّ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ الْخَاصَّ ؛ لِأَنَّ وُجُودَ الاثْنَيْنِ الْفَرْدِ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ وُجُودِ الاِثْنَيْنِ. نَعَمْ، تَصْدُقُ اتَّفَاقِيَّةٌ ، وَلَوْ تَشَبَّتَ بِكَوْنِهَا مِنْ لَوَازِمِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ لَلَزِمَ صِدْقُ النَّتِيجَةِ الْمَفْرُوضِ كَذِبُهَا فِي هَذَا الْجَوَابِ ، فَتَأَمَّلْ . وَاخْتَارَ الرَّئيسُ فِي الْحَلِّ ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى رَأْيِهِ أَنَّ الصُّغْرَى كَاذِبَةٌ. أَقُولُ : قَوْلُنَا كُلَّمَا لَمْ يَكُن الإثْنَانِ عَدَدًا لَمْ يَكُنْ فَرْدًا يَصْدُقُ لُزُومِيَّةٌ ؛ فَإِنَّ انْتِفَاءَ الْعَامُ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِانْتِفَاءِ الْخَاصٌ ، وَهُوَ يَنْعَكِسُ بِعَكس النَّقِيض إِلَى تِلْكَ الصُّغْرَى ، وَمِنْهُ يَتَبَيَّنُ ضُعْفُ مَذْهَبِهِ، وَالْحَقُّ فِي الْجَوَابِ مَنْعُ كَذِبِ النَّتِيجَةِ، بِنَاءً عَلَى تَجْوِيزِ الاسْتِلْزَامِ بَيْنَ الْمُتَنَافِيَيْنِ )) .
It was a pair, and it produces according to your claim what you denied. I say: You may deny the minor premise, for we do not accept that the twoness of the individual pair is an effect of existence, because impossibilities are not caused. And you may deny the major premise, based on the fact that the general does not entail the specific, because the existence of the individual pair is part of the existence of the pair. Yes, an accidental truth holds, and if he clung to it being among the necessary concomitants of the essence, then the supposedly false conclusion would be true in this answer, so reflect. The Chief (Ibn Sina) chose in the solution, based on his opinion, that the minor premise is false. I say: Our statement 'Whenever the two is not a number, it is not a pair' is a true implicative statement, for the negation of the general entails the negation of the specific, and it converts by contraposition to that minor premise, and from this the weakness of his doctrine becomes clear. The truth in the answer is to deny the falsity of the conclusion, based on allowing implication between two contradictories.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a logical argument about a pair (zawj) and the number two. The author begins by presenting an opponent's claim: 'It was a pair, and it produces what you denied.' The author then offers two ways to refute this: first, reject the minor premise (i.e., deny that the twoness of an individual pair is an effect of existence, since impossibilities are uncaused); second, reject the major premise on the grounds that the general (existence as a pair) does not entail the specific (existence as an individual pair), because the specific is only part of the general. The author notes that an accidental truth might hold, but if one insists on necessary concomitance, the conclusion would be true—which shows the opponent's answer is self-defeating. Next, the author discusses the solution of the Chief (Ibn Sina), who considers the minor premise false. The author then presents his own counter: the implicative statement 'Whenever the two is not a number, it is not a pair' is true because negation of the general entails negation of the specific. By contraposition (aks al-naqid), this yields the minor premise, thus showing the weakness of Ibn Sina's position. The author concludes that the correct answer is to deny the falsity of the conclusion, by allowing implication between two contradictories (i.e., two mutually exclusive terms can still imply each other in certain contexts).

Key Terms
صغرى (sughra): Minor premise of a syllogism [Note: Stable term]
كبرى (kubra): Major premise of a syllogism [Note: Stable term]
لزومية (luzumiyya): Implicative (conditional) statement, expressing necessary entailment [Note: In logic, often contrasted with 'ittifaqiyya' (accidental)]
عكس النقيض (aks al-naqid): Contraposition: converting a conditional by negating and swapping terms [Note: Standard term in Arabic logic]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, what is one reason the author gives for denying the minor premise?
A) The general does not entail the specific.
B) Impossibilities are not caused.
C) Contraposition shows it is false.
D) The conclusion is false.

Key Takeaway
The passage illustrates that a syllogism can be refuted by challenging its premises on causal or logical grounds, and that contraposition can reveal hidden entailments that undermine a critic's position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the argument: 'All intoxicants are forbidden; wine is an intoxicant; therefore wine is forbidden.' Show two ways to deny the premises: one by denying the minor premise and one by denying the major premise, using plausible Islamic legal reasoning (e.g., citing evidence that wine is not intoxicating or that some intoxicants are permitted).
?

The First Objection: When Predicate is Existence
First objection: predicate existence leads to possibility issues

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the first objection against the definition of absolute necessary proposition and evaluate the response and counter-argument.

Core Idea
When the predicate is existence, absolute necessity collapses into specific possibility.

In this lesson, we dive into the first objection raised against the standard definition of the absolute necessary proposition. The objection targets cases where the predicate is existence itself, forcing us to reconsider the relationship between necessity and possibility. We'll follow the dialectical exchange: the objection, the response, a further objection, and a counterexample involving essential attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the standard definition being critiqued
Find the definition of 'absolute necessary proposition' in the source: a judgment with necessity as long as the subject's essence exists.
The definition is: 'the one in which it is judged with necessity of affirmation or negation, as long as the subject's essence exists.'
2
Extract the first objection
The objection says: if the predicate is existence itself, then necessity does not contradict specific possibility (imkan khass). Why? Because existence is not a necessary predicate for a subject that is not necessary.
Specific possibility: a proposition that is not necessary in any way.
3
Analyze the response
The response distinguishes between 'necessity in the time of existence' and 'necessity under the condition of existence'. The former allows for possibility when the subject doesn't exist, the latter does not.
This distinction attempts to save the definition.
4
Examine the further objection and counterexample
The further objection claims that this restricts absolute necessity to eternal propositions (azaliyya), making it not more general. But it is countered by essential attributes (dhatiyyat) like 'animality of man', which are necessary always, not just under the condition of existence.
If animality were contingent on existence, it would be maj'ula (contingent), which is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشْتَهَرَ تَعْرِيفُ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ بِأَنَّهَا الَّتِي يُحْكَمُ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ تُبُوتِ الْمَحْمُولِ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ أَوْ سَلْبِهِ عَنْهُ، مَا دَامَ ذَاتُ الْمَوْضُوعِ مَوْجُودًا. وَفِيهِ شَكٍّ مِنْ وَجْهَيْنِ ، الْأَوَّلُ : أَنَّهُ إِذَا كَانَ الْمَحْمُولُ هُوَ الْوُجُودُ لَزِمَ عَدَمُ مُنَافَاةِ الضَّرُورَةِ لِلْإِمْكَانِ الْخَاصٌ . وَأُجِيبَ بِالْفَرْقِ بَيْنَ الضَّرُورَةِ في زَمَانِ الْوُجُودِ وَبَيْنَهَا بِشَرْطِ الْوُجُودِ، وَأُورِدَ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ حَصْرُهَا فِي الْأَزَلِيَّةِ الَّتِي يُحْكَمُ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ النِّسْبَةِ أَزَلًا وَأَبَدًا، فَلَا تَكُونُ أَعَمَّ ؛ لأَنَّهُ لَمَّا لَمْ يَجِبْ وُجُودُ الْمَوْضُوعِ لَمْ يَجِبْ لَهُ شَيْءٌ فِي وَقْتِ وُجُودِهِ، وَنُوقِضَ بِثْبُوتِ الذَّاتِيَّاتِ ; فَإِنَّهُ ضَرُورِيُّ لِلذَّاتِ دَائِمًا لَا بِشَرْطِ الْوُجُودِ ، وَإِلَّا لَكَانَتْ حَيَوَانِيَّةُ الْإِنْسَانِ - مَثَلاً ـ مَجْعُولَةٌ ، فَافْهَمْ. الثَّانِي: السَّلْبُ مَا دَامَ الْوُجُودُ لَا يَصْدُقُ بِدُونِهِ، فَلَا تَكُونُ الصَّالِبَةُ أَعَمَّ، وَيَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يَصْدُقَ «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْعَنْقَاءِ بِإِنْسَانٍ بِالضَّرُورَةِ». وَأُجِيبَ بِأَنَّ مَا دَامَ ظَرْفٌ لِلنُّبُوتِ الَّذِي يَتَضَمَّنُهُ السَّلْبُ، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَجُوزُ صِدْقُهَا بِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَوْضُوعِ وَبِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَحْمُولِ ، إِمَّا فِي جَمِيعِ الْأَوْقَاتِ أَوْ بَعْضِهَا، نَحْوُ لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْقَمَرِ بِمُنْخَسِفَ بِالضَّرُورَةِ». وَفِيهِ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يُنَافِيَ الْإِمْكَانَ ; فَإِنَّ كُلَّ فَمَرٍ مُنْخَسِفٌ بِالْفِعْل صَادِقٌ ، فَيَصْدُقُ بِالْإِمْكَانِ ، وَيَبْطُلُ مَا قَالُوا: إِنَّ السَّالِبَةَ الضَّرُورِيَّةَ الْأَزَلِيَّةَ وَالْمُطْلَقَةَ مُتَسَاوِيَتَانِ ; فَإِنَّ سَلْبَ الْأَعَمِّ أَخَصُّ مِنْ سَلْبِ الْأَخَصُ . وَبِالْجُمْلَةِ يَلْزَمُ مَفَاسِدُ غَيْرُ عَدِيدَةٍ لَا تَخْفَى عَلَى الْمُتَدَرِّبِ. وَغَايَةُ مَا يُجَابُ بِهِ: أَنَّ الْوُجُودَ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الْمُحَقِّقِ وَالْمُقَدَّرِ ، وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ.
The common definition of the absolute necessary [proposition] is: that in which it is judged with the necessity of the affirmation of the predicate of the subject or its negation, as long as the essence of the subject exists. And there is a doubt in it from two aspects: The first: that if the predicate is existence, it follows that necessity does not contradict specific possibility. And it was answered by distinguishing between necessity in the time of existence and necessity under the condition of existence. And it was objected that it necessitates its restriction to the eternal [proposition] in which the necessity of the relation is judged eternally and perpetually, so it would not be more general; because when the existence of the subject is not necessary, nothing is necessary for it at the time of its existence. And it was countered by the subsistence of essential attributes; for it is necessary for the essence always, not under the condition of existence, otherwise the animality of man, for example, would be contingent. So understand. The second: the negation as long as existence does not hold without it, so the negative [proposition] would not be more general, and it follows that 'Nothing of the anqa' is a human being by necessity' would not hold. And it was answered that 'as long as' is a container for the judgment that the negation contains, and then it is permissible for it to hold by the non-existence of the subject and by the non-existence of the predicate, either in all times or some of them, such as 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed by necessity'. And there is in it that it would not contradict possibility; for every moon actually eclipsed is true, so it holds by possibility, and what they said becomes false: that the eternal necessary negative and the absolute are equivalent; for the negation of the more general is more specific than the negation of the specific. In summary, many corruptions follow that are not hidden from the trained student. The ultimate answer that can be given is that existence is more general than actual and estimated, and there is something to that effect.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of the standard definition of the absolute necessary proposition (daruriyya mutlaqa). The definition states that it is a proposition where the predicate is affirmed or negated with necessity as long as the subject's essence exists. The author presents two objections (shakk). The first objection: if the predicate is 'existence' itself, then the necessity would not contradict specific possibility (imkan khass) because existence is not necessary for a subject that is not necessary. The response distinguishes between necessity in the time of existence and necessity under the condition of existence. Then a further objection is raised: this would restrict the definition to eternal propositions (azaliyya) where the relation holds eternally, making it not more general; because if the subject's existence is not necessary, nothing is necessary for it at the time of its existence. But this is countered by the example of essential attributes (dhatiyyat) like 'animality of man', which is necessarily true of man always, not under the condition of existence; otherwise it would be contingent (maj'ula). The second objection deals with negative propositions: the phrase 'as long as existence' would make the negative proposition not more general, and would invalidate the necessity of 'No anqa' is a man' (since the subject does not exist). The response reinterprets 'as long as' as a container for the judgment, allowing the negative to hold even when the subject does not exist, as in 'No moon is eclipsed by necessity'. But this leads to another problem: it would not contradict possibility, and it would also show that the eternal necessary negative and the absolute are not equivalent, because the negation of a more general term is more specific. The passage concludes that many problems remain, and the best response is that existence is broader than actual and estimated existence, but this itself is problematic. This is a sophisticated dialectical exchange typical of later commentaries (hashiya).

Key Terms
ضَرُورِيَّة مُطْلَقَة
إِمْكَان خَاص
ذَاتِيَّات
أَزَلِيَّة

The Second Objection: Negatives and 'As Long as Existence'
Second objection: negatives and the condition of existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the second objection regarding negative absolute necessary propositions and assess the proposed solutions and their consequences.

Core Idea
The phrase 'as long as existence' makes negative absolute propositions too restrictive and leads to contradictions.

The second objection targets negative absolute necessary propositions. The phrase 'as long as existence' seems to make these propositions dependent on the subject's existence, which causes problems for universally true negatives like 'No anqa' (a mythical bird) is a man.' We will dissect the objection, the response reinterpreting 'as long as', and the further complications regarding possibility and the equivalence of eternal and absolute negatives.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second objection
The objection states: For a negative proposition like 'No thing is a man' to be necessary, the subject must exist. But if the subject does not exist, the negative cannot hold, making it not universally true. The example of 'anqa' (a non-existent creature) illustrates this.
The phrase 'as long as existence' is seen as a condition that fails when the subject doesn't exist.
2
Examine the response's reinterpretation
The response says 'as long as' is a container (zarf) for the judgment, not a condition. This allows the negative to hold even when the subject does not exist, as in 'No moon is eclipsed by necessity' (eclipses are not necessary).
The example shows a temporary condition that is not necessary.
3
Identify the further problem about possibility
The response leads to another issue: if the negative can hold by possibility (like the moon not being eclipsed), then it does not contradict possibility. This undermines the distinction between necessary and possible negatives.
The text says: 'for every moon actually eclipsed is true, so it holds by possibility.'
4
Evaluate the claim about equivalence
The objector then argues that the eternal necessary negative and the absolute negative are not equivalent, because the negation of the more general is more specific. This refutes the claim that they are equal.
The author concludes that many corruptions follow, and the best answer (existence broader than actual and estimated) is itself problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشْتَهَرَ تَعْرِيفُ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ بِأَنَّهَا الَّتِي يُحْكَمُ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ تُبُوتِ الْمَحْمُولِ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ أَوْ سَلْبِهِ عَنْهُ، مَا دَامَ ذَاتُ الْمَوْضُوعِ مَوْجُودًا. وَفِيهِ شَكٍّ مِنْ وَجْهَيْنِ ، الْأَوَّلُ : أَنَّهُ إِذَا كَانَ الْمَحْمُولُ هُوَ الْوُجُودُ لَزِمَ عَدَمُ مُنَافَاةِ الضَّرُورَةِ لِلْإِمْكَانِ الْخَاصٌ . وَأُجِيبَ بِالْفَرْقِ بَيْنَ الضَّرُورَةِ في زَمَانِ الْوُجُودِ وَبَيْنَهَا بِشَرْطِ الْوُجُودِ، وَأُورِدَ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ حَصْرُهَا فِي الْأَزَلِيَّةِ الَّتِي يُحْكَمُ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ النِّسْبَةِ أَزَلًا وَأَبَدًا، فَلَا تَكُونُ أَعَمَّ ؛ لأَنَّهُ لَمَّا لَمْ يَجِبْ وُجُودُ الْمَوْضُوعِ لَمْ يَجِبْ لَهُ شَيْءٌ فِي وَقْتِ وُجُودِهِ، وَنُوقِضَ بِثْبُوتِ الذَّاتِيَّاتِ ; فَإِنَّهُ ضَرُورِيُّ لِلذَّاتِ دَائِمًا لَا بِشَرْطِ الْوُجُودِ ، وَإِلَّا لَكَانَتْ حَيَوَانِيَّةُ الْإِنْسَانِ - مَثَلاً ـ مَجْعُولَةٌ ، فَافْهَمْ. الثَّانِي: السَّلْبُ مَا دَامَ الْوُجُودُ لَا يَصْدُقُ بِدُونِهِ، فَلَا تَكُونُ الصَّالِبَةُ أَعَمَّ، وَيَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يَصْدُقَ «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْعَنْقَاءِ بِإِنْسَانٍ بِالضَّرُورَةِ». وَأُجِيبَ بِأَنَّ مَا دَامَ ظَرْفٌ لِلنُّبُوتِ الَّذِي يَتَضَمَّنُهُ السَّلْبُ، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَجُوزُ صِدْقُهَا بِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَوْضُوعِ وَبِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَحْمُولِ ، إِمَّا فِي جَمِيعِ الْأَوْقَاتِ أَوْ بَعْضِهَا، نَحْوُ لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْقَمَرِ بِمُنْخَسِفَ بِالضَّرُورَةِ». وَفِيهِ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يُنَافِيَ الْإِمْكَانَ ; فَإِنَّ كُلَّ فَمَرٍ مُنْخَسِفٌ بِالْفِعْل صَادِقٌ ، فَيَصْدُقُ بِالْإِمْكَانِ ، وَيَبْطُلُ مَا قَالُوا: إِنَّ السَّالِبَةَ الضَّرُورِيَّةَ الْأَزَلِيَّةَ وَالْمُطْلَقَةَ مُتَسَاوِيَتَانِ ; فَإِنَّ سَلْبَ الْأَعَمِّ أَخَصُّ مِنْ سَلْبِ الْأَخَصُ . وَبِالْجُمْلَةِ يَلْزَمُ مَفَاسِدُ غَيْرُ عَدِيدَةٍ لَا تَخْفَى عَلَى الْمُتَدَرِّبِ. وَغَايَةُ مَا يُجَابُ بِهِ: أَنَّ الْوُجُودَ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الْمُحَقِّقِ وَالْمُقَدَّرِ ، وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ.
The common definition of the absolute necessary [proposition] is: that in which it is judged with the necessity of the affirmation of the predicate of the subject or its negation, as long as the essence of the subject exists. And there is a doubt in it from two aspects: The first: that if the predicate is existence, it follows that necessity does not contradict specific possibility. And it was answered by distinguishing between necessity in the time of existence and necessity under the condition of existence. And it was objected that it necessitates its restriction to the eternal [proposition] in which the necessity of the relation is judged eternally and perpetually, so it would not be more general; because when the existence of the subject is not necessary, nothing is necessary for it at the time of its existence. And it was countered by the subsistence of essential attributes; for it is necessary for the essence always, not under the condition of existence, otherwise the animality of man, for example, would be contingent. So understand. The second: the negation as long as existence does not hold without it, so the negative [proposition] would not be more general, and it follows that 'Nothing of the anqa' is a human being by necessity' would not hold. And it was answered that 'as long as' is a container for the judgment that the negation contains, and then it is permissible for it to hold by the non-existence of the subject and by the non-existence of the predicate, either in all times or some of them, such as 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed by necessity'. Commentary

The second objection deals with negative propositions: the phrase 'as long as existence' would make the negative proposition not more general, and would invalidate the necessity of 'No anqa' is a man' (since the subject does not exist). D) No angel is a devil by necessity

Key Takeaway
The second objection demonstrates that the phrase 'as long as existence' in the definition of absolute necessity creates serious logical difficulties for negative propositions, particularly regarding non-existent subjects and the distinction between necessity and possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'No creature is eternally self-sufficient'. Identify whether this is an absolute necessary negative or an eternal necessary negative according to the definitions. Use the logical tools from this lesson to explain.
?
Rewrite the second objection using a contemporary Islamic example (e.g., 'No soul is unjust by necessity'). Then evaluate whether the same problems arise.

The Claim of Partial Necessity and Its Proof
Author discusses the claim of partial necessity and its proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the argument for partial necessity and identify the logical structure of the proof, including the role of the third figure.

Core Idea
Necessity means impossibility of separation, not causation.

This lesson examines a controversial claim: that there is a minimal necessity between any two things, even contradictories. The author presents a proof using a conditional syllogism and then engages with objections. You will learn to dissect the logical argument and understand the key concept of partial necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim of partial necessity
The claim is that for any two things, a positive conditional holds: if the whole (sum) is realized, each part is realized. Even contradictories are included.
This implies that negative conditionals are false; only affirmative true or universal coincidental are possible.
2
Locate the proof in the third figure
The proof uses two conditional premises: 'Whenever the sum is realized, one part is realized' and 'Whenever the sum is realized, the other part is realized.' The conclusion is that the sum implies both parts.
In categorical terms, this is a third-figure syllogism with the middle term 'realized sum'.
3
Understand the objection about causation
Some object that the whole implies a part only if the part has a causal role; the other part is extraneous. The author replies that necessity does not require causation, only inseparability.
This is a critical refinement: the nature of necessity is debated.
4
Consider the Shaykh's response and further objection
The Shaykh argues that if you assume the antecedent with the non-existence of the consequent, you get the consequent's non-existence, proving the implication. Another objection says the universality fails if the whole is impossible; the author counters by restricting to actual existents.
The debate shows the complexity of modal logic in Islamic thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السَّادِسُ : أَنَّ مِنْهُمْ مَنِ ادَّعَى اللُّزُومَ الْجُزْئِيَّ بَيْنَ كُلِّ أَمْرَيْنِ، حَتَّى النَّقِيضَيْنِ، فَلَا يَصْدُقُ الصَّالِبَةُ اللُّزُومِيَّةُ ، بَل الْمُوجَبَةُ الْحَقِيقِيَّةُ، بَل الاتِّفَاقِيَّةُ الكلياتُ ، وَبُرْهِنَ عَلَيْهِ بِالشَّكْلِ الثَّالِثِ ، وَهُوَ كُلَّمَا تَحَقَّقَ مَجْمُوعُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ تَحَقَّقَ أَحَدُهُمَا ، وَكُلَّمَا تَحَقَّقَ الْمَجْمُوعُ تَحَقَّقَ الْآخَرُ ، بَلْ بِالشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ بِعَكْس الصُّغُرَى، قَرَامَ التَّفَصَّيَ عَنْهُ بَعْضُ الْمُحَفِّقِينَ ، بِأَنَّ الْمَجْمُوعَ إِنَّمَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ الْجُزْء لَوْ كَانَ لِكُلِّ مِنَ الْأَجْزَاءِ مَدْخَلٌ في الاِقْتِضَاءِ، وَمِنَ الْبَيِّنِ أَنَّ الْجُزْءَ الْآخَرَ لَا دَخْلَ لَهُ فِيهِ، بَلْ يَجْرِي مَجْرَى الْحَشْوِ. وَفِيهِ: أَنَّ اللُّزُومَ لَا يَقْتَضِي الاِقْتِضَاءَ وَالتَّأْثِيرَ؛ فَإِنَّهُ امْتِنَاعُ الانْفِكَاكِ، فَارْتِبَاطُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ بِهَذَا النَّمَطِ كَافٍ فِيهِ. قَالَ الشَّيْخُ إِذَا فُرِضَ الْمُقَدَّمُ مَعَ عَدَمِ التَّالِي يَسْتَلْزِمُ عَدَمَ التَّالِي، فَقَالَ بِاسْتِلْزَامِ الْمَجْمُوعِ الْجُزْء. وَبَعْضُهُمْ بِأَنَّا لَا نُسَلِّمُ تِلْكَ الْكُلِّيَّةَ؛ لِجَوَازِ اسْتِحَالَةِ الْمَجْمُوعِ ، فَعَلَى تَقْدِيرِ ثُبُوتِهِ يَنْفَكُ عَنِ الْجُزْءِ، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ. بقي شَيْءٌ، وَهُوَ أَنَّا نَدْعِي ذَلِكَ اللُّزُومَ بَيْنَ كُلِّ أَمْرَيْنِ وَاقِعِيَّيْنِ ، وَنُبَرْهِنُ . عَلَيْهِ بِأَخْذِ تِلْكَ الْكُلِّيَّةِ بِاعْتِبَارِ التَّقَادِيرِ الْوَاقِعِيَّةِ ، فَبَطَلَ الاتِّفَاقِيَّةُ الْكُلِّيَةُ الْخَاصَّةُ، فَتَأَمَّلْ . فَضْل : كُلُّ أَمْرَيْنِ أَحَدُهُمَا رَفْعُ الْآخَرِ فَهُمَا نَقِيضَانِ ، وَمِنْ ثَمَّ قَالُوا: إِنَّ التَّناقُضَ مِنَ النَّسَبِ الْمُتَكَرِّرَةِ ، وَإِنَّ لِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ نَقِيضا وَاحِدًا. وَمَا قِيلَ: إِنَّ التَّصَوُّرَاتِ لَا نَقَائِض لَهَا فَهُوَ بِمَعْنِّى آخَرَ .
The sixth: That among them is one who claimed partial necessity between any two things, even contradictories, so the negative necessary [proposition] is not true, but rather the positive true, rather the universal coincidental. And it was proved by the third figure: 'Whenever the sum of the two things is realized, one of them is realized, and whenever the sum is realized, the other is realized.' Yes, by the first figure through conversion of the minor. Some verifiers attempted to escape from it, [arguing] that the whole only implies the part if each of the parts has a role in necessitation, and it is evident that the other part has no role in it, but rather is like a filler. And [the reply] to that: necessity does not entail causation and effect; for it is impossibility of separation, so the connection of the two things in this manner is sufficient for it. The Shaykh said: if the antecedent is assumed with the non-existence of the consequent, it entails the non-existence of the consequent, so he said about the implication of the whole for the part. And some [argued] that we do not accept that universality; because of the possibility of the whole being impossible, so on its supposed existence it would be separate from the part, and that is the truth. Something remains: that we claim that [partial] necessity between any two actually existing things, and we prove it by taking that universality with consideration of actual suppositions, so the particular universal coincidental is invalidated. So ponder. Benefit: Any two things, one of which is the removal of the other, are contradictories. Hence they said that contradiction is among the repeated relations, and that everything has one contradictory. And what is said that concepts have no contradictories is in another sense.

Commentary
This passage discusses the sixth point: the claim that there is a partial necessity (luzum juz'i) between any two things, even contradictories—meaning that the negative necessary conditional (salb luzumiyya) is never true, and only the affirmative true or universal coincidental holds. The proof uses the third figure: if the whole of two things exists, each exists. But this is criticized: the whole implies a part only if each part is causally involved; otherwise the other part is extraneous. The reply is that necessity does not require causation, only inseparability. The Shaykh (maybe a specific scholar) argues that if you assume the antecedent with the nonexistence of the consequent, you get the consequent's nonexistence, thus the whole implies the part. Others reject the universality because the whole may be impossible. The author then says we claim this necessity between any two actual existents, proving it with actual suppositions, thus invalidating the particular universal coincidental. A separate benefit (Fadl) defines contradiction as two things where one is the removal of the other, noting that contradiction is a reciprocal relation and each thing has one contradictory; concepts (tasawwurat) have no contradictors in the same sense.

الشكل الثالث
الموجبة الحقيقية
الاتفاقية الكليات

C) The whole must be actually existing.

Key Takeaway
Partial necessity is defined by the impossibility of separation, not by causal connection; the proof uses a compound conditional syllogism in the third figure.

The Definition of Contradiction and Its Properties
Author defines contradiction and its properties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define contradiction in logical terms and identify its properties: reciprocal relation and uniqueness.

Core Idea
Contradiction is a mutual relation; each thing has exactly one contradictory.

The passage concludes with a benefit (fadl) that defines contradiction and clarifies its properties. This lesson unpacks that definition, explaining why contradiction is a repeated relation and why each thing has a single contradictory. You will also learn the exception for concepts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of contradiction
Two things are contradictories if one is the removal (raf') of the other. This means they are logical opposites where one exactly negates the other.
This applies to propositions, not concepts.
2
Recognize that contradiction is a reciprocal relation
If A is the contradictory of B, then B is the contradictory of A. This is why it is called a 'repeated relation' – it holds symmetrically.
The Arabic term is 'al-nasab al-mutakarrir'.
3
Identify the uniqueness claim
Each thing has exactly one contradictory. This follows from the nature of bivalence: a proposition can only have one negation that excludes it.
There is no double negation in the sense of multiple distinct contradictories.
4
Clarify the exception for concepts
Some said concepts (tasawwurat) have no contradictories. The author notes this is true in a different sense: concepts are not truth-evaluable; only propositions are subject to contradiction.
This avoids confusion between mental concepts and assertions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Syllogism

10 min
The Claim of Universal Partial Entailment: Proof and Refutation
Author examines the claim of universal partial entailment and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the syllogistic argument for universal partial entailment using the first and third figures, and analyze the logical objections raised against it.

Core Idea
The claim that every two things partially entail each other is 'proved' by a third figure syllogism, but the rebuttal reveals the fallacy of conflating sum-part correlation with causation.

In this dense passage, the author presents a controversial logical claim: that between any two things, even contradictories, there is a partial entailment (اللزوم الجزئي). The proponents attempt to prove this using the third figure syllogism, and alternatively via the first figure after conversion. However, some verificators object by pointing out the redundancy of one part in the sum. The author then clarifies that entailment is not causal implication but merely impossibility of separation, and the claim is only about actual existents. We will unpack the logical structure of the argument and the critiques.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and its motivation
The claim is that between every two things, even contradictories, there is a partial entailment. This denies universal privative entailment (الصالبة اللزومية) and asserts only affirmative accidental universal. Recognize that this is a strong claim about logical relatedness.
The term 'partial entailment' (اللزوم الجزئي) means that one thing follows from another at least sometimes, not always.
2
Extract the third figure argument
The proponents argue: 'Whenever the sum of two things is realized, one of them is realized; and whenever the sum is realized, the other is realized.' In syllogistic form, take the sum as middle term. Formalize: All sum entails A, All sum entails B, therefore Some A entails B (or some B entails A). This is a valid third figure mood (Darapti).
The text explicitly says 'وَبُرْهِنَ عَلَيْهِ بِالشَّكْلِ الثَّالِثِ'.
3
Formalize the alternative first figure proof
The text mentions that the same conclusion can be reached via the first figure by converting the minor premise. The minor 'All sum is B' becomes 'Some B is sum'. Then we have: All sum is A, Some B is sum, therefore Some B is A. This is a valid first figure mood (Darii).
Conversion (عكس) swaps subject and predicate while preserving truth (for universal affirmatives it gives a particular).
4
Analyze the first rebuttal
Some verificators object: a sum entails a part only if that part plays a role in the implication; a redundant part cannot be implied. The author counters by clarifying that entailment (لزوم) is not causal implication (اقتضاء) but merely impossibility of separation (امتناع الانفكاك). Thus, the mere fact that the two parts are together in the sum suffices for entailment.
This rebuttal hinges on the definition of entailment. The author insists it is a logical relation, not a causal one.
5
Evaluate the final resolution
The author concedes that the universal accidental claim (الاتفاقية الكلية) is false if applied to impossible sums (e.g., contradictories that cannot coexist). However, he maintains that for actual existents (things that can coexist), the entailment holds when considering actual possibilities. This restricts the claim from absolute universality to a conditioned one.
The passage ends with 'فتأمل' (reflect), indicating the author expects the reader to ponder the nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السَّادِسُ : أَنَّ مِنْهُمْ مَنِ ادَّعَى اللُّزُومَ الْجُزْئِيَّ بَيْنَ كُلِّ أَمْرَيْنِ، حَتَّى النَّقِيضَيْنِ، فَلَا يَصْدُقُ الصَّالِبَةُ اللُّزُومِيَّةُ ، بَل الْمُوجَبَةُ الْحَقِيقِيَّةُ، بَل الاتِّفَاقِيَّةُ الكلياتُ ، وَبُرْهِنَ عَلَيْهِ بِالشَّكْلِ الثَّالِثِ ، وَهُوَ كُلَّمَا تَحَقَّقَ مَجْمُوعُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ تَحَقَّقَ أَحَدُهُمَا ، وَكُلَّمَا تَحَقَّقَ الْمَجْمُوعُ تَحَقَّقَ الْآخَرُ ، بَلْ بِالشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ بِعَكْس الصُّغُرَى، قَرَامَ التَّفَصَّيَ عَنْهُ بَعْضُ الْمُحَفِّقِينَ ، بِأَنَّ الْمَجْمُوعَ إِنَّمَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ الْجُزْء لَوْ كَانَ لِكُلِّ مِنَ الْأَجْزَاءِ مَدْخَلٌ في الاِقْتِضَاءِ، وَمِنَ الْبَيِّنِ أَنَّ الْجُزْءَ الْآخَرَ لَا دَخْلَ لَهُ فِيهِ، بَلْ يَجْرِي مَجْرَى الْحَشْوِ.
وَفِيهِ: أَنَّ اللُّزُومَ لَا يَقْتَضِي الاِقْتِضَاءَ وَالتَّأْثِيرَ؛ فَإِنَّهُ امْتِنَاعُ الانْفِكَاكِ،
فَارْتِبَاطُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ بِهَذَا النَّمَطِ كَافٍ فِيهِ.
قَالَ الشَّيْخُ إِذَا فُرِضَ الْمُقَدَّمُ مَعَ عَدَمِ التَّالِي يَسْتَلْزِمُ عَدَمَ التَّالِي، فَقَالَ بِاسْتِلْزَامِ الْمَجْمُوعِ الْجُزْء. وَبَعْضُهُمْ بِأَنَّا لَا نُسَلِّمُ تِلْكَ الْكُلِّيَّةَ؛ لِجَوَازِ اسْتِحَالَةِ الْمَجْمُوعِ ، فَعَلَى تَقْدِيرِ ثُبُوتِهِ يَنْفَكُ عَنِ الْجُزْءِ، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ.
بقي شَيْءٌ، وَهُوَ أَنَّا نَدْعِي ذَلِكَ اللُّزُومَ بَيْنَ كُلِّ أَمْرَيْنِ وَاقِعِيَّيْنِ ، وَنُبَرْهِنُ . عَلَيْهِ بِأَخْذِ تِلْكَ الْكُلِّيَّةِ بِاعْتِبَارِ التَّقَادِيرِ الْوَاقِعِيَّةِ ، فَبَطَلَ الاتِّفَاقِيَّةُ الْكُلِّيَةُ الْخَاصَّةُ،
فَتَأَمَّلْ .
The sixth: That among them is one who claimed partial entailment between every two things, even contradictories, so the universal privative entailment is not true, rather the affirmative real [entailment], rather the accidental universal [entailment]. And it is proved by the third figure: whenever the sum of the two things is realized, one of them is realized, and whenever the sum is realized, the other is realized. Rather by the first figure with conversion of the minor. Some verificators escaped from it by [saying] that the sum entails a part only if each of the parts has a role in the implication, and it is evident that the other part has no role in it, rather it is redundant. Against it: that entailment does not require implication and effect; for it is impossibility of separation, so the connection of two things in this manner is sufficient in it. The shaykh said: if the antecedent is assumed with the non-existence of the consequent, it entails the non-existence of the consequent, so he said regarding the entailment of the sum of the part. And some [said] that we do not accept that universality; because of the possibility of the impossibility of the sum, so on the assumption of its existence it separates from the part, and that is the truth. Something remains: that we claim that entailment between every two actual things, and we prove it by taking that universal with respect to actual possibilities, so the specific accidental universal is falsified. So reflect.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage examines a logical claim: that between any two things (even contradictories) there is a partial entailment (اللزوم الجزئي). The author states that if this were true, then the universal privative entailment (الصالبة اللزومية) would be false, and only the affirmative real or accidental universal would hold. The claim is 'proved' using the third figure syllogism: whenever the sum of two things is realized, each part is realized; thus there is entailment. Also via the first figure by converting the minor premise. Some verificators rebut by arguing that a sum entails a part only if each part contributes to the implication; but one part is redundant, so the sum does not entail both parts simultaneously. The author counters that entailment does not mean causal implication, only that separation is impossible; thus such a connection suffices. Then a shaykh adds: if we assume the antecedent and the non-existence of the consequent, it entails the non-existence of the consequent, so the sum entails the part. Others reject the universal because the sum may be impossible; if it exists, the part may separate. Finally, the author says he claims entailment only between actual things, using a universal over actual possibilities, which falsifies the specific accidental universal. The passage ends with 'reflect' (فتأمل).

الشكل الثالث
الشكل الأول

Key Takeaway
The claim of universal partial entailment is logically supported by syllogistic proofs, but the objections reveal that entailment must be understood as impossibility of separation, not causal efficacy, and its universality is restricted to actual existents.

The Syllogism

Definition of Qiyas and Essential Entailment
Author defines qiyas as the primary means to assent and explains essential entailment with the counterexample of qiyas al-musawa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a valid qiyas (syllogism) and distinguish between essential entailment (luzūm dhātī) and arguments that require an extraneous premise, using the example of qiyas al-musāwāh.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism needs no outside help: the conclusion follows by itself.

The author begins by stating that the means to assent (taṣdīq) are proof and evidence, which must involve inclusion or entailment. The primary mean is qiyas, which the author defines as a composite statement from which another follows necessarily by itself. This essential entailment excludes arguments that require an extra premise. To illustrate, the author gives an example of qiyas al-musāwāh (syllogism of equality), which needs an additional premise to be valid. In this lesson, we will unpack this definition and see why qiyas al-musāwāh falls short of essential entailment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key elements of the definition of qiyas
The author says qiyas is a statement composed of propositions (qadaya) from which another statement follows necessarily by itself (li-dhatiha). Note the two conditions: composition and self-sufficiency.
2
Understand what 'essential entailment' excludes
The condition 'by itself' excludes arguments that need an extraneous premise (muqaddima ajnabiyya). This is the core of essential entailment (luzum dhati).
3
Examine the example of qiyas al-musawa
The author provides: A is equal to B, B is equal to C. This seems to imply A = C, but without the additional premise 'Every equal of the equal of C is equal to C', the conclusion does not follow necessarily. So this is not a qiyas with essential entailment.
The extra premise is the key difference.
4
Formalize the argument using logical notation
The author uses variables (أ،ب،ج). We see that the argument requires an extra premise to be valid.
The logical notation panel below shows the full form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَضْل الْمُوصِلُ إِلَى التَّصْدِيقِ حُجَّةٌ وَدَلِيلٌ ، وَلَيْسَ بُدٌ مِنْ مُنَاسَبَةٍ بِاشْتِمَالٍ، أَوِ اسْتِلْزَامٍ، وَيَنْحَصِرُ فِي ثَلَاثَةٍ، وَالْعُمْدَةُ الْقِيَاسُ، وَهُوَ قَوْلٌ مُؤَلَّفٌ مِنْ قَضَايَا ، يَلْزَمُ عَنْهَا لِذَاتِهَا قَوْلُ آخَرُ. وَأَخْرَجُوا بِاللُّزُومِ الذَّاتِي مَا يَكُونُ لِمُقَدِّمَةٍ أَجْنَبِيَّة. أَمَّا غَيْرُ لاَزِمَةٍ، كَمَا فِي قِيَاسِ الْمُسَاوَاةِ، وَهُوَ الْمُرَكَّبُ مِنْ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ مُتَعَلِّقُ مَحْمُولِ الْأُولَى مَوْضُوعُ الْأُخْرَى ، نَحْوُ (أ) مُسَاءٍ لِ(بَ)، وَ(بَ) مُسَاءٍ لِ (ج)، يَلْزَمُ مِنْهُ بِوَاسِطَةِ (كُلُّ مُسَاوِ لِمُسَاوِ لِ(ج) مُسَاوِ لِ(ج)» «أَنَّ (أ) مُسَاوِ
Chapter: That which leads to assent (taṣdīq) is proof and evidence, and it must involve either inclusion (ishtimāl) or entailment (istilzām), and it is limited to three. The main one is qiyas (syllogism), and it is a statement composed of propositions from which, by themselves, another statement follows necessarily. And they excluded by 'essential entailment' that which requires an extraneous premise. As for a non-necessary [syllogism], like qiyas al-musāwāh (syllogism of equality), which is composed of two propositions where the predicate of the first is related to the subject of the second, such as 'A is equal to B, and B is equal to C', from which it follows by means of 'Every equal to the equal of C is equal to C' that 'A is equal to C'.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage defines the means to assent (taṣdīq) as proof (ḥujja) and evidence (dalīl), which must involve either inclusion (ishtimāl) or entailment (istilzām). These means are limited to three types, but the author only mentions qiyas as the primary one. Qiyas is defined as a composite statement made up of propositions from which another statement follows necessarily by itself (li-dhātihā). The phrase 'by themselves' excludes cases where an extraneous premise is needed. The author then illustrates a non-necessary syllogism, specifically qiyas al-musāwāh (syllogism of equality), where the predicate of the first premise (B) is related to the subject of the second (B). The example is: A is equal to B, and B is equal to C, which implies A is equal to C, but only with the help of an additional premise (every equal of the equal of C is equal to C). This shows that qiyas al-musāwāh requires an extra premise, hence it is not a 'necessary' syllogism in the strict sense.

Key Terms
الْقِيَاس
قِيَاس الْمُسَاوَاة
لُزُوم ذَاتِي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is composed of three propositions
B) It does not require an extraneous premise
C) It uses equality (musāwāh)
D) It leads to assent (taṣdīq)

Key Takeaway
Essential entailment requires the conclusion to follow from the premises alone, without any external premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In fiqh, a jurist argues: 'This sale is invalid because it involves gharar (uncertainty), and all sales involving gharar are invalid.' Is this a complete qiyas with essential entailment? What extraneous premise might be needed to make it valid?

Contradictory Premises and Contrapositive Inference
Example of contradictory premise inference and nature of luzum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the inference from a syllogism with contradictory premises using contrapositive and distinguish between real implication and implication according to knowledge.

Core Idea
Contradictory premises can still yield a valid conclusion through contrapositive; the nature of implication depends on one's school.

The author presents a puzzling case where two premises have contradictory terms, yet a conclusion follows. This example is used to illustrate how contrapositive ('aks al-naqid) transforms a premise, and to explore what 'implication' (luzum) really means in logic. Understanding this deepens your grasp of immediate inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Find the two premises in the example: (1) 'The part of the substance, its removal necessitates the removal of the substance.' (2) 'Everything that is not a substance, its removal does not necessitate the removal of the substance.' Notice the terms 'part of substance' and 'not substance' are contradictory.
2
Apply contrapositive to the second premise
The conclusion that 'the part of the substance is a substance' is derived by contrapositive (عكس النقيض) of the second premise. That is, from 'non-substance does not necessitate removal of substance' we infer 'whatever necessitates removal of substance is a substance'.
Contrapositive of 'if P then Q' is 'if not Q then not P'.
3
Combine with the first premise
The first premise states that the part of the substance does necessitate removal of substance. Combining with the contrapositive of the second premise, we conclude that the part of the substance is a substance.
This is a valid immediate inference, similar to direct conversion.
4
Analyze the author's remarks on implication
The author notes that implication (luzum) can be taken as real (fi nafs al-amr) or according to knowledge (bi hasab al-'ilm). The more famous view is the latter, meaning subsequence after awareness of inclusion. Different schools explain this subsequence as habit (ada), generation (tawlid), or preparation (i'dad).
This meta-logical discussion affects how we understand the necessity in logical implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا لَازِمَةٌ مُتَنَافِضَةٌ فِي الْحُدُودِ، كَمَا تَقُولُ جُزْءُ الْجَوْهَرِ يُوجِبُ ارْتِفَاعُهُ ارْتِفَاعَ الْجَوْهَرِ، وَكُلُّ مَا لَيْسَ بِجَوْهَرٍ لَا يُوجِبُ ارْتِفَاعُهُ ارْتِفَاعَ الْجَوْهَرِ، يَلْزَمُ مِنْهُ بِوَاسِطَةِ عَكْسِ نَقِيضِ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ الثَّانِيَةِ أَنَّ جُزْءَ الْجَوْهَرِ جَوْهَرٌ، وَلَا أَدْرِي وَجْهًا قَوِيًّا لِإِخْرَاجِ هَذَا الْقِسْمِ؛ فَإِنَّهُ كَالْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي، سِوَى أَنَّ مُنَاقَضَةَ الْحُدُودِ أَبْعَدَهُ عَنِ الطَّبَعِ جِدًّا، وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ. ثُمَّ إِنْ أُخِذَ اللُّزُومُ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ فَبِهَا، وَإِنِ اعْتُبِرَ بِحَسَبِ الْعِلْمِ، وَهُوَ الْأَشْهَرُ ، فَالْمُرَادُ الِاسْتِعْقَابُ بَعْدَ تَفَطُّنِ الِانْدِرَاجِ، كَمَا قَالَ ابْنُ سِينَا وَذَلِكَ الِاسْتِعْقَابُ عَلَى سَبِيلِ الْعَادَةِ، أَوِ التَّوْلِيدِ ، أَوِ الْإِعْدَادِ، عَلَى اخْتِلَافِ الْمَذَاهِبِ. وَهُوَ اسْتِثْنَائِيٌّ ، إِنْ كَانَ النَّتِيجَةُ أَوْ نَقِيضُهَا مَذْكُورًا فِيهِ بِهَيْئَتِهِ، وَإِلَّا فَاقْتِرَانِيٌّ، فَإِنْ تَرَكَّبَ مِنَ الْحَمْلِيَّاتِ الصِّرْفَةِ فَحَمْلِيٌّ ، وَإِلَّا فَشَرْطِيٌّ. وَمَوْضُوعُ الْمَطْلُوبِ يُسَمَّى أَصْغَرَ ، وَمَا هُوَ فِيهِ الصُّغْرَى، وَمَحْمُولُهُ أَكْبَر، وَمَا هُوَ فِيهِ الْكُبْرَى ، وَالْمُتَكَرِّرُ الْأَوْسَطُ ، وَالْقَضِيَّةُ الَّتِي جُعِلَتْ جُزْءَ قِيَاسٍ مُقَدِّمَةٌ ، وَطَرَفَاهَا حَدًّا ، وَاقْتِرَانُ الصُّغْرَى بِالْكُبْرَى قَرِينَةٌ وَضَرْبٌ وَهَيْئَةٌ . وَنِسْبَةُ الْأَوْسَطِ إِلَى طَرَفَيْ الْمَطْلُوبِ شَكْلًا، فَالْأَوْسَطُ إِمَّا مَحْمُولٌ فِي
As for a contradictory premise in the terms, like when you say: 'The part of the substance, its removal necessitates the removal of the substance; and everything that is not a substance, its removal does not necessitate the removal of the substance', it follows from it by the mediation of the contrapositive of the second premise that the part of the substance is a substance. And I do not know a strong reason for excluding this division, for it is like direct conversion, except that the contradiction of the terms distances it from nature greatly, and therein lies what lies. Then if implication is taken in reality itself, then it is [valid]; and if it is considered according to knowledge—and that is the more famous—then the intended meaning is subsequence after awareness of inclusion, as Ibn Sina said. And that subsequence is by way of habit, or generation, or preparation, depending on the different schools. And it is istithna'i (exceptive) if the conclusion or its contrary is explicitly mentioned in it in its form; otherwise it is iqtirani (conjunctive). If it is composed of pure categoricals, then it is hamli (categorical); otherwise it is sharti (conditional). The subject of the sought [conclusion] is called asghar (minor), and what contains it is the sughra (minor premise); its predicate is akbar (major), and what contains it is the kubra (major premise); the repeated [term] is awsat (middle); the proposition that is made a part of a syllogism is muqaddima (premise), and its two extremes are hadd (terms). The conjunction of the minor with the major is qarina (adjunct), darb (mood), and hay'a (figure). The relation of the middle to the two extremes of the sought is shakl (figure). So the middle is either a predicate in...
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of types of syllogisms. First, the author presents a case where two premises have contradictory terms: e.g., 'the part of the substance, its removal necessitates the removal of the substance' and 'everything that is not a substance, its removal does not necessitate the removal of the substance.' Using the contrapositive of the second premise, we conclude that the part of the substance is a substance. The author expresses doubt about excluding this from essential syllogism, as it seems similar to direct conversion. Then the author discusses the nature of implication (luzum): if taken as real (fi nafs al-amr), it holds; if taken according to knowledge (bi hasab al-'ilm, the more famous view), it means subsequence after awareness of the inclusion (tadakhul), as Ibn Sina said. Different schools explain this subsequence as habit (ada), generation (tawlid), or preparation (i'dad). The passage then classifies syllogisms: istithna'i (exceptive) if the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly stated, otherwise iqtirani (conjunctive). If composed of categorical propositions, it is hamli (categorical); otherwise sharti (conditional). Finally, the terminology of syllogism is defined: the subject of the conclusion is asghar (minor term), the premise containing it is sughra (minor premise); the predicate is akbar (major term), its premise is kubra (major premise); the repeated term is awsat (middle); the premises are muqaddimat; the conjunction of premises is qarina, darb (mood), hay'a (figure); and the relation of the middle to the extremes gives the shakl (figure). The text is cut off here, stating 'the middle is either a predicate in...'

لازمة متنافضة
عكس نقيض
لزوم

Key Takeaway

The Syllogism

Classification of Syllogisms and Logical Terminology
Classification of syllogisms and definition of logical terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any syllogism as istithna'i or iqtirani, hamli or sharti, and correctly identify its terms (asghar, akbar, awsat) and premises (sughra, kubra).

Core Idea
A syllogism is istithna'i if the conclusion is explicit in the premises; otherwise iqtirani. Categorical premises make it hamli, conditional make it sharti.

After the special case of contradictory terms, the author provides the standard classification of syllogisms and defines the key terms. This classification is the foundation for analyzing any logical argument in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary classification criterion
The author distinguishes two kinds of syllogism based on whether the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly mentioned: if yes, it is istithna'i (exceptive); if no, it is iqtirani (conjunctive).
This is about the presence of the conclusion in the premises as part of their form.
2
Identify the secondary classification
Then the syllogism is classified based on the type of propositions: if composed entirely of categorical (hamli) propositions, it is hamli; if any conditional (sharti) propositions, it is sharti.
A syllogism can be hamli iqtirani, sharti istithna'i, etc.
3
Define the terms of the conclusion
In any syllogism, the subject of the conclusion is called asghar (minor term), and its predicate is akbar (major term). The middle term (awsat) is repeated in both premises but not in the conclusion.
The premises are called muqaddimāt.
4
Relate terms to premises
The premise that contains the minor term is the sughra (minor premise); the premise containing the major term is the kubra (major premise). The conjunction (qarina) of the two premises, along with their arrangement, determines the mood (darb) and figure (shakl).
The figure depends on the position of the middle term in the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا لَازِمَةٌ مُتَنَافِضَةٌ فِي الْحُدُودِ، كَمَا تَقُولُ جُزْءُ الْجَوْهَرِ يُوجِبُ ارْتِفَاعُهُ ارْتِفَاعَ الْجَوْهَرِ، وَكُلُّ مَا لَيْسَ بِجَوْهَرٍ لَا يُوجِبُ ارْتِفَاعُهُ ارْتِفَاعَ الْجَوْهَرِ، يَلْزَمُ مِنْهُ بِوَاسِطَةِ عَكْسِ نَقِيضِ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ الثَّانِيَةِ أَنَّ جُزْءَ الْجَوْهَرِ جَوْهَرٌ، وَلَا أَدْرِي وَجْهًا قَوِيًّا لِإِخْرَاجِ هَذَا الْقِسْمِ؛ فَإِنَّهُ كَالْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي، سِوَى أَنَّ مُنَاقَضَةَ الْحُدُودِ أَبْعَدَهُ عَنِ الطَّبَعِ جِدًّا، وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ. ثُمَّ إِنْ أُخِذَ اللُّزُومُ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ فَبِهَا، وَإِنِ اعْتُبِرَ بِحَسَبِ الْعِلْمِ، وَهُوَ الْأَشْهَرُ ، فَالْمُرَادُ الِاسْتِعْقَابُ بَعْدَ تَفَطُّنِ الِانْدِرَاجِ، كَمَا قَالَ ابْنُ سِينَا وَذَلِكَ الِاسْتِعْقَابُ عَلَى سَبِيلِ الْعَادَةِ، أَوِ التَّوْلِيدِ ، أَوِ الْإِعْدَادِ، عَلَى اخْتِلَافِ الْمَذَاهِبِ. وَهُوَ اسْتِثْنَائِيٌّ ، إِنْ كَانَ النَّتِيجَةُ أَوْ نَقِيضُهَا مَذْكُورًا فِيهِ بِهَيْئَتِهِ، وَإِلَّا فَاقْتِرَانِيٌّ، فَإِنْ تَرَكَّبَ مِنَ الْحَمْلِيَّاتِ الصِّرْفَةِ فَحَمْلِيٌّ ، وَإِلَّا فَشَرْطِيٌّ. وَمَوْضُوعُ الْمَطْلُوبِ يُسَمَّى أَصْغَرَ ، وَمَا هُوَ فِيهِ الصُّغْرَى، وَمَحْمُولُهُ أَكْبَر، وَمَا هُوَ فِيهِ الْكُبْرَى ، وَالْمُتَكَرِّرُ الْأَوْسَطُ ، وَالْقَضِيَّةُ الَّتِي جُعِلَتْ جُزْءَ قِيَاسٍ مُقَدِّمَةٌ ، وَطَرَفَاهَا حَدًّا ، وَاقْتِرَانُ الصُّغْرَى بِالْكُبْرَى قَرِينَةٌ وَضَرْبٌ وَهَيْئَةٌ . وَنِسْبَةُ الْأَوْسَطِ إِلَى طَرَفَيْ الْمَطْلُوبِ شَكْلًا، فَالْأَوْسَطُ إِمَّا مَحْمُولٌ فِي
As for a contradictory premise in the terms, like when you say: 'The part of the substance, its removal necessitates the removal of the substance; and everything that is not a substance, its removal does not necessitate the removal of the substance', it follows from it by the mediation of the contrapositive of the second premise that the part of the substance is a substance. And I do not know a strong reason for excluding this division, for it is like direct conversion, except that the contradiction of the terms distances it from nature greatly, and therein lies what lies. Then if implication is taken in reality itself, then it is [valid]; and if it is considered according to knowledge—and that is the more famous—then the intended meaning is subsequence after awareness of inclusion, as Ibn Sina said. And that subsequence is by way of habit, or generation, or preparation, depending on the different schools. And it is istithna'i (exceptive) if the conclusion or its contrary is explicitly mentioned in it in its form; otherwise it is iqtirani (conjunctive). If it is composed of pure categoricals, then it is hamli (categorical); otherwise it is sharti (conditional). The subject of the sought [conclusion] is called asghar (minor), and what contains it is the sughra (minor premise); its predicate is akbar (major), and what contains it is the kubra (major premise); the repeated [term] is awsat (middle); the proposition that is made a part of a syllogism is muqaddima (premise), and its two extremes are hadd (terms). The conjunction of the minor with the major is qarina (adjunct), darb (mood), and hay'a (figure). The relation of the middle to the two extremes of the sought is shakl (figure). So the middle is either a predicate in...
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of types of syllogisms. First, the author presents a case where two premises have contradictory terms: e.g., 'the part of the substance, its removal necessitates the removal of the substance' and 'everything that is not a substance, its removal does not necessitate the removal of the substance.' Using the contrapositive of the second premise, we conclude that the part of the substance is a substance. The author expresses doubt about excluding this from essential syllogism, as it seems similar to direct conversion. Then the author discusses the nature of implication (luzum): if taken as real (fi nafs al-amr), it holds; if taken according to knowledge (bi hasab al-'ilm, the more famous view), it means subsequence after awareness of the inclusion (tadakhul), as Ibn Sina said. Different schools explain this subsequence as habit (ada), generation (tawlid), or preparation (i'dad). The passage then classifies syllogisms: istithna'i (exceptive) if the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly stated, otherwise iqtirani (conjunctive). If composed of categorical propositions, it is hamli (categorical); otherwise sharti (conditional). Finally, the terminology of syllogism is defined: the subject of the conclusion is asghar (minor term), the premise containing it is sughra (minor premise); the predicate is akbar (major term), its premise is kubra (major premise); the repeated term is awsat (middle); the premises are muqaddimat; the conjunction of premises is qarina, darb (mood), hay'a (figure); and the relation of the middle to the extremes gives the shakl (figure). The text is cut off here, stating 'the middle is either a predicate in...'
