The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Classification of Syllogism and Its Key Terms
Author classifies syllogism and defines key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any syllogism as istithna'i or iqtirani, and identify the minor, major, and middle terms in a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
The structure of a syllogism is defined by its terms and figures.

In this passage, the author lays the foundation by distinguishing the two main kinds of syllogism and introducing the technical vocabulary used throughout logic. Mastering these terms is essential for analyzing any argument in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish istithna'i from iqtirani
Read the first sentence. A syllogism is istithna'i if the conclusion or its contradictory is stated as a premise; otherwise it is iqtirani. Check which type a given syllogism belongs to.
Istithna'i corresponds to conditional syllogisms (modus ponens/tollens), iqtirani to categorical syllogisms.
2
Identify the minor and major terms
The subject of the sought conclusion is the minor term (asghar), the predicate the major term (akbar). Find them in a sample syllogism.
The premise containing the minor is the minor premise (sughra), and that containing the major is the major premise (kubra).
3
Locate the middle term
The middle term (awsat) is the term repeated in both premises but not appearing in the conclusion. Identify it in the same example.
If there is no repeated term, it is not a categorical syllogism.
4
Determine the figure
Observe the position of the middle term in the two premises. If it is predicate in the minor and subject in the major, it is the first figure; predicate in both: second; subject in both: third; opposite of first: fourth.
The first figure is considered natural and most evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهُوَ اسْتِثْنَائِيُّ ، إِنْ كَانَ النَّتِيجَةُ أَوْ نَقِيضُهَا مَذْكُورًا فِيهِ بِهَيْئَتِهِ، وَإِلَّا فَاقْتِرَانِيُّ، فَإِنْ تَرَكَّبَ مِنَ الْحَمْلِيَّاتِ الصِّرْفَةِ فَحَمْلِيُّ ، وَإِلَّا فَشَرْطِيٌّ. وَمَوْضُوعُ الْمَطْلُوب يُسَمَّى أَصْغَرَ ، وَمَا هُوَ فِيهِ الصُّغْرَى، وَمَحْمُولُهُ أَكْبَر، وَمَا هُوَ فِيهِ الْكُبْرَى ، وَالْمُتَكَرِّرُ الْأَوْسَطُ ، وَالْقَضِيَّةُ الَّتِي جُعِلَتْ جُزْء قِيَاسِ مُقَدِّمَةٌ ، وَطَرَفَاهَا حَدًا ، وَاقْتِرَانُ الصُّغْرَى بِالْكُبْرَى فَرِينَةٌ وَضَرْبًا وَهَيْئَةٌ . وَنِسْبَةُ الْأَوْسَطِ إِلَى طَرَفَيْ الْمَطْلُوبِ شَكلًا، فَالْأَوْسَطُ إِمَّا مَحْمُولٌ فِي الصُّغْرَى وَمَوْضُوعٌ فِي الْكُبْرَى، فَهُوَ الْأَوَّلُ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ عَلَى نَظْمِ طَبِيعِيٌّ، أَوْ مَحْمُولُهُمَا فَالثَّانِي، وَهُوَ أَقْرَبُ مِنَ الْأَوَّلِ، حَتَّى ادَّعَى بَعْضُهُمْ أَنَّهُ بَيِّنُ، أَوْ مَوْضُوعُهُمَا فَالثَّالِثُ، أَوْ عَكْسُ الْأَوَّلِ فَالرَّابِعُ ، وَهُوَ أَبْعَدُ جِدًّا، حَتَّى أَسْقَطَهُ الشَّيْخَانِ عَن الاعْتِبَارِ. وكُلُّ شَكْلٍ يَرْتَدُّ إِلَى الْآخَرِ بِعَكْسِ مَا تَخَالَفَا فِيهِ. وَلَا قِيَاسَ مُطْلَقًا مِنْ جُزْيَّتَيْن ، وَلَا سَالِبتَيْنِ، وَالتَّتِيجَةُ تَتْبَعُ أَخَسَّ الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْن كَما وَكَيْفًا، بِالاسْتِقْرَاء. وَيُشْتَرَطُ فِي الْأَوَّلِ إِيجَابُ الصُّغْرَى وَكُلِّيَّةُ الْكُبْرَى؛ لِيَلْزَمَ الاِنْدِرَاجُ ، وَاحْتِمَالُ الضُّرُوبِ فِي كُلِّ شَكْلِ سِتَّةَ عَشَرَ، وَأَسْقَطَ هَهُنَا شَرْطُ الْإِيجَابِ ثَمَانِيَةٌ ، وَشَرْطُ الكُلْيَةِ أَرْبَعَةٌ ، بَقِيَ أَرْبَعَةُ الْمُوجَبَتَيْنِ مَعَ الْكُلِّيتَيْنِ، مُنْتِجَةً لِمَطَالِب أَرْبَعَةِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ ، وَذَلِكَ مِنْ خَوَاصٌهِ ، كَالْإِيجَابِ الْكُلّي . وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ مَشْهُورٌ مِنْ وَجْهَيْنِ: الْأَوَّلُ أَنَّ النَّتِيجَةَ مَوْقُوفَةٌ عَلَى كُلِّيّةِ الْكُبْرَى ، وَبِالْعَكْسِ ؛ لِأَنَّ الْأَصْغَرَ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ الْأَوْسَطِ، فَدَارَ . وَحَلَّهُ: أَنَّ التَّفْصِيلَ مَوْقُوفٌ عَلَى الْإِجْمَالِ، وَالْحُكْمُ يَخْتَلِفُ بِاخْتِلَافِ الْأَوْصَافِ ، فَلَا إِشْكَالَ . الثَّانِي: أَنَّ قَوْلَنَا الْخَلَةُ لَيْسَ بِمَوْجُودٍ، وَكُلُّ مَا لَيْسَ بِمَوْجُودٍ لَيْسَ بِمَحْسُوس يُنتجُ ، مَعَ أَنَّ الصُّغُرَى سَالِبَةٌ، بَلْ كُلَّمَا تَكَرَّرَتِ النِّسْبَةُ السَّلْبيَّةُ أَنْتَجَتْ . وَحَلُهُ كَمَا قِيلَ : إِنَّهَا مُوجَبَةٌ سَالِبَةُ الْمَحْمُولٍ، يَدُلُّ عَلَى ذَلِكَ جَعْلُ النَّسْبَةِ السَّلْبِيَّةِ مِرْآةَ لِلْأَفْرَادِ فِي الْكُبْرَى أَقُولُ: وَلَكَ أَنْ تَسْتَدِلَّ مِنْ هَهُنَا عَلَى عَدَمِ اسْتِدْعَاء تِلْكَ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْوُجُودَ ، فَتَدَبَّرْ .
It is conditional (istithna'i) if the conclusion or its contradictory is mentioned in it in its form, otherwise it is conjunctive (iqtirani). If it is composed of pure categoricals then it is categorical (hamli), otherwise it is conditional (sharti). The subject of the sought conclusion is called the minor term (asghar), and the premise containing it is the minor premise (sughra). Its predicate is the major term (akbar), and the premise containing it is the major premise (kubra). The repeated term is the middle term (awsat). The proposition made a part of a syllogism is a premise (muqaddima), and its two extremes are terms (haddan). The conjunction of the minor with the major is a connection (qarina), a mood (darb), and a form (hay'a). The relation of the middle term to the two extremes of the sought conclusion is a figure (shakl). The middle term is either a predicate in the minor and a subject in the major – that is the first figure, because it is natural order – or it is a predicate in both – the second, which is closer than the first, so much so that some claimed it is self-evident – or it is a subject in both – the third – or it is the opposite of the first – the fourth, which is very remote, such that the two shaykhs dropped it from consideration. Every figure reduces to the other by the converse of that in which they differ. Absolutely no syllogism from two negative premises. The conclusion follows the inferior of the two premises in quantity and quality, by induction. In the first figure, it is required that the minor be affirmative and the major universal, so that subsumption is necessary. The possible moods in every figure are sixteen; here the condition of affirmation drops eight, and the condition of universality drops four; there remain four affirmative with universal in each, producing four sought conclusions necessarily, and that is among its properties, like universal affirmation. Here there is a famous doubt from two aspects: First, the conclusion depends on the universality of the major, and vice versa, because the minor is from the totality of the middle term, so there is circularity. Its solution: the detailed depends on the summary, and the judgment differs with the difference of attributes, so there is no problem. Second, our statement 'the deficiency is not existent, and everything not existent is not perceptible' produces a conclusion, even though the minor is negative; rather, whenever the negative relation is repeated it produces. Its solution, as was said: it is an affirmative with a negative predicate; that is indicated by making the negative relation a mirror for individuals in the major. I say: and you may infer from here that that affirmative does not require existence, so reflect.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory expansion (sharh) on the types of syllogism (qiyas) and their technical terminology, followed by the classification of figures, the conditions for validity, and the resolution of two famous doubts. The author begins by distinguishing between two main types of syllogism: istithna'i (conditional, where the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly stated in the premise) and iqtirani (conjunctive, where it is not). Within iqtirani, he further subdivides into hamli (categorical, composed of simple categorical propositions) and sharti (conditional, when at least one premise is conditional). He then defines the key parts of a categorical syllogism: the subject of the conclusion is the minor term (asghar), the premise containing it is the minor premise (sughra); the predicate of the conclusion is the major term (akbar), its premise the major premise (kubra); the term repeated in the premises is the middle term (awsat). Each premise is a muqaddima, its extremes are hadd (terms), and the pairing of minor and major premises is called qarina (connection), darb (mood), and hay'a (form). The relation of the middle term to the extremes determines the figure (shakl). The author enumerates four figures: first (middle is predicate in minor, subject in major), second (middle predicate in both), third (middle subject in both), fourth (opposite of first). He notes that the first figure is natural and the fourth was rejected by the two shaykhs (al-Abhari and al-Razi?). He states that no syllogism can have two negative premises, and the conclusion follows the weaker premise in quantity and quality, based on induction. For the first figure, the conditions are that the minor premise must be affirmative and the major premise universal, ensuring subsumption. He explains that out of sixteen possible mood combinations per figure, the conditions of affirmation and universality reduce the first figure to four productive moods, all yielding universal affirmative conclusions if valid. Finally, he addresses two doubts: first, an apparent circularity between the universality of the major and the conclusion; he solves it by noting that the detailed judgment depends on the summary, and differences in attributes affect judgments. Second, an example where a syllogism with a negative minor seems to produce a conclusion (e.g., 'the deficiency is not existent; everything non-existent is imperceptible' yields 'the deficiency is imperceptible'). His solution: such a minor premise is actually affirmative in form but has a negative predicate, as the negative relation acts as a mirror for individuals. He adds a personal comment that this shows the affirmative does not require existence.

Key Terms
اسْتِثْنَائِيُّ
اقْتِرَانِيُّ
أَصْغَر
أَكْبَر
أَوْسَط
شَكْل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what do we call a syllogism in which the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly mentioned as a premise?
A) Conjunctive (iqtirani)
B) Conditional (istithna'i)
C) Categorical (hamli)
D) Conditional (sharti)

Key Takeaway
The first figure, where the middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major, is the most natural and evident arrangement for a categorical syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the syllogism: 'All intoxicants are forbidden; wine is an intoxicant; therefore wine is forbidden.' Identify the minor, major, and middle terms, and state the figure.
?
Classify the following: 'If it is tahir, then it is permissible to use; it is not tahir; therefore it is not permissible to use.' Is this istithna'i or iqtirani? Why?

The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
Conditions for the First Figure and Resolving Two Doubts
Author states conditions for first figure and resolves two doubts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions of the first figure to determine the validity of a categorical syllogism and resolve apparent counterexamples.

Core Idea
The first figure requires an affirmative minor and universal major, but seemingly negative minors can be reinterpreted as affirmative with negative predicates.

After establishing terminology, the author discusses the general rules of syllogisms and the specific conditions for the first figure. He then addresses two famous doubts that challenge the necessity of these conditions, providing solutions that deepen our understanding of propositional form.

Guided Path
1
State the two general rules
Read the first two sentences after 'Absolutely no syllogism from two negative premises.' and 'The conclusion follows the inferior of the premises in quantity and quality.' These rules apply to all categorical syllogisms.
These rules are derived by induction from many examples.
2
Identify the conditions for the first figure
Find the sentence: 'In the first figure, it is required that the minor be affirmative and the major universal.' Understand why: subsumption of the minor under the major requires these conditions.
The author explains that these conditions reduce the 16 possible moods to 4 productive ones.
3
Analyze the first doubt
The doubt is circularity: the conclusion depends on the universality of the major, and vice versa. Read the solution: the detailed depends on the summary, and judgments differ by attributes.
The author resolves it by distinguishing between general and specific judgments.
4
Analyze the second doubt
The example: 'The deficiency is not existent; everything not existent is imperceptible; therefore the deficiency is imperceptible.' The minor appears negative but still yields a conclusion. Read the solution: the minor is actually an affirmative proposition with a negative predicate.
This shows that 'not existent' functions as a term in an affirmative proposition.

وَهُوَ اسْتِثْنَائِيُّ ، إِنْ كَانَ النَّتِيجَةُ أَوْ نَقِيضُهَا مَذْكُورًا فِيهِ بِهَيْئَتِهِ، وَإِلَّا فَاقْتِرَانِيُّ، فَإِنْ تَرَكَّبَ مِنَ الْحَمْلِيَّاتِ الصِّرْفَةِ فَحَمْلِيُّ ، وَإِلَّا فَشَرْطِيٌّ. وَمَوْضُوعُ الْمَطْلُوب يُسَمَّى أَصْغَرَ ، وَمَا هُوَ فِيهِ الصُّغْرَى، وَمَحْمُولُهُ أَكْبَر، وَمَا هُوَ فِيهِ الْكُبْرَى ، وَالْمُتَكَرِّرُ الْأَوْسَطُ ، وَالْقَضِيَّةُ الَّتِي إِلَى الْآخَرِ بِعَكْسِ مَا تَخَالَفَا فِيهِ. وَلَا قِيَاسَ مُطْلَقًا مِنْ جُزْيَّتَيْن ، وَلَا سَالِبتَيْنِ، وَالتَّتِيجَةُ تَتْبَعُ أَخَسَّ الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْن كَما وَكَيْفًا، بِالاسْتِقْرَاء. وَيُشْتَرَطُ فِي الْأَوَّلِ إِيجَابُ الصُّغْرَى وَكُلِّيَّةُ الْكُبْرَى؛ لِيَلْزَمَ الاِنْدِرَاجُ ، وَاحْتِمَالُ الضُّرُوبِ فِي كُلِّ شَكْلِ سِتَّةَ عَشَرَ، وَأَسْقَطَ هَهُنَا شَرْطُ الْإِيجَابِ ثَمَانِيَةٌ ، وَشَرْطُ الكُلْيَةِ أَرْبَعَةٌ ، بَقِيَ أَرْبَعَةُ الْمُوجَبَتَيْنِ مَعَ الْكُلِّيتَيْنِ، مُنْتِجَةً لِمَطَالِب أَرْبَعَةِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ ، وَذَلِكَ مِنْ خَوَاصٌهِ ، كَالْإِيجَابِ الْكُلّي . وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ مَشْهُورٌ مِنْ وَجْهَيْنِ: الْأَوَّلُ أَنَّ النَّتِيجَةَ مَوْقُوفَةٌ عَلَى كُلِّيّةِ الْكُبْرَى ، وَبِالْعَكْسِ ؛ لِأَنَّ الْأَصْغَرَ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ الْأَوْسَطِ، فَدَارَ . وَحَلَّهُ: أَنَّ التَّفْصِيلَ مَوْقُوفٌ عَلَى الْإِجْمَالِ، وَالْحُكْمُ يَخْتَلِفُ بِاخْتِلَافِ الْأَوْصَافِ ، فَلَا إِشْكَالَ . الثَّانِي: أَنَّ قَوْلَنَا الْخَلَةُ لَيْسَ بِمَوْجُودٍ، وَكُلُّ مَا لَيْسَ بِمَوْجُودٍ لَيْسَ بِمَحْسُوس يُنتجُ ، مَعَ أَنَّ الصُّغُرَى سَالِبَةٌ، بَلْ كُلَّمَا تَكَرَّرَتِ النِّسْبَةُ السَّلْبيَّةُ أَنْتَجَتْ . وَحَلُهُ كَمَا قِيلَ : إِنَّهَا مُوجَبَةٌ سَالِبَةُ الْمَحْمُولٍ، يَدُلُّ عَلَى ذَلِكَ جَعْلُ النَّسْبَةِ السَّلْبِيَّةِ مِرْآةَ لِلْأَفْرَادِ فِي الْكُبْرَى أَقُولُ: وَلَكَ أَنْ تَسْتَدِلَّ مِنْ هَهُنَا عَلَى عَدَمِ اسْتِدْعَاء تِلْكَ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْوُجُودَ ، فَتَدَبَّرْ .
It is conditional (istithna'i) if the conclusion or its contradictory is mentioned in it in its form, otherwise it is conjunctive (iqtirani). If it is composed of pure categoricals then it is categorical (hamli), otherwise it is conditional (sharti). The subject of the sought conclusion is called the minor term (asghar), and the premise containing it is the minor premise (sughra). Its predicate is the major term (akbar), and the premise containing it is the major premise (kubra). The repeated term is the middle term (awsat). The proposition made a part of a syllogism is a premise (muqaddima), and its two extremes are terms (haddan). The conjunction of the minor with the major is a connection (qarina), a mood (darb), and a form (hay'a). The relation of the middle term to the two extremes of the sought conclusion is a figure (shakl). The middle term is either a predicate in the minor and a subject in the major – that is the first figure, because it is natural order – or it is a predicate in both – the second, which is closer than the first, so much so that some claimed it is self-evident – or it is a subject in both – the third – or it is the opposite of the first – the fourth, which is very remote, such that the two shaykhs dropped it from consideration. Every figure reduces to the other by the converse of that in which they differ. Absolutely no syllogism from two negative premises. The conclusion follows the inferior of the two premises in quantity and quality, by induction. In the first figure, it is required that the minor be affirmative and the major universal, so that subsumption is necessary. The possible moods in every figure are sixteen; here the condition of affirmation drops eight, and the condition of universality drops four; there remain four affirmative with universal in each, producing four sought conclusions necessarily, and that is among its properties, like universal affirmation. Here there is a famous doubt from two aspects: First, the conclusion depends on the universality of the major, and vice versa, because the minor is from the totality of the middle term, so there is circularity. Its solution: the detailed depends on the summary, and the judgment differs with the difference of attributes, so there is no problem. Second, our statement 'the deficiency is not existent, and everything not existent is not perceptible' produces a conclusion, even though the minor is negative; rather, whenever the negative relation is repeated it produces. Its solution, as was said: it is an affirmative with a negative predicate; that is indicated by making the negative relation a mirror for individuals in the major. I say: and you may infer from here that that affirmative does not require existence, so reflect.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory expansion (sharh) on the types of syllogism (qiyas) and their technical terminology, followed by the classification of figures, the conditions for validity, and the resolution of two famous doubts. The author begins by distinguishing between two main types of syllogism: istithna'i (conditional, where the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly stated in the premise) and iqtirani (conjunctive, where it is not). Within iqtirani, he further subdivides into hamli (categorical, composed of simple categorical propositions) and sharti (conditional, when at least one premise is conditional). He then defines the key parts of a categorical syllogism: the subject of the conclusion is the minor term (asghar), the premise containing it is the minor premise (sughra); the predicate of the conclusion is the major term (akbar), its premise the major premise (kubra); the term repeated in the premises is the middle term (awsat). Each premise is a muqaddima, its extremes are hadd (terms), and the pairing of minor and major premises is called qarina (connection), darb (mood), and hay'a (form). The relation of the middle term to the extremes determines the figure (shakl). The author enumerates four figures: first (middle is predicate in minor, subject in major), second (middle predicate in both), third (middle subject in both), fourth (opposite of first). He notes that the first figure is natural and the fourth was rejected by the two shaykhs (al-Abhari and al-Razi?). He states that no syllogism can have two negative premises, and the conclusion follows the weaker premise in quantity and quality, based on induction. For the first figure, the conditions are that the minor premise must be affirmative and the major premise universal, ensuring subsumption. He explains that out of sixteen possible mood combinations per figure, the conditions of affirmation and universality reduce the first figure to four productive moods, all yielding universal affirmative conclusions if valid. Finally, he addresses two doubts: first, an apparent circularity between the universality of the major and the conclusion; he solves it by noting that the detailed judgment depends on the summary, and differences in attributes affect judgments. Second, an example where a syllogism with a negative minor seems to produce a conclusion (e.g., 'the deficiency is not existent; everything non-existent is imperceptible' yields 'the deficiency is imperceptible'). His solution: such a minor premise is actually affirmative in form but has a negative predicate, as the negative relation acts as a mirror for individuals. He adds a personal comment that this shows the affirmative does not require existence.

The Four Figures of Syllogism Based on Middle Term Position
Author defines the four figures based on middle term position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four figures a given categorical syllogism belongs to by locating the middle term's position in the premises.

Core Idea
The figure of a syllogism is determined solely by the middle term's position.

In a categorical syllogism, the middle term connects the two premises. Its position can vary, giving rise to four distinct figures. This passage defines them clearly. Understanding these figures is essential because, for example, the first figure yields certain conclusions without needing conversion, while the fourth is so ambiguous that leading authorities excluded it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
The middle term is the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. First, locate it in the syllogism.
In many texts, the conclusion is given first; find the term common to both premises.
2
Determine its position in the minor premise
The minor premise contains the subject of the conclusion. Is the middle term the subject or predicate in this premise?
Minor premise is usually the premise containing the subject term of the conclusion.
3
Determine its position in the major premise
The major premise contains the predicate of the conclusion. Where does the middle term sit here?
Major premise is usually the premise containing the predicate term of the conclusion.
4
Match the pattern to the four figures
Use the definitions: first figure: middle term is subject in minor, predicate in major; second: predicate in both; third: subject in both; fourth: predicate in minor, subject in major.
The fourth figure is the reverse of the first and is often considered remote or even excluded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالصُّغْرَى وَمَوْضُوعٌ فِي الْكُبْرَى، فَهُوَ الْأَوَّلُ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ عَلَى نَظْمِ طَبِيعِيٌّ، أَوْ مَحْمُولُهُمَا فَالثَّانِي، وَهُوَ أَقْرَبُ مِنَ الْأَوَّلِ، حَتَّى ادَّعَى بَعْضُهُمْ أَنَّهُ بَيِّنُ، أَوْ مَوْضُوعُهُمَا فَالثَّالِثُ، أَوْ عَكْسُ الْأَوَّلِ فَالرَّابِعُ ، وَهُوَ أَبْعَدُ جِدًّا، حَتَّى أَسْقَطَهُ الشَّيْخَانِ عَن الاعْتِبَارِ.
The minor premise with the subject in the major premise, that is the first because it is in a natural arrangement. Or both are predicates, then the second, and it is closer than the first, so much so that some claimed it is self-evident. Or both are subjects, then the third. Or the reverse of the first, then the fourth, and it is very remote, so much so that the two Shaykhs dropped it from consideration.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage defines the four figures of the categorical syllogism. The author classifies figures based on the position of the middle term in the two premises. In the first figure, the middle term is the subject of the minor premise and predicate of the major, which is the natural order. In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. In the third figure, it is the subject in both. In the fourth figure, it is the predicate of the minor and subject of the major—the reverse of the first. The author notes that the second figure is so clear that some considered it self-evident, while the fourth is so obscure that al-Farabi and Ibn Sina (the two Shaykhs) excluded it from consideration entirely.

General Rules: Reduction, Particular and Negative Premises
Author states three general rules for categorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the three general rules of categorical syllogisms: reduction via conversion, prohibition of two particulars or two negatives, and the conclusion following the weaker premise.

Core Idea
Two negatives or two particulars never yield a conclusion; the conclusion always follows the weaker premise.

In this passage, the author condenses three fundamental rules that govern all valid categorical syllogisms. These rules are derived from induction over all valid moods. They are essential for quickly evaluating whether a given pair of premises can produce a valid conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule about reduction
The author states that every figure can be reduced to another by converting the premise that differs. Note how conversion (aks) is used to transform one figure into another, which is key for proving validity.
Reduction is a method to check validity by converting premises to a standard form.
2
Recognize the prohibition of two particulars
Two particular premises (e.g., Some A is B, Some B is C) yield no conclusion. This is a hard rule: no syllogism from two particulars.
3
Recognize the prohibition of two negatives
Two negative premises also yield no conclusion. For example, 'No A is B' and 'No B is C' produce no necessary relation between A and C.
4
Apply the 'conclusion follows the weaker premise' rule
The conclusion takes the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the weaker premise. In quantity, particular is weaker than universal; in quality, negative is weaker than affirmative.
This rule determines the form of the conclusion when premises are valid.
5
Test with an example
If one premise is universal affirmative and the other is particular affirmative, the conclusion must be particular affirmative because particular is weaker in quantity.
The same applies for quality: if one premise is affirmative and the other negative, the conclusion is negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكُلُّ شَكْلٍ يَرْتَدُّ إِلَى الْآخَرِ بِعَكْسِ مَا تَخَالَفَا فِيهِ. وَلَا قِيَاسَ مُطْلَقًا مِنْ جُزْئِيَّتَيْن ، وَلَا سَالِبَتَيْنِ، وَالتَّتِيجَةُ تَتْبَعُ أَخَسَّ الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْن كَما وَكَيْفًا، بِالاسْتِقْرَاءِ.
And every figure is reduced to the other by conversion of what they differ in. And there is no syllogism at all from two particulars, nor from two negatives, and the conclusion follows the inferior of the two premises in quantity and quality, by induction.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage provides three general rules for categorical syllogisms. First, each figure can be reduced to another by converting the premise that differs between them. For example, the second figure can be reduced to the first by converting the major premise. Second, no syllogism is formed from two particular premises or two negative premises. This is a fundamental restriction: both premises cannot be particular, and both cannot be negative. Third, the conclusion always follows the weaker premise in both quantity (particular is weaker than universal) and quality (negative is weaker than affirmative). This is derived empirically from examining all valid moods, an induction (istiqra').

Key Terms
عكس
أخس

Key Takeaway
The conclusion of a categorical syllogism always follows the weaker premise in both quantity and quality; two particulars or two negatives never yield a conclusion.

Conditions and Valid Moods of the First Figure
Author specifies conditions for the first figure and derives its four valid moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the two conditions for the first figure and identify its four valid moods (Barbara, Celarent, Darii, Ferio).

Core Idea
First figure needs affirmative minor and universal major for direct subsumption.

The first figure of the categorical syllogism is the most natural and powerful. To ensure valid inference, two conditions must hold: the minor premise must be affirmative and the major premise universal. This lesson shows how these conditions reduce the 16 possible moods to exactly 4 that always yield necessary conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The author states: the minor premise must be affirmative (إِيجَابُ الصُّغْرَى) and the major premise must be universal (كُلِّيَّةُ الْكُبْرَى). These conditions guarantee that the minor term is fully included under the middle term.
Subsumption (اندراج) requires the minor to be affirmative so that it falls under the middle, and the major to be universal to cover all of the middle.
2
Count the 16 possible moods
In any figure, each premise has 4 types (A, E, I, O), so there are 4×4 = 16 possible moods.
3
Apply the affirmative minor condition
The minor must be affirmative (A or I). This eliminates all 8 moods where the minor is negative (E or O).
8 moods removed: Ei, Eo, Oi, Oo, etc. (any with E or O as minor).
4
Apply the universal major condition
The major must be universal (A or E). From the remaining 8, eliminate those with particular major (I or O). This leaves 4 moods.
The remaining 4 have major A or E, and minor A or I.
5
List the four valid moods
The four valid moods are: Barbara (AAA), Celarent (EAE), Darii (AII), and Ferio (EIO). These are the only moods that satisfy both conditions and produce necessary conclusions.
Latin names are traditional memorization aids: Barbara (AAA), Celarent (EAE), Darii (AII), Ferio (EIO).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيُشْتَرَطُ فِي الْأَوَّلِ إِيجَابُ الصُّغْرَى وَكُلِّيَّةُ الْكُبْرَى؛ لِيَلْزَمَ الاِنْدِرَاجُ ، وَاحْتِمَالُ الضُّرُوبِ فِي كُلِّ شَكْلِ سِتَّةَ عَشَرَ، وَأَسْقَطَ هَهُنَا شَرْطُ الْإِيجَابِ ثَمَانِيَةٌ ، وَشَرْطُ الكُلِّيَّةِ أَرْبَعَةٌ ، بَقِيَ أَرْبَعَةُ الْمُوجَبَتَيْنِ مَعَ الْكُلِّيَّتَيْنِ، مُنْتِجَةً لِمَطَالِبَ أَرْبَعَةَ بِالضَّرُورَةِ ، وَذَلِكَ مِنْ خَوَاصِّهِ ، كَالْإِيجَابِ الْكُلِّيِّ .
And it is required in the first figure that the minor be affirmative and the major be universal, so that subsumption is necessary. And the possible moods in every figure are sixteen. Here, the condition of affirmativeness eliminates eight, and the condition of universality eliminates four. There remain four of the two affirmatives with the two universals, producing four necessary conclusions. And that is from its properties, like universal affirmative.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage specifies the two conditions for the first figure: the minor premise must be affirmative, and the major premise must be universal. The reason is that subsumption (indiraj) of the minor under the major requires these conditions. The author then explains that each figure has 16 possible moods (combinations of quantity and quality of two premises: 4x4 = 16). By applying the condition that the minor must be affirmative, 8 moods with a negative minor are eliminated. From the remaining 8, the condition that the major must be universal eliminates 4 with a particular major. This leaves 4 moods. These four are: both premises affirmative and both universal? The text says 'أَرْبَعَةُ الْمُوجَبَتَيْنِ مَعَ الْكُلِّيَّتَيْنِ', which literally means 'four of the two affirmatives with the two universals'. This is likely a concise way of saying that the remaining moods have an affirmative minor and a universal major. Among these four, two have affirmative major (Barbara, Darii) and two have negative major (Celarent, Ferio). The author notes that these produce four necessary conclusions, and this is a special property of the first figure, like the universal affirmative mood.

The first figure requires an affirmative minor and a universal major, which leaves exactly four valid moods (Barbara, Celarent, Darii, Ferio) that always yield necessary conclusions.

Resolving Two Famous Doubts About the First Figure
Author presents two objections to first figure conditions and provides responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve objections regarding the conditions of the first figure and identify an affirmative proposition with a negative predicate.

Core Idea
Logical conditions can be refined without circularity; a negative minor can be recast as an affirmative.

In this passage, the author addresses two classic objections to the rules of the first figure of the categorical syllogism. These doubts challenge the necessity of a universal major and the requirement of an affirmative minor. Understanding their solutions deepens your grasp of syllogistic validity and prepares you for real debates in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two doubts
Read the Arabic passage and locate the two distinct objections (shakk). The first concerns circularity between the universality of the major and the conclusion. The second concerns a syllogism with a negative minor that seems to produce a valid conclusion.
Note the phrase 'من وجهين' (from two aspects) introduces them.
2
Analyze the first doubt and its solution
The first doubt: The conclusion depends on the major being universal, but the major's universality depends on the conclusion? This seems circular. The solution: the detailed ruling (the conclusion) depends on the summary (the major premise), but judgments differ with different descriptions, so no circularity. Understand that the dependency is not bidirectional in the same respect.
The phrase 'التفصيل موقوف على الإجمال' captures the asymmetry.
3
Analyze the second doubt and its solution
The second doubt: The syllogism 'The void is non-existent; everything non-existent is not sensible' yields 'The void is not sensible', yet the minor is negative. This violates the rule that the minor must be affirmative. The solution: the minor is actually an affirmative proposition with a negative predicate (mūjaba sālibatu l-maḥmūl). Its form is affirmative (copula is positive), but the predicate is negated. This satisfies the condition.
The author adds that such an affirmative does not require existence of the subject, important for existential import.
4
Synthesize the refined rules
The first doubt refines our understanding of dependency between premises and conclusion. The second doubt shows that 'affirmative minor' must be interpreted by form, not content. Recognising affirmative-with-negative-predicate ensures the first figure's conditions are not overly restrictive.
This refines the standard rules: the minor must be affirmative in form, not necessarily in meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ مَشْهُورٌ مِنْ وَجْهَيْنِ: الْأَوَّلُ أَنَّ النَّتِيجَةَ مَوْقُوفَةٌ عَلَى كُلِّيَّةِ الْكُبْرَى ، وَبِالْعَكْسِ ؛ لِأَنَّ الْأَصْغَرَ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ الْأَوْسَطِ، فَدَارَ . وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّ التَّفْصِيلَ مَوْقُوفٌ عَلَى الْإِجْمَالِ، وَالْحُكْمُ يَخْتَلِفُ بِاخْتِلَافِ الْأَوْصَافِ ، فَلَا إِشْكَالَ . الثَّانِي: أَنَّ قَوْلَنَا الْخَلَاءُ لَيْسَ بِمَوْجُودٍ، وَكُلُّ مَا لَيْسَ بِمَوْجُودٍ لَيْسَ بِمَحْسُوسٍ يُنتِجُ ، مَعَ أَنَّ الصُّغْرَى سَالِبَةٌ، بَلْ كُلَّمَا تَكَرَّرَتِ النِّسْبَةُ السَّلْبِيَّةُ أَنْتَجَتْ . وَحَلُّهُ كَمَا قِيلَ : إِنَّهَا مُوجَبَةٌ سَالِبَةُ الْمَحْمُولِ، يَدُلُّ عَلَى ذَلِكَ جَعْلُ النَّسْبَةِ السَّلْبِيَّةِ مِرْآةً لِلْأَفْرَادِ فِي الْكُبْرَى . أَقُولُ: وَلَكَ أَنْ تَسْتَدِلَّ مِنْ هَهُنَا عَلَى عَدَمِ اسْتِدْعَاءِ تِلْكَ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْوُجُودَ ، فَتَدَبَّرْ .
And here is a famous doubt from two aspects: The first is that the conclusion depends on the universality of the major, and vice versa, because the minor is part of the middle, so there is circularity. Its solution: that detail depends on summary, and the judgment differs with differences of descriptions, so there is no problem. The second: that our saying 'The void is non-existent' and 'Everything non-existent is not sensible' produces a conclusion, even though the minor is negative. Rather, whenever a negative relation is repeated, it produces. Its solution as has been said: it is an affirmative with a negative predicate, and evidence for that is making the negative relation a mirror for individuals in the major. I say: And you may infer from here that that affirmative does not require existence, so ponder.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage addresses two famous objections to the first figure conditions. The first doubt questions the necessity of a universal major: if the conclusion depends on the major being universal, and vice versa, there seems to be circularity. The solution is that the detail (the conclusion) depends on the summary (the major), but judgments differ based on different descriptions, so no circularity. The second doubt presents a counterexample: the syllogism 'The void is non-existent' (negative minor) and 'Everything non-existent is not sensible' (universal negative) seems to produce a conclusion 'The void is not sensible', even though the minor is negative, violating the condition of an affirmative minor. The solution is that the minor is actually an affirmative proposition with a negative predicate, i.e., it is equivalent to 'The void is non-existent' which is affirmative in form but has a negative predicate. The author adds that this shows such affirmative does not imply existence of the subject. The commentator (Bahr Ulum) interjects to emphasize that the affirmative with negative predicate does not require actual existence of the subject.

موجبة سالبة المحمول

Key Takeaway
The first figure conditions are not absolute; they can be refined by analyzing propositional form: a negative minor can be valid if it is an affirmative with a negative predicate.

Conditions for the Second Figure
Author specifies conditions for second figure and its valid moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions required for a valid second-figure syllogism and determine the correct conclusion (universal negative or particular negative) based on the quantity of the premises.

Core Idea
Second figure: differing quality and universal major yield negative conclusions.

In this lesson, we examine the logical conditions that govern the second figure of categorical syllogisms. The author specifies that the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and that the major premise must be universal. Violating either condition renders the syllogism invalid. The passage also explains how the valid moods (Cesare, Camestres, Festino) are derived, and the proof methods of reductio and conversion are mentioned.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two essential conditions
The passage states 'difference of the two premises in quality, and universality of the major'. So condition one: premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). Condition two: major premise must be universal. Why? If major were particular, you could not guarantee a valid inference.
2
Apply conditions to determine valid premise pairs
With differing quality, only two valid combinations: both universal (e.g., universal affirmative + universal negative) produce universal negative conclusion; one universal and one particular produce particular negative conclusion. The author calls these 'the two universals' and 'the ones that differ in quantity'.
3
Derive the conclusion
When both premises are universal, conclusion is universal negative (e.g., Cesare: No M is P, All S is M → No S is P; or Camestres: All P is M, No S is M → No S is P). When one universal and one particular, conclusion is particular negative (e.g., Festino: No M is P, Some S is M → Some S is not P).
4
Understand the proof methods
The author notes these moods are proven by khalf (reductio ad absurdum: assume contradictory of conclusion and derive contradiction) or by conversion of major or minor, then rearrangement to reduce to first figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَفِي الثَّانِي اخْتِلَافُ الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ فِي الْكَيْفِ، وَكُلِّيَّةُ الْكُبْرَى، وَإِلَّا يَلْزَمُ الاخْتِلَافُ ، فَيُنْتِجُ الْكُلِّيَّتَانِ سَالِبَةً كُلِّيَّةً، وَالْمُخْتَلِفَانِ كَمَّا سَالِبَةً جُزْئِيَّةً ، بِالْخُلْفِ ، أَوْ بِعَكْسِ الْكُبْرَى ، أَوِ الصُّغْرَى ، ثُمَّ التَّرْتِيبِ ، ثُمَّ النَّتِيجَةِ .
And in the second figure: difference of the two premises in quality, and universality of the major. Otherwise, difference occurs. So the two universals produce a universal negative, and the ones that differ in quantity produce a particular negative, by reductio (al-khalf), or by conversion of the major or the minor, then arrangement, then the conclusion.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage sets the conditions for the second figure of the syllogism. Two conditions are required: (1) the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), and (2) the major premise must be universal. If these are not met, the syllogism is invalid (difference occurs). The valid moods are then derived: when both premises are universal, the conclusion is a universal negative (e.g., Cesare and Camestres). When the premises differ in quantity (one universal, one particular), the conclusion is a particular negative (e.g., Festino). The author notes these can be proven by reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf) or by converting the major or minor premise and then rearranging to reduce to the first figure.

Key Terms
خلف (khalf)
شكل ثان (shakl thani)
كيف (kayf - quality)
كل (kull - universal)
كبرى (kubra - major)
صغرى (sughra - minor)

Key Takeaway
The second figure requires the premises to differ in quality and the major to be universal, yielding only negative conclusions.

Conditions and Valid Moods of the Third Figure
Author specifies conditions for the third figure and its valid moods with reduction methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid moods of the third figure and state the logical conditions that make them valid.

Core Idea
The third figure yields particular conclusions and unique natural arrangements that make it indispensable.

The third figure of the categorical syllogism has distinct conditions: the minor premise must be affirmative, and at least one premise universal. These constraints produce only particular conclusions. Understanding these conditions allows you to recognise valid moods and appreciate why the third figure is not reducible without loss, as Ibn Sina noted in al-Shifa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure conditions
Examine the passage: it states two conditions for the third figure: the minor premise must be affirmative (إِيجَابُ الصُغْرَى) and one of the two premises must be universal (مَعَ كُلِّيَّةِ إِحْدَاهُمَا). These are necessary for valid inference.
The third figure has the middle term subject in both premises.
2
List the valid moods
From the conditions, enumerate the moods: when both premises are affirmative (وجبتان) with at least one universal, the conclusion is particular affirmative (موجبة جزئية). When one is universal negative and the other affirmative, the conclusion is particular negative (سالبة جزئية). The standard mnemonic moods are Darapti, Disamis, Datisi (affirmative) and Felapton, Ferison (negative).
The passage explicitly mentions 'الموجبتان مع الموجبة الكلية' and 'الكلية مع الموجبة الجزئية' for affirmative, and similarly for negative.
3
Determine proof methods
The passage outlines three ways to prove these moods: reductio ad absurdum (بالخلف), conversion of the minor or major (عكس الصغرى أو الكبرى) then rearrangement (الترتيب) then the conclusion, or reduction to the second figure by converting both premises (الرد إلى الثاني بعكسهما).
These methods show that the third figure moods are reducible to the first and second figures, though not superfluous.
4
Understand the natural arrangement argument
Ibn Sina (al-Shifa) is cited: the third and second figures have a special property—the natural arrangement (الطبيعي) of terms sometimes determines which term is subject or predicate. Inverting this would be unnatural, making these figures necessary for natural composition.
This justifies why the third figure is not merely a derivative of the first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَفِي الثَّالِثِ إِيجَابُ الصُّغْرَى مَعَ كُلِّيَّةِ إِحْدَاهُمَا ؛ لِيُنْتِجَ الْمُوجَبَتَانِ مَعَ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ، أَوِ الْكُلِّيَّةُ مَعَ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ مُوجَبَةً جُزْئِيَّةً ، وَمَعَ السَّالِبَةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ، أَوِ الْكُلِّيَّةُ مَعَ السَّالِبَةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ سَالِبَةً جُزْئِيَّةً بِالْخُلْفِ، أَوْ عَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى ، أَوِ الْكُبْرَى ، ثُمَّ التَّرْتِيبِ ، ثُمَّ النَّتِيجَةِ، أَوِ الرَّدُّ إِلَى الثَّانِي بِعَكْسِهِمَا. وَفِي الشِّفَاءِ أَنَّ هَذَيْنِ وَإِنْ رَجَعَا إِلَى الْأَوَّلِ فَلَهُمَا خَاصَّيَّةٌ، وَهِيَ أَنَّ الطَّبَعِيَّ فِي بَعْضِ الْمُقَدِّمَاتِ أَنَّ أَحَدَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ مُتَعَيِّنٌ لِلْمَوْضُوعِيَّةِ أَوِ الْمَحْمُولِيَّةِ، حَتَّى لَوْ عُكِسَ كَانَ غَيْرَ طَبَعِيٍّ ، فَالتَّأْلِيفُ الطَّبَعِيُّ رُبَّمَا لَمْ يَنْتَظِمْ إِلَّا عَلَى أَحَدِ هَذَيْنِ، فَلَيْسَ عَنْهُمَا غُنْيَةٌ.
And in the third figure: affirmation of the minor with universality of one of them, so that the two affirmatives together with the universal affirmative, or the universal with the particular affirmative produce a particular affirmative; and with the universal negative, or the universal with the particular negative produce a particular negative, by reductio, or conversion of the minor or the major, then arrangement, then the conclusion, or reduction to the second by converting both. And in al-Shifa: that these two, even if they reduce to the first, they have a special property: that the natural arrangement in some premises is that one of the two extremes is determined for subjecthood or predicateness, so that if it were converted it would be unnatural. So the natural composition may not be organized except upon one of these two, thus there is no need to dispense with them.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage details the conditions for the third figure: the minor must be affirmative, and one of the two premises must be universal. Under these, the valid moods produce particular conclusions. Specifically, when both premises are affirmative and at least one universal, the conclusion is particular affirmative (e.g., Darapti, Disamis, Datisi). When one premise is negative (and universal) and the other affirmative, the conclusion is particular negative (e.g., Felapton, Ferison). Proofs can be by reductio, conversion of a premise and rearrangement, or by reducing to the second figure via conversion of both premises. The author then cites Ibn Sina's al-Shifa, noting that the third and second figures, though reducible to the first, have a special property: in some premises, the natural order determines which term is subject or predicate, and reversing that would be unnatural. Hence, these figures are indispensable because natural composition may only be achieved through them.

Key Terms
الطبيعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The third figure requires the minor premise to be affirmative and at least one premise universal, producing only particular conclusions, and its natural arrangement makes it indispensable despite reducibility.

Conditions for Valid Fourth Figure Syllogisms
Conditions for fourth figure validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the valid moods of the fourth figure by applying the conditions of premise quality and quantity.

Core Idea
Fourth figure requires either both affirmatives with universal minor or mixed quality with one universal.

The fourth figure of categorical syllogisms has its own set of conditions. The author explains that valid moods require either both premises affirmative with the minor universal, or premises of differing quality with one universal. Understanding these conditions is crucial for checking the validity of syllogisms in this figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Ensure the syllogism is in the fourth figure: the middle term is predicate of the major premise and subject of the minor premise.
2
Check condition for both affirmative
If both premises are affirmative, the minor premise must be universal. Otherwise, the syllogism is invalid.
3
Check condition for mixed quality
If the premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), then at least one premise must be universal. This is required to avoid an invalid conclusion.
4
Identify the valid mood
Based on the conditions, match the pattern to one of the valid fourth figure moods (e.g., Bramantip: both universal affirmative, minor universal). The author lists several combinations.
5
Verify the conclusion
Check that the conclusion matches the expected pattern: e.g., for Bramantip, the conclusion is particular affirmative. If the conditions are not met, the syllogism is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَفِي الرَّابِعِ إِيجَابُهُمَا مَعَ كُلِّيَّةِ الصُّغْرَى، أَوِ اخْتِلَافُهُمَا مَعَ كُلِّيَّةِ إِحْدَاهُمَا ، وَإِلَّا لَزِمَ الاخْتِلَافُ ، فَيُنْتِجُ الْمُوجَبَةُ الْكُلِّيَّةُ مَعَ الْأَرْبَعَ ، وَالْجُزْئِيَّةُ مَعَ السَّالِبَةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ ، وَالسَّالِبَتَانِ مَعَ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ ، وَالسَّالِبَةُ الْكُلِّيَّةُ مَعَ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ مُوجَبَةً جُزْئِيَّةً ، إِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ سَلْبٌ ، وَإِلَّا فَسَالِبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ ، إِلَّا فِي وَاحِدٍ ، بِالْخُلْفِ، أَوْ بِعَكْسِ التَّرْتِيبِ ، ثُمَّ التَّتِيجَةِ ، أَوْ بِعَكْسِ الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، أَوِ الصُّغْرَى، أَوِ الْكُبْرَى. وَأَمَّا بِحَسَبِ الْجِهَةِ فِي الْمُخْتَلِطَاتِ فَفِي الْأَوَّلِ فِعْلِيَّةُ الصُّغْرَى، عَلَى مَذْهَبِ الشَّيْخ ; لِمَا قَدْ سَلَفَ، وَذَهَبَ هُوَ وَالْإِمَامُ إِلَى إِنْتَاجِ الْمُمْكِنَةِ، لِأَنَّهَا مُمْكِنَةٌ مَعَ الْكُبْرَى ، فَأَمْكَنَ وُقُوعُهَا مَعَهَا ، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ فَرْضِ الْوُقُوعِ مُحَالٌ.
And in the fourth figure: both premises affirmative with universality of the minor, or their difference with universality of one of them. Otherwise difference occurs. So the universal affirmative with the four, and the particular with the universal negative, and the two negatives with the universal affirmative, and the universal negative with the particular affirmative produce a particular affirmative, if there is no negation, otherwise a particular negative, except in one case, by reductio, or by reversal of arrangement, then the conclusion, or by conversion of both premises, or the minor, or the major. And as for according to modality in mixed premises, in the first figure the minor must be actual, according to the school of the Shaykh (Ibn Sina), for what has preceded. And he and the Imam (al-Ghazali?) went to the production of the possible, because it is possible with the major, so its occurrence with it is possible, so assuming its occurrence does not entail impossibility.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage covers the fourth figure and then transitions to modal syllogisms. For the fourth figure, conditions are: either both premises are affirmative with the minor universal, or they differ in quality with one universal. If not, the syllogism is invalid. The valid moods produce various conclusions: when the major is universal affirmative and the minor is universal affirmative, it yields a particular affirmative (Bramantip), etc. The author mentions exceptions (one case). Proofs are by reductio, reversal of order, or conversion. Then the author turns to modal syllogisms (mixed modalities). In the first figure, according to the Shaykh (Ibn Sina), the minor must be actual (actuality/necessity) and the major can be possible, producing a possible conclusion. The Shaykh and the Imam (perhaps al-Ghazali or Fakhr al-Razi) held that a necessary minor with a possible major yields a possible conclusion, because the conjunction is not impossible.

Key Terms
كلية
جزئية
موجبة
سالبة

C) It must be affirmative.
D) It must be negative.

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure yields valid conclusions only when its specific conditions of quantity and quality are met.

Mixed Modal Syllogisms in the First Figure
Rules for mixed modal syllogisms in first figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the validity of a mixed modal syllogism in the first figure by checking the modality of the minor premise.

Core Idea
In first figure mixed modals, the minor must be actual; otherwise the conclusion is invalid.

When premises have different modalities (necessity, actuality, possibility), special rules apply. According to Ibn Sina, in the first figure the minor premise must be actual (or necessary). This ensures the conclusion is possible when the major is possible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Ensure the syllogism is in the first figure: the middle term is subject of major and predicate of minor.
2
Check the modality of the minor
According to Ibn Sina, the minor premise must be actual (or necessary). If it is possible, the syllogism is invalid.
3
Check the modality of the major
The major can be possible, necessary, or actual. The conclusion's modality depends on the combination.
4
Determine the conclusion
If the minor is actual and the major is possible, the conclusion is possible. The reasoning is that the conjunction of actual and possible is not impossible.
5
Validate by the principle of non-contradiction
The author says: 'so its occurrence with it is possible, so assuming its occurrence does not entail impossibility.' This justifies the possible conclusion.

Second Figure Syllogism: Conditions and Moods
Author explains second figure conditions and resulting moods with proof methods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid second figure categorical syllogism and determine the correct conclusion mood using al-khalf or 'aks.

Core Idea
Second figure demands differing quality and a universal major – otherwise no valid conclusion.

The second figure is defined by the middle term being the predicate in both premises. But not every pair of premises in this figure yields a valid conclusion. The author gives two essential conditions: the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise must be universal. If these hold, the conclusion is a universal negative when both premises are universal, or a particular negative when they differ in quantity. To prove these moods, logicians use reduction: either by assuming the opposite (al-khalf) or by converting one premise and then ordering. This passage is the key to mastering the second figure.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the second figure
Identify that the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) is the predicate in both premises. This defines the figure.
2
Check condition 1: Differing quality
The two premises must be one affirmative and one negative. If both are affirmative or both negative, no valid conclusion can be drawn in the second figure.
3
Check condition 2: Universal major
The major premise (the one containing the predicate of the conclusion) must be universal (either universal affirmative or universal negative). The minor can be universal or particular.
4
Determine the conclusion mood
If both premises are universal (and differ in quality), the conclusion is a universal negative (e.g., No S is P). If the premises differ in quantity (one universal, one particular), the conclusion is a particular negative (Some S is not P).
5
Apply proof method: al-khalf or 'aks
To derive the conclusion, you can either assume the opposite (suppose the conclusion is false, derive a contradiction with a premise) or convert one premise (flip subject and predicate) and then reorder the premises to produce a valid first-figure syllogism.
The author lists: bi al-khalf (reductio), aw bi 'aks al-kubra aw al-sughra (conversion of major or minor), thumma al-tartib (then ordering), thumma al-natija (then the conclusion).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَفِي الثَّانِي اخْتِلَافُ الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ فِي الْكَيْفِ، وَكُلِّيَّةُ الْكُبْرَى، وَإِلَّا يَلْزَمُ الاخْتِلَافُ ، فَيُنْتِجُ الْكُلِّيتَانِ سَالِبَةٌ كُلَّيَّةٌ، وَالْمُخْتَلِفَانِ كَمَّا سَالِبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ ، بِالْخُلْفِ ، أَوْ بِعَكْسِ الْكُبْرَى ، أَوِ الصُّغْرَى ، ثُمَّ التَّرْتِيبِ ، ثُمَّ النَّتِيجَةِ .
In the second [figure]: the two premises differ in quality, and the major premise is universal; otherwise a contradiction would follow. So two universals produce a universal negative, and the two differing [in quantity] produce a particular negative, by reductio ad absurdum, or by conversion of the major or the minor, then ordering, then the conclusion.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage defines the essential conditions for a valid syllogism in the second figure. The second figure has the middle term as predicate in both premises. The author states two conditions: the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), and the major premise must be universal. If both were affirmative, no negative conclusion can follow; if both were negative, no conclusion at all. When both premises are universal but differ in quality, the conclusion is a universal negative. When they differ in quantity (one universal, one particular) but still differ in quality, the conclusion is a particular negative. The proof methods are then listed: by reductio (al-khalf, assuming the opposite of the conclusion), or by conversion of either premise, followed by proper ordering of the premises and conclusion. This is standard logical procedure for deriving conclusions in the second figure.

Key Terms
الْخُلْف
عَكْس
التَّرْتِيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Universal affirmative
B) Universal negative
C) Particular affirmative
D) Particular negative

Key Takeaway
A valid second figure syllogism requires premises differing in quality and a universal major premise, yielding a universal or particular negative conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid second figure categorical syllogism on a Fiqh topic, ensuring the premises differ in quality and the major is universal. State the mood and the proof method you would use to derive the conclusion (al-khalf or 'aks).

Third Figure Syllogism: Conditions and Moods
Author explains third figure conditions and resulting moods with proof methods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions of the third figure categorical syllogism and determine the conclusion mood for any valid premise pair.

Core Idea
The third figure's minor premise must be affirmative, and only one premise need be universal.

The third figure places the middle term as subject in both premises, which imposes special conditions. This passage from Bahr al-Ulum spells out those conditions and lists the moods that follow. Understanding these rules lets you verify whether a third figure argument is valid and what conclusion it yields.

Guided Path
1
Locate the middle term
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises. Visualize the arrangement: minor premise: M is P; major premise: M is S; conclusion: some S is P.
2
Apply the two conditions
The minor premise must be affirmative (ījāb al-ṣughrā), and at least one premise must be universal (kulliyya). These conditions prevent invalid conclusions.
Without these, the middle term fails to distribute properly.
3
Enumerate the premise pairs
List all combinations of quality and quantity that satisfy the conditions: both affirmative; one universal affirmative and one particular affirmative; one universal negative with an affirmative; one universal negative with a particular affirmative; and the universal negative with a particular negative.
The author explicitly mentions these combinations.
4
Determine each mood
From each pair, derive the conclusion: two affirmatives yield a particular affirmative; a universal affirmative with a particular affirmative yields a particular affirmative; a universal negative with any affirmative yields a particular negative; a universal negative with a particular negative also yields a particular negative.
All valid moods in the third figure have a particular conclusion.
5
Prove by reduction or reductio
The conclusion can be proven by conversion of the minor or major premise, then arranging the premises into a first or second figure, or by reductio ad absurdum (khulf). Alternatively, convert both premises to reduce to the second figure (radd ilā al-thānī).
This shows the interconnectedness of the figures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَفِي الثَّالِثِ إِيجَابُ الصُّغْرَى مَعَ كُلَّيَّةِ إِحْدَاهُمَا ; لِيُنْتِجَ الْمُوجَبَتَانِ مَعَ المُوجَبَةِ الْكُلّيّة، أو الكُلْيَةُ مَعَ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْجُزْنِيَّةِ مُوجَبَةٌ جُزْنِيَّةٌ ، وَمَعَ السَّالِبَةِ الكُليّةِ، أَوِ الْكُلِّيَّةُ مَعَ السَّالِبَةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ سَالِبَةً جُزْئِيَّةٌ بِالْخُلْفِ، أَوْ عَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى ، أَوِ الْكُبْرَى ، ثُمَّ التَّرْتِيبِ ، ثُمَّ النَّتِيجَةِ، أَوِ الرَّدُّ إِلَى الثَّانِي بِعَكْسِهِمَا.
In the third [figure]: the minor premise is affirmative with the universality of one of them, so that both affirmatives with the universal affirmative produce an affirmative particular, or the universal with the particular affirmative produce an affirmative particular, and with the universal negative, or the universal with the particular negative produce a negative particular, by reductio, or conversion of the minor or major, then ordering, then the conclusion, or reduction to the second figure by converting both.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions and moods of the third figure. The third figure has the middle term as subject in both premises. The conditions are: the minor premise must be affirmative, and at least one premise must be universal. The author enumerates the resulting moods: if both premises are affirmative, the conclusion is a particular affirmative; if one is universal affirmative and the other is particular affirmative, the conclusion is also particular affirmative. If one is universal negative, the conclusion is a particular negative. The proof methods are similar to the second figure: by reductio, or by conversion of either premise, then ordering, then conclusion. Additionally, the third figure can be reduced to the second figure by converting both premises (aks al-muqaddimatayn). This highlights the interdependence of the figures.

Key Terms
إيجَابُ الصُّغْرَى
الرَّدُّ إِلَى الثَّانِي
كُلَّيَّة
الْخُلْف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the required condition of the minor premise in the third figure?
A) It must be negative.
B) It must be universal.
C) It must be affirmative.
D) It must be particular.

Key Takeaway
The third figure yields only particular conclusions, requiring an affirmative minor and at least one universal premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid third figure categorical syllogism on a Fiqh topic. For example: 'Some contracts are sales; every sale requires consent; therefore, some contracts require consent.' Identify the minor premise, major premise, and middle term. Verify that the minor is affirmative and at least one premise is universal. State the mood and prove it by converting one premise to fit the first figure.
?
Using the same example, prove the conclusion by reductio ad absurdum: assume the opposite (no contracts require consent) and derive a contradiction.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Fourth Figure Syllogism: Conditions and Moods
Author explains conditions and moods for fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given fourth-figure categorical syllogism is valid by checking the conditions on quality and quantity of the premises.

Core Idea
The fourth figure requires either both affirmatives with universal minor, or differing quality with one universal.

The fourth figure of categorical syllogisms has specific conditions that must be met for a valid conclusion. This lesson will guide you through those conditions and the resulting moods, as outlined in the source.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises' quality and quantity
Check if both premises are affirmative and the minor premise is universal. If so, the syllogism is valid. If not, proceed to the next step.
The minor premise is the one containing the subject of the conclusion.
2
Check if premises differ in quality
If the premises are not both affirmative, they must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). Additionally, at least one of them must be universal. If these conditions hold, the syllogism is valid.
3
Determine the conclusion type
Based on the premises' combination, the conclusion will be a particular affirmative or particular negative, following the patterns listed in the passage.
For example, both affirmatives with universal minor yields an affirmative conclusion; universal negative with particular affirmative yields a particular affirmative (if no negation) or particular negative.
4
Note the exception and proof methods
There is one exception to these patterns. The validity of these moods can be proven by reductio, conversion of order, conversion of premises, or conversion of one premise.

هَذَا، وَفِي الرَّابع إِيجَابُهُمَا مَعَ كُلُّيَّةِ الصُّغُرَى، أَوِ اخْتِلَافُهُما مَعَ كُلّيّة إِحْدَاهُمَا ، وَإِلَّا لَزِمَ الاِخْتِلَافُ ، فَيُنْتِجُ الْمُوجَبَةُ الْكُلِّيَّةُ مَعَ الْأَرْبَعَ ، وَالْجُزْيَّةُ مَعَ السَّالِبَةِ الْكُلّيّةِ ، وَالسَّالِبَتَانِ مَعَ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ ، وَالسَّالِبَةُ الْكُلَّيَةُ مَعَ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْجُزنِيَّةِ مُوجَبَةً جُزْئِيَّةٌ ، إِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ سَلْبٌ ، وَإِلَّا فَسَالِبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ ، إِلَّا فِي وَاحِدٍ ، بِالْخُلْفِ، أَوْ بِعَكْسِ التَّرْتِيبِ ، ثُمَّ التَّتِيجَةِ ، أَوْ بِعَكْسِ الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، أَوِ الصُّغْرَى، أَوِ الْكُبْرَى. وَفِي الشَّفَاءِ أَنَّ هَذَيْنِ وَإِنْ رَجَعَا إِلَى الْأَوَّلِ فَلَهُمَا خَاصَّيَّةٌ، وَهِيَ أَنَّ الطَّبَعِيَّ فِي بَعْضِ الْمُقَدِّمَاتِ أَنَّ أَحَدَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ مُتَعَيِّنُ لِلْمَوْضُوعِيَّةِ أَوِ الْمَحْمُولِيَّةِ، حَتَّى لَوْ عُكِسَ كَانَ غَيْرَ طَبَعِيٌّ ، فَالتَّأْلِيفُ الطَّبَعِيُّ رُبَّمَا لَمْ يَنْتَظِمْ إِلَّا عَلَى أَحَدٍ هَذَيْنِ، فَلَيْسَ عَنْهُمَا غُنْيَةٌ.
This; and in the fourth [figure]: both premises are affirmative with universality of the minor, or they differ in quality with universality of one of them; otherwise a contradiction would follow. So the universal affirmative with the four [moods] produces, and the particular with the universal negative, and both negatives with the universal affirmative, and the universal negative with the particular affirmative produce a particular affirmative, if there is no negation, otherwise a particular negative, except in one [case], by reductio, or by conversion of order, then the conclusion, or by conversion of both premises, or the minor, or the major. And in al-Shifa: that these two [the second and third figures] even if they reduce to the first, still have a special property, namely that the natural [ordering] in some premises is that one of the two terms is determined for subjecthood or predication, such that if reversed it would be unnatural. So the natural composition may not be arranged except in one of these two, so they are indispensable.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage first explains the conditions and moods of the fourth figure. The conditions are: either both premises are affirmative while the minor is universal, or they differ in quality with one premise universal. The author lists the resulting combinations: universal affirmative with various moods, particular with universal negative, both negatives with universal affirmative, etc., leading to particular affirmative or negative conclusions, with one exception. The proof methods include reductio, conversion of order (aks al-tartīb), conversion of both premises, or conversion of one premise. Then the author cites Ibn Sina's al-Shifa, noting that the second and third figures, though reducible to the first, have a special property: in some arguments, the natural placement of terms requires that a particular term be subject or predicate in a fixed way, so that reversing it would be unnatural. Therefore, these figures are indispensable for natural logical expression. This highlights that the first figure is not always the most natural for every argument.

الصُّغُرَى
الْكُبْرَى
الْمُوجَبَة
السَّالِبَة

B) Both premises must be negative.
C) The major premise must be particular.

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure has specific conditions that ensure valid conclusions, and its moods can be proven through various reductions.

Ibn Sina on the Natural Ordering of Syllogisms
Author cites Ibn Sina on the necessity of second and third figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Sina's argument that the second and third figures are indispensable due to the natural ordering of terms.

Core Idea
Natural term ordering makes second and third figures indispensable despite reducibility.

After discussing the fourth figure, the author cites Ibn Sina's observation that the second and third figures, despite being reducible to the first, have a special property: they preserve the natural placement of terms. This lesson explores that insight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim from al-Shifa
Ibn Sina states that the second and third figures have a special property (khāṣṣiyya) that makes them indispensable.
Even though they can be reduced to the first figure.
2
Understand 'natural ordering' (ṭabī'ī)
In some premises, one term is naturally determined to be the subject or predicate. Reversing that would be unnatural.
This is a conceptual, not formal, necessity.
3
Recognize the implication
The natural composition (ta'līf ṭabī'ī) may only be possible in one of these figures, so we cannot always replace them with the first figure without losing naturalness.
Thus they are indispensable for natural logical discourse.
4
Apply to a concrete Islamic argument
Consider an argument about the necessity of worship: 'Every obligation is commanded; Nothing forbidden is commanded; therefore, Nothing forbidden is an obligation.' This is a second-figure argument with natural ordering. Converting to first figure would require unnatural rephrasing.
This illustrates Ibn Sina's point.

Modal Conditions for First Figure Mixed Syllogisms
Author discusses modal conditions for first figure mixed syllogisms with objection and reply

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the validity of a first figure mixed modal syllogism by applying the rules regarding actuality of the minor and the specific sense of possibility.

Core Idea
A possible minor yields a possible conclusion only if possibility is taken in the specific sense.

This passage addresses a classic problem in modal logic: when premises have different modalities, what conclusion follows? The author focuses on the first figure, where the minor is actual and the major is necessary. Understanding this rule is crucial for evaluating theological and legal arguments that combine different modal claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modalities
Read the passage and note that the minor is described as 'actual' (fi'liyya) and the major is taken as necessary. The conclusion is claimed to be possible.
The minor's actuality is key according to Ibn Sina.
2
Understand the objection to the possibility transfer
The first objection states that the possibility of one thing with another does not guarantee the possibility of their coexistence. The author provides a counterexample: the occurrence of the minor might cancel the truth of the major.
This is a subtle point about modal contexts.
3
Apply the distinction of possibility
The author's resolution: if possibility is taken in the most specific sense (al-imkān bi-l-ma'nā al-akhass), it is equivalent to absoluteness, and then the conclusion follows. Otherwise, it does not.
The specific sense excludes cases where the minor's occurrence would falsify the major.
4
Check for descriptive propositions
If the premises are not descriptive (wasfiyyāt), the conclusion takes the modality of the major. If they are descriptive, the conclusion follows the minor with adjustments.
Descriptive propositions involve temporary attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا بِحَسَبِ الْجِهَةِ فِي الْمُخْتَلِطَاتِ فَفِي الْأَوَّلِ فِعْلِيَّةُ الصُّغْرَى، عَلَى مَذْهَبِ الشَّيْخ ; لِمَا قَدْ سَلَفَ، وَذَهَبَ هُوَ وَالْإِمَامُ إِلَى إنتاج الْمُمْكِنَةِ، لِأَنَّهَا مُمْكِنَةٌ مَعَ الْكُبْرَى ، فَأَمْكَنَ وُقُوعُهَا مَعَهَا ، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ فَرْضِ الْوُقُوعِ مُحَالٌ. فَيَلْزَمُ النَّتِيجَةُ. وَأُجِيبَ تَارَةٌ بِأَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ ثُبُوتِ إِمْكَانِ شَيْءٍ مَعَ آخَرَ إِمْكَانُ تُبُوتِهِ مَعَهُ. أَلَا تَرَى مِنَ الْجَائِزِ أَنْ يَكُونَ وُقُوعُ الصُّغْرَى رَافِعًا لِصِدْقِ الْكُبْرَى ، وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ. وَأُخْرَى بِمَنْعِ لُزُومِ النَّتِيجَةِ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ الْوُقُوعِ؛ لِأَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِي الْكُبْرَى عَلَى مَا هُو أَوْسَطُ بِالْفِعْل فِي نَفْس الْأَمْرِ ، فَتَفَكَّرُ. وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ أَخْذَ الْإِمْكَانِ بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَخَص ، فَهُوَ مُسَاوِ لِلْإِطْلَاقِ ، كَالدَّوَامِ لِلضَّرُورَةِ بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَعَمِّ، فَيَلْزَمُ النَّيجَةُ ، وَإِلَّا فَلَا. ثُمَّ النَّنِيجَةُ كَالْكُبْرَى، إن كَانَتْ مِنْ غَيْرِ الْوَصْفِيَّاتِ، وَإِلَّا فَكَالصُّغْرَى، مَحْذُوفًا عَنْهَا قَيْدُ الْوُجُودِ، وَالضَّرُورَةُ الْمُخْتَصَّةُ بِالصُّغُرَى ، وَمُنْضَمَّا إِلَيْهَا قَيْدُ الْوُجُودِ فِي الْكُبْرَى.
As for modality in mixed [syllogisms], in the first figure: the minor is actual, according to the doctrine of the Shaykh, as previously; and he and the Imam went to the production of the possible, because it is possible with the major, so its occurrence with it is possible, so assuming occurrence does not entail an impossible thing. So the conclusion follows. And it was answered once: that from the established possibility of one thing with another, the possibility of its existence with it does not follow. Do you not see that it is possible that the occurrence of the minor could cancel the truth of the major? And there is something in this. And another time: by denying the entailment of the conclusion on the assumption of occurrence, because the judgment in the major is on that which is actually the middle term in itself, so reflect. And the truth is: if possibility is taken in the more specific sense, it is equivalent to absoluteness, just as permanence is to necessity in the more general sense. So the conclusion follows; otherwise not. Then the conclusion is like the major, if it is not among descriptive propositions; otherwise it is like the minor, with the condition of existence removed from it, and the necessity specific to the minor, and with the condition of existence added to it in the major.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage deals with modal syllogisms when premises have different modalities (mixed). Focus is on the first figure. The author states that according to the Shaykh (Ibn Sina), the minor premise must be actual (fi'liyya). Both Ibn Sina and the Imam (al-Razi) held that a possible minor with a necessary major yields a possible conclusion, because it is possible that the minor occurs with the major, so no impossibility arises from assuming occurrence. However, an objection is raised: from the possibility of one thing with another, it does not follow that it can exist with it, because the occurrence of the minor might cancel the truth of the major (e.g., if the minor's occurrence changes the conditions). Another objection denies that the conclusion follows even on assumption of occurrence, because the judgment in the major is about the middle term as actual in reality. The author then gives the correct position: if 'possibility' is taken in the more specific sense (al-imkān bi-l-ma'nā al-akhass), it is equivalent to absoluteness (al-iṭlāq), just as permanence (al-dawām) is to necessity in the more general sense. In that case, the conclusion follows; otherwise not. Then the author explains that the conclusion follows the major in modality unless the premises are 'descriptive' (wasfiyyāt), in which case it follows the minor, with certain adjustments regarding the condition of existence and necessity.

Key Terms
الْجِهَة
الْوَصْفِيَّات
الْإِمْكَان بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَخَص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, when does a possible minor in the first figure yield a valid conclusion?
A) When possibility is taken in the general sense.
B) When possibility is taken in the most specific sense, equivalent to absoluteness.
C) Only when the major is also possible.
D) Never, because possibility cannot coexist with necessity.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a mixed modal syllogism in the first figure hinges on taking 'possibility' in its most specific sense, which is equivalent to absoluteness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Objections and Replies on Modal Syllogisms
Author discusses modal conditions for first figure mixed syllogisms with objection and reply

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the two objections against the mixed modal syllogism and evaluate the author's replies.

Core Idea
Two objections challenge the modal transfer: coexistence possibility and the actuality of the middle term.

In this part of the passage, the author presents two objections that were raised against the claim that a possible minor with a necessary major yields a possible conclusion. Understanding these objections and the author's responses is essential for mastering modal syllogistic and defending it against sceptics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection
The first objection argues: from the possibility of one thing with another, it does not follow that they can actually co-exist, because the occurrence of the minor might cancel the truth of the major.
This objection attacks the assumption that the minor and major can be simultaneously true.
2
Identify the second objection
The second objection denies that the conclusion follows even on the assumption of occurrence, because the major's judgment is about the middle term as actual in itself, not about the minor's actuality.
This is a more subtle point about the scope of the major premise.
3
Understand the author's reply to both
The author concedes that the objections have force but then offers his resolution: if possibility is taken in the most specific sense (equated with absoluteness), then the conclusion follows; otherwise not. This distinction neutralizes the objections.
The author does not fully refute the objections but circumscribes the condition under which the rule holds.
