⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا بِحَسَبِ الْجِهَةِ فِي الْمُخْتَلِطَاتِ فَفِي الْأَوَّلِ فِعْلِيَّةُ الصُّغْرَى، عَلَى مَذْهَبِ الشَّيْخ ; لِمَا قَدْ سَلَفَ، وَذَهَبَ هُوَ وَالْإِمَامُ إِلَى إنتاج الْمُمْكِنَةِ، لِأَنَّهَا مُمْكِنَةٌ مَعَ الْكُبْرَى ، فَأَمْكَنَ وُقُوعُهَا مَعَهَا ، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ فَرْضِ الْوُقُوعِ مُحَالٌ. فَيَلْزَمُ النَّتِيجَةُ. وَأُجِيبَ تَارَةٌ بِأَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ ثُبُوتِ إِمْكَانِ شَيْءٍ مَعَ آخَرَ إِمْكَانُ تُبُوتِهِ مَعَهُ. أَلَا تَرَى مِنَ الْجَائِزِ أَنْ يَكُونَ وُقُوعُ الصُّغْرَى رَافِعًا لِصِدْقِ الْكُبْرَى ، وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ. وَأُخْرَى بِمَنْعِ لُزُومِ النَّتِيجَةِ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ الْوُقُوعِ؛ لِأَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِي الْكُبْرَى عَلَى مَا هُو أَوْسَطُ بِالْفِعْل فِي نَفْس الْأَمْرِ ، فَتَفَكَّرُ. وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ أَخْذَ الْإِمْكَانِ بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَخَص ، فَهُوَ مُسَاوِ لِلْإِطْلَاقِ ، كَالدَّوَامِ لِلضَّرُورَةِ بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَعَمِّ، فَيَلْزَمُ النَّيجَةُ ، وَإِلَّا فَلَا. ثُمَّ النَّنِيجَةُ كَالْكُبْرَى، إن كَانَتْ مِنْ غَيْرِ الْوَصْفِيَّاتِ، وَإِلَّا فَكَالصُّغْرَى، مَحْذُوفًا عَنْهَا قَيْدُ الْوُجُودِ، وَالضَّرُورَةُ الْمُخْتَصَّةُ بِالصُّغُرَى ، وَمُنْضَمَّا إِلَيْهَا قَيْدُ الْوُجُودِ فِي الْكُبْرَى.
As for modality in mixed [syllogisms], in the first figure: the minor is actual, according to the doctrine of the Shaykh, as previously; and he and the Imam went to the production of the possible, because it is possible with the major, so its occurrence with it is possible, so assuming occurrence does not entail an impossible thing. So the conclusion follows. And it was answered once: that from the established possibility of one thing with another, the possibility of its existence with it does not follow. Do you not see that it is possible that the occurrence of the minor could cancel the truth of the major? And there is something in this. And another time: by denying the entailment of the conclusion on the assumption of occurrence, because the judgment in the major is on that which is actually the middle term in itself, so reflect. And the truth is: if possibility is taken in the more specific sense, it is equivalent to absoluteness, just as permanence is to necessity in the more general sense. So the conclusion follows; otherwise not. Then the conclusion is like the major, if it is not among descriptive propositions; otherwise it is like the minor, with the condition of existence removed from it, and the necessity specific to the minor, and with the condition of existence added to it in the major.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage deals with modal syllogisms when premises have different modalities (mixed). Focus is on the first figure. The author states that according to the Shaykh (Ibn Sina), the minor premise must be actual (fi'liyya). Both Ibn Sina and the Imam (al-Razi) held that a possible minor with a necessary major yields a possible conclusion, because it is possible that the minor occurs with the major, so no impossibility arises from assuming occurrence. However, an objection is raised: from the possibility of one thing with another, it does not follow that it can exist with it, because the occurrence of the minor might cancel the truth of the major (e.g., if the minor's occurrence changes the conditions). Another objection denies that the conclusion follows even on assumption of occurrence, because the judgment in the major is about the middle term as actual in reality. The author then gives the correct position: if 'possibility' is taken in the more specific sense (al-imkān bi-l-ma'nā al-akhass), it is equivalent to absoluteness (al-iṭlāq), just as permanence (al-dawām) is to necessity in the more general sense. In that case, the conclusion follows; otherwise not. Then the author explains that the conclusion follows the major in modality unless the premises are 'descriptive' (wasfiyyāt), in which case it follows the minor, with certain adjustments regarding the condition of existence and necessity.

Key Terms
الْجِهَة
الْوَصْفِيَّات
الْإِمْكَان بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَخَص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the second objection against the modal transfer rule?
A) The minor cannot be actual and possible at the same time.
B) The conclusion does not follow because the major's judgment is about the middle term as actual in itself.
C) The major must be possible for the conclusion to be possible.
D) The minor's occurrence always cancels the major's truth.

Key Takeaway
The objections highlight that modal transfer is not automatic; the specific sense of possibility is the key condition for validity.

Second Figure Modal Syllogisms
Author outlines modal conditions for second figure syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a second figure categorical syllogism based on the permanence of the minor or conversion of the major negative.

Core Idea
In the second figure, the conclusion's modality depends on whether the minor is permanent or the major negative is converted.

The second figure of categorical syllogisms has unique modal constraints. When premises involve modalities like necessity (ḍarūra) and possibility (imkān), the conclusion's modality follows specific rules. This passage from Bahr al-‘Ulūm details those conditions, emphasizing the role of permanence (dawām) and the conversion of negative premises. We will walk through the rules to ensure your syllogisms are sound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal status of the minor premise
Check whether the minor premise is permanent (dā'ima, i.e., necessary or always true) or possible. This is the first condition given for the second figure.
Permanence here is akin to necessity but with nuances; refer to the key term al-dawām.
2
Check the major premise: negative and convertible or not
If the minor is not permanent, then the major negative must be convertible to achieve a valid mood. The conversion of a negative proposition (e.g., 'No A is B' becomes 'No B is A') is allowed.
Conversion is only applicable to negative propositions in this context.
3
Determine the conclusion's modality
If there is permanence in the premises (i.e., the minor is permanent or the conversion leads to permanence), the conclusion is permanent. If not, the conclusion follows the minor premise but with the conditions of existence (wujūd) and necessity (ḍarūra) removed, meaning the conclusion is possible or contingent.
The phrase 'makhdhūfan 'anhā qayd al-wujūd wa al-ḍarūra' means the conclusion loses those modal constraints.
4
Account for scholarly discussion
The author notes 'fīhi mā fīhi' (there is discussion), indicating disagreement among logicians. Be aware that not all details are settled; this is a condensed summary.
For deeper analysis, refer to longer works like al-Muṭawwalāt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَفِي الثَّانِي إِمَّا دَوَامُ الصُّغُرَى ، أَوِ انْعِكَاسُ سَالِبَةِ الْكُبْرَى ، وَكَوْنُ الْمُمْكِنَة مَعَ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ ، أَوْ كُبْرَى مَشْرُوطَةٌ، وَالتَّيجَةُ دَائِمَةٌ إِنْ كَانَ هُنَاكَ دَوَامٌ، وَإِلَّا فَكَالصُّغْرَى ، مَحْذُوفًا عَنْهَا قَيْدُ الْوُجُودِ وَالضَّرُورَةِ، وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ. وَفِي الثَّالِثِ مَا فِي الْأَوَّلِ ، وَالنَّتِيجَةُ كَالْكُبْرَى فِي غَيْرِ الْوَصْفِيَّاتِ، وَإِلَّا فَعَكْسُ الصُّغُرَى ، مَحْذُوفًا عَنْهُ لَادَوَامُهُ ، وَمَضْمُومًا إِلَيْهِ لَادَوَامُ الْكُبْرَى . وَأَحْكَامُ اخْتِلَاطِ الرَّابِعِ تُعْرَفُ فِي الْمُطَوَّلَاتِ.
And in the second [figure]: either the permanence of the minor, or the conversion of the negative of the major, and the state of the possible with the necessary, or a conditional major, and the conclusion is permanent if there is permanence, otherwise it is like the minor, with the condition of existence and necessity removed from it, and there is discussion. And in the third [figure]: what is in the first, and the conclusion is like the major in non-descriptive propositions, otherwise it is the converse of the minor, with its non-permanence removed and the non-permanence of the major added to it. And the rules of mixing in the fourth are known in the longer works.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage covers modal conditions for the second and third figures. For the second figure, two possibilities are given: either the minor premise is permanent (dā'ima, i.e., necessary or always true), or the major negative is converted (to achieve a proper mood). The possible premise with a necessary one, or a conditional major, leads to a conclusion that is permanent if there was permanence; otherwise, the conclusion follows the minor but with conditions of existence and necessity removed. The author notes there is discussion (fīhi mā fīhi) indicating scholarly disagreement. For the third figure, the conditions are the same as the first figure (previously discussed). The conclusion follows the major in modality unless the premises are descriptive (waṣfiyyāt), in which case the conclusion is the converse of the minor, with non-permanence removed and the non-permanence of the major added. The author then refers to longer works for the rules of mixing in the fourth figure, implying this is a standard reference point.

Key Terms
الدَّوَام
الْمُطَوَّلَات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Necessary
B) Possible (with existence and necessity conditions removed)
C) Impossible
D) Permanent

Key Takeaway
In the second figure, the conclusion's modality is permanent if permanence exists; otherwise, it follows the minor premise stripped of modal constraints.

Third Figure Modal Syllogisms
Author outlines modal conditions for third figure syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a third figure categorical syllogism and handle the special case of descriptive propositions.

Core Idea
In the third figure, the conclusion's modality mirrors the major unless the premises are descriptive, in which case it becomes the converse of the minor.

The third figure of categorical syllogisms, where the middle term is the subject of both premises, has its own modal rules. The author states that the conditions are the same as in the first figure, but with a twist: for descriptive propositions (waṣfiyyāt), the conclusion is the converse of the minor premise with modified modality. This distinction is crucial for accurate reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recall first figure modal rules
The passage says 'what is in the first [figure]' applies to the third figure for non-descriptive premises. So start by reviewing how modality transfers in first figure syllogisms: the conclusion generally follows the weaker premise, but details vary by mood.
This passage assumes familiarity with first figure modal logic.
2
Identify whether the premises are descriptive (waṣfiyyāt)
Descriptive propositions predicate a temporary or essential attribute (waṣf) of the subject. For example, 'Every human is rational' is descriptive of a permanent attribute, but 'Some humans are writing' is descriptive of a transient act. The rule changes for such propositions.
The term waṣfiyyāt is not defined here; rely on standard usage in logic.
3
If not descriptive: conclusion's modality equals major's modality
For non-descriptive (i.e., absolute) propositions, the conclusion takes the same modality as the major premise.
Example: major is necessary, conclusion is necessary.
4
If descriptive: conclusion is the converse of the minor with modifications
In descriptive propositions, the conclusion is the converse of the minor premise, but its modality is adjusted: the non-permanence (lā dawām) of the minor is removed, and the non-permanence of the major is added. This yields a weaker conclusion.
This is a technical maneuver to preserve validity while dealing with accidental attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَفِي الثَّانِي إِمَّا دَوَامُ الصُّغُرَى ، أَوِ انْعِكَاسُ سَالِبَةِ الْكُبْرَى ، وَكَوْنُ الْمُمْكِنَة مَعَ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ ، أَوْ كُبْرَى مَشْرُوطَةٌ، وَالتَّيجَةُ دَائِمَةٌ إِنْ كَانَ هُنَاكَ دَوَامٌ، وَإِلَّا فَكَالصُّغْرَى ، مَحْذُوفًا عَنْهَا قَيْدُ الْوُجُودِ وَالضَّرُورَةِ، وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ. وَفِي الثَّالِثِ مَا فِي الْأَوَّلِ ، وَالنَّتِيجَةُ كَالْكُبْرَى فِي غَيْرِ الْوَصْفِيَّاتِ، وَإِلَّا فَعَكْسُ الصُّغُرَى ، مَحْذُوفًا عَنْهُ لَادَوَامُهُ ، وَمَضْمُومًا إِلَيْهِ لَادَوَامُ الْكُبْرَى . وَأَحْكَامُ اخْتِلَاطِ الرَّابِعِ تُعْرَفُ فِي الْمُطَوَّلَاتِ.
And in the second [figure]: either the permanence of the minor, or the conversion of the negative of the major, and the state of the possible with the necessary, or a conditional major, and the conclusion is permanent if there is permanence, otherwise it is like the minor, with the condition of existence and necessity removed from it, and there is discussion. And in the third [figure]: what is in the first, and the conclusion is like the major in non-descriptive propositions, otherwise it is the converse of the minor, with its non-permanence removed and the non-permanence of the major added to it. And the rules of mixing in the fourth are known in the longer works.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage covers modal conditions for the second and third figures. For the second figure, two possibilities are given: either the minor premise is permanent (dā'ima, i.e., necessary or always true), or the major negative is converted (to achieve a proper mood). The possible premise with a necessary one, or a conditional major, leads to a conclusion that is permanent if there was permanence; otherwise, the conclusion follows the minor but with conditions of existence and necessity removed. The author notes there is discussion (fīhi mā fīhi) indicating scholarly disagreement. For the third figure, the conditions are the same as the first figure (previously discussed). The conclusion follows the major in modality unless the premises are descriptive (waṣfiyyāt), in which case the conclusion is the converse of the minor, with non-permanence removed and the non-permanence of the major added. Conditional Syllogisms: Composition and Figures
Author defines composition of conditional syllogisms and their figures, with a doubt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five compositions of conditional syllogisms and explain how the four figures govern them.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms follow categorical figures, but their premises are conditionals.

This passage extends categorical logic to conditional syllogisms. The author lists five ways to combine conjunctive, disjunctive, and categorical premises. Understanding these compositions and the figures that apply is essential for analyzing conditional arguments in Islamic reasoning, such as those found in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
List the five compositions
Write down each combination: two conjunctives, two disjunctives, categorical+conjunctive, categorical+disjunctive, conjunctive+disjunctive. This covers all pairings.
Conjunctives are 'if...then' statements; disjunctives are 'either...or'.
2
Identify the figures
The four figures (shapes) from categorical syllogisms apply here. The first figure is the principal (al-umda al-awwal).
The natural form (al-matbū‘) requires the two premises to share a complete part (juz’ tāmm).
3
Apply categorical conditions
The conditions for producing a conclusion (shurūṭ al-intāj) and the type of conclusion (ḥāl al-natīja) are the same as in categorical syllogisms.
This means rules like the middle term must be distributed, etc., apply analogously.
4
Analyze the doubt example
The author gives a doubt: 'whenever the two are one, it is a number'. Even incomplete, it shows a logical puzzle that challenges the theory.
You can try to complete the example: if 'the two are one' and 'the two are a number', does it produce a valid conditional conclusion?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الشَّرْطِيُّ يَتَرَكَّبُ مِنْ مُتَّصِلَتَيْنِ ، أَوْ مُنْفَصِلَتَيْنِ، أَوْ حَمْلِيَّةٍ وَمُتَّصِلَةٍ، أَوْ حَمْلِيَّةٍ وَمُنْفِصِلَةٍ ، أَوْ مُتَّصِلَةٍ وَمُنْفَصِلة . وَيَنْعَقِدُ فِيهِ الْأَشْكَالُ الْأَرْبَعَةُ ، وَالْعُمْدَةُ الْأَوَّلُ ، وَالْمَطْبُوعُ مِنْهُ اشْتِرَاكُ الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ فِي جُزْءٍ تَامٌ، وَشَرَائِطُ الْإِنْتاجِ وَحَالُ النَّتِيجَةِ فِيهِ كَمَا فِي الْحَمْلِيَّاتِ ، فَإنتاج اللزومِيَّتيْن لُزُومِيَّةٌ فِي الْأَوَّلِ بَيْن. وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ ، وَهُوَ أَنَّهُ يَصْدُقُ كُلَّمَا كَانَ الإثْنَانِ فَرْدًا كَانَ عَدَدًا ، وَكُلَّمَا
Then the conditional [syllogism] is composed of two conjunctives, or two disjunctives, or a categorical and a conjunctive, or a categorical and a disjunctive, or a conjunctive and a disjunctive. And the four figures are formed in it, and the main one is the first, and its natural [form] is the two premises sharing a complete part. And the conditions of production and the state of the conclusion in it are as in categoricals. So the production of two implicatives is an implicative in the first figure clearly. And here is a doubt: that it is true: whenever the two are one, it is a number, and whenever...
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage introduces conditional syllogisms (al-sharṭī). The author lists five possible compositions: both premises conjunctive (muttaṣila, i.e., implicative conditionals), both disjunctive (munfaṣila), one categorical (ḥamliyya) and one conjunctive, one categorical and one disjunctive, or one conjunctive and one disjunctive. The four syllogistic figures apply to these conditional syllogisms, with the first figure being the principal. The natural form (al-maṭbū‘) requires the two premises to share a complete part (i.e., a common full proposition or clause). The conditions for concluding and the nature of the conclusion follow the same rules as categorical syllogisms. For example, two implicative premises in the first figure yield an implicative conclusion (luzūmiyya) clearly. The author then mentions a doubt (shakk): an example that seems to be a counterexample, but the text cuts off: 'whenever the two are one, it is a number' — likely a logical puzzle. This doubt would be resolved in the original, but the excerpt ends here.

Key Terms
الشَّرْطِي
الْمُتَّصِلَة
الْمُنْفَصِلَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Three
B) Four

D) Six

Key Takeaway
Conditional syllogisms combine conjunctive, disjunctive, and categorical premises in five ways, but the four figures and conditions for validity are the same as in categorical syllogisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid conditional syllogism in the first figure using two conjunctive premises on a fiqh topic: e.g., 'If wine is intoxicating, it is forbidden; if it is forbidden, its sale is invalid; therefore, if wine is intoxicating, its sale is invalid.' Identify the complete part shared between premises.
?

Correct Modal Equivalence in Syllogisms
Author discusses responses to a modal syllogism objection and gives the correct view on conclusion modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the equivalence of 'imkān bi-l-maʿnā al-akhaṣṣ' to absolute modality and determine when a modal syllogism yields a necessary conclusion.

Core Idea
Possibility in the specific sense equals absoluteness; permanence equals broader necessity.

In this passage, the author corrects two flawed responses to an objection about modal syllogisms. He establishes that when possibility is taken in its specific sense (contingency), it is equivalent to absolute modality, just as permanence is equivalent to necessity in the broader sense. This equivalence is the key to determining whether the conclusion follows in a modal syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal terms
Find the terms 'imkān bi-l-maʿnā al-akhaṣṣ' (possibility in specific sense) and 'ḍarūra bi-l-maʿnā al-aʿamm' (necessity in broader sense) in the Arabic. Note their definitions given in the passage.
Also note 'dawām' (permanence) as equivalent to broader necessity.
2
Understand the inadequate responses
The author mentions two responses to an objection: (1) that possibility of two things together does not imply possibility of one with the other when the other occurs; (2) that the conclusion does not follow because the major is about the actual middle term. The author says 'there is in it what is in it' indicating these are insufficient.
3
Grasp the correct view
The author states the correct view: when possibility is taken in the specific sense, it is equivalent to absolute (muṭlaq), and permanence is equivalent to necessity in the broader sense. Therefore, the conclusion does follow. The conclusion's modality depends on whether the premises are descriptive or not.
The equivalence is crucial: in modal syllogisms, the modality of the conclusion is determined by the modality of the major premise unless the premises are descriptive (waṣfiyyāt).

فَيَلْزَمُ النَّتِيجَةُ. وَأُجِيبَ تَارَةٌ بِأَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ ثُبُوتِ إِمْكَانِ شَيْءٍ مَعَ آخَرَ إِمْكَانُ ثُبُوتِهِ مَعَهُ. أَلَا تَرَى مِنَ الْجَائِزِ أَنْ يَكُونَ وُقُوعُ الصُّغْرَى رَافِعًا لِصِدْقِ الْكُبْرَى ، وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ. وَأُخْرَى بِمَنْعِ لُزُومِ النَّتِيجَةِ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ الْوُقُوعِ؛ لِأَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِي الْكُبْرَى عَلَى مَا هُوَ أَوْسَطُ بِالْفِعْل فِي نَفْس الْأَمْرِ ، فَتَفَكَّرُ. وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ أَخْذَ الْإِمْكَانِ بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَخَصِّ ، فَهُوَ مُسَاوٍ لِلْإِطْلَاقِ ، كَالدَّوَامِ لِلضَّرُورَةِ بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَعَمِّ، فَيَلْزَمُ النَّتِيجَةُ ، وَإِلَّا فَلَا. ثُمَّ النَّتِيجَةُ كَالْكُبْرَى، إِنْ كَانَتْ مِنْ غَيْرِ الْوَصْفِيَّاتِ، وَإِلَّا فَكَالصُّغْرَى، مَحْذُوفًا عَنْهَا قَيْدُ الْوُجُودِ، وَالضَّرُورَةُ الْمُخْتَصَّةُ بِالصُّغْرَى ، وَمُنْضَمًّا إِلَيْهَا قَيْدُ الْوُجُودِ فِي الْكُبْرَى. وَفِي الثَّانِي إِمَّا دَوَامُ الصُّغْرَى ، أَوِ انْعِكَاسُ سَالِبَةِ الْكُبْرَى ، وَكَوْنُ الْمُمْكِنَةِ مَعَ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ ، أَوْ كُبْرَى مَشْرُوطَةٌ، وَالنَّتِيجَةُ دَائِمَةٌ إِنْ كَانَ هُنَاكَ دَوَامٌ، وَإِلَّا فَكَالصُّغْرَى ، مَحْذُوفًا عَنْهَا قَيْدُ الْوُجُودِ وَالضَّرُورَةِ، وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ. وَفِي الثَّالِثِ مَا فِي الْأَوَّلِ ، وَالنَّتِيجَةُ كَالْكُبْرَى فِي غَيْرِ الْوَصْفِيَّاتِ، وَإِلَّا فَعَكْسُ الصُّغْرَى ، مَحْذُوفًا عَنْهُ لَا دَوَامُهُ ، وَمَضْمُومًا إِلَيْهِ لَا دَوَامُ الْكُبْرَى . وَأَحْكَامُ اخْتِلَاطِ الرَّابِعِ تُعْرَفُ فِي الْمُطَوَّلَاتِ.
Then the conclusion follows. And it was answered sometimes: that the possibility of a thing with another does not entail the possibility of its existence with it. Do you not see that it is possible that the occurrence of the minor premise negates the truth of the major? And there is in it what is in it. And another time: by denying the necessity of the conclusion on the assumption of occurrence; because the judgment in the major is about what is actually the middle term in reality, so reflect. And the correct view is that taking possibility in the more specific sense is equivalent to absolute, like permanence for necessity in the more general sense, so the conclusion follows; otherwise not. Then the conclusion is like the major, if it is not among the descriptive propositions, otherwise like the minor, with the condition of existence removed from it, and the necessity specific to the minor, and added to it the condition of existence in the major. And in the second, either permanence of the minor, or conversion of the negative of the major, and the conjunction of the possible with the necessary, or a conditioned major, and the conclusion is permanent if there is permanence, otherwise like the minor, with the condition of existence and necessity removed from it, and there is in it what is in it. And in the third, what is in the first, and the conclusion is like the major in non-descriptive propositions, otherwise the converse of the minor, with its permanence removed, and the non-permanence of the major added to it. And the rulings of the mixture of the fourth are known in the elaborations.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about modal syllogisms, specifically regarding the modality of the conclusion in mixtures of possible and necessary premises. The author presents two responses to a previous objection: first, that possibility of two things together does not imply that one can exist with the other when the other actually occurs; second, by denying that the conclusion necessarily follows even if the premises are true, because the major premise is about the middle term in actuality. Then the author gives the correct view: when 'possibility' is taken in its more specific sense (i.e., contingent possibility), it is equivalent to absoluteness (i.e., non-necessity), just as 'permanence' is to necessity in the broader sense. Thus, the conclusion follows. The conclusion takes the modality of the major if the premises are not descriptive (i.e., not about states of affairs); otherwise, it takes the modality of the minor, with the condition of existence removed from it and the necessity specific to the minor, and the condition of existence added from the major. Then the author details the three figures: in the second figure, either the minor must be permanent, or the negative major must be converted, or the major is conditioned; the conclusion is permanent if there is permanence, otherwise like the minor. In the third figure, the conclusion follows the major in non-descriptive propositions, otherwise it is the converse of the minor with its permanence removed and the non-permanence of the major added. The rules for the fourth figure are left to detailed works.

الضرورة بالمعنى الأعم

A) The conclusion is necessary.
B) The conclusion is possible.
C) The conclusion is absolute (non-necessary).

Key Takeaway
In modal syllogisms, possibility in the specific sense (contingency) is equivalent to absolute modality, which determines the conclusion's modality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
Conclusion Modality and Descriptive Propositions
The author gives the rule for conclusion modality in descriptive vs non-descriptive propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of a syllogistic conclusion by identifying whether the premises are descriptive (waṣfiyyāt) or not and applying the rule that the conclusion follows the major in non-descriptive cases and the minor in descriptive cases.

Core Idea
Descriptive propositions shift conclusion modality from the major to the minor.

The author explains that the conclusion's modality depends on whether the premises are 'descriptive' (waṣfiyyāt). In non-descriptive syllogisms, the conclusion takes the modality of the major premise. In descriptive syllogisms, the conclusion takes the modality of the minor premise, but with the condition of existence removed from the minor and added from the major. This rule is essential for properly evaluating modal arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish descriptive from non-descriptive propositions
Descriptive propositions (waṣfiyyāt) are those where the predicate is attributed to the subject as a description of a state, rather than as a permanent attribute. For example, 'Zayd is writing' is descriptive; 'Zayd is human' is not.
In modal logic, this affects how the conclusion's modality is derived.
2
Apply the rule for non-descriptive premises
If the premises are not descriptive, the conclusion takes the modality of the major premise. For example, if the major is necessary and the minor is possible, the conclusion is necessary (if the major's modality is necessary).
This is straightforward.
3
Apply the rule for descriptive premises
If the premises are descriptive, the conclusion takes the modality of the minor premise, but with the condition of existence (qayd al-wujūd) removed from the minor and added from the major. Also, the necessity specific to the minor is removed.
The author says: 'مَحْذُوفًا عَنْهَا قَيْدُ الْوُجُودِ، وَالضَّرُورَةُ الْمُخْتَصَّةُ بِالصُّغْرَى، وَمُنْضَمًّا إِلَيْهَا قَيْدُ الْوُجُودِ فِي الْكُبْرَى'.

Modal Rules for the Second and Third Figures
The author gives modal rules for the second and third figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the specific modal rules for the second and third figures of the syllogism, including conditions like permanence of the minor, conversion of the major, and the case of the third figure where the conclusion is the converse of the minor.

Core Idea
Each syllogistic figure has distinct modal conditions that must be satisfied for a valid conclusion.

The author provides detailed rules for the modality of the conclusion in the second and third figures. In the second figure, the minor must often be permanent, or the major must be converted or conditioned. In the third figure, the conclusion follows the major in non-descriptive cases but is the converse of the minor in descriptive cases, with adjustments. These rules are essential for constructing valid modal syllogisms in Islamic logical debates.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second figure conditions
The author lists several options for the second figure: either the minor is permanent (dawām), or the negative major is converted (inʿikās), or the major is conditioned (mashrūṭa). The conclusion is permanent if there is permanence, otherwise it takes the modality of the minor with conditions removed.
Second figure: P-M, S-M -> conclusion S-P (P as middle).
2
Understand the third figure conditions
In the third figure, if the premises are not descriptive, the conclusion follows the major. If descriptive, the conclusion is the converse of the minor (ʿaks al-ṣughrā) with its permanence removed and the non-permanence of the major added.
Third figure: M-P, M-S -> conclusion S-P (M as middle).
3
Apply to an example
Take a second figure syllogism: 'Every permanent thing is stable' (major possible), 'Every body is temporally originated' (minor necessary). Is the conclusion permanent? According to the rule, if there is permanence (dawām) in the minor? Here the minor is necessary, so it is not permanent in the sense of permanence of the proposition? Actually, permanence here refers to the modality of the minor being permanent (i.e., always true). The author says 'either permanence of the minor, or conversion of the negative major, etc.' So we need to check if the minor is permanent. If not, the conclusion is like the minor with conditions. This is complex, but the step is to identify which condition applies.

فَيَلْزَمُ النَّتِيجَةُ. وَأُجِيبَ تَارَةٌ بِأَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ ثُبُوتِ إِمْكَانِ شَيْءٍ مَعَ آخَرَ إِمْكَانُ ثُبُوتِهِ مَعَهُ. أَلَا تَرَى مِنَ الْجَائِزِ أَنْ يَكُونَ وُقُوعُ الصُّغْرَى رَافِعًا لِصِدْقِ الْكُبْرَى ، وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ. وَأُخْرَى بِمَنْعِ لُزُومِ النَّتِيجَةِ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ الْوُقُوعِ؛ لِأَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِي الْكُبْرَى عَلَى مَا هُوَ أَوْسَطُ بِالْفِعْل فِي نَفْس الْأَمْرِ ، فَتَفَكَّرُ. وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ أَخْذَ الْإِمْكَانِ بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَخَصِّ ، فَهُوَ مُسَاوٍ لِلْإِطْلَاقِ ، كَالدَّوَامِ لِلضَّرُورَةِ بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَعَمِّ، فَيَلْزَمُ النَّتِيجَةُ ، وَإِلَّا فَلَا. ثُمَّ النَّتِيجَةُ كَالْكُبْرَى، إِنْ كَانَتْ مِنْ غَيْرِ الْوَصْفِيَّاتِ، وَإِلَّا فَكَالصُّغْرَى، مَحْذُوفًا عَنْهَا قَيْدُ الْوُجُودِ، وَالضَّرُورَةُ الْمُخْتَصَّةُ بِالصُّغْرَى ، وَمُنْضَمًّا إِلَيْهَا قَيْدُ الْوُجُودِ فِي الْكُبْرَى. وَفِي الثَّانِي إِمَّا دَوَامُ الصُّغْرَى ، أَوِ انْعِكَاسُ سَالِبَةِ الْكُبْرَى ، وَكَوْنُ الْمُمْكِنَةِ مَعَ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ ، أَوْ كُبْرَى مَشْرُوطَةٌ، وَالنَّتِيجَةُ دَائِمَةٌ إِنْ كَانَ هُنَاكَ دَوَامٌ، وَإِلَّا فَكَالصُّغْرَى ، مَحْذُوفًا عَنْهَا قَيْدُ الْوُجُودِ وَالضَّرُورَةِ، وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ. وَفِي الثَّالِثِ مَا فِي الْأَوَّلِ ، وَالنَّتِيجَةُ كَالْكُبْرَى فِي غَيْرِ الْوَصْفِيَّاتِ، وَإِلَّا فَعَكْسُ الصُّغْرَى ، مَحْذُوفًا عَنْهُ لَا دَوَامُهُ ، وَمَضْمُومًا إِلَيْهِ لَا دَوَامُ الْكُبْرَى . وَأَحْكَامُ اخْتِلَاطِ الرَّابِعِ تُعْرَفُ فِي الْمُطَوَّلَاتِ.
Then the conclusion follows. And it was answered sometimes: that the possibility of a thing with another does not entail the possibility of its existence with it. Do you not see that it is possible that the occurrence of the minor premise negates the truth of the major? And there is in it what is in it. And another time: by denying the necessity of the conclusion on the assumption of occurrence; because the judgment in the major is about what is actually the middle term in reality, so reflect. And the correct view is that taking possibility in the more specific sense is equivalent to absolute, like permanence for necessity in the more general sense, so the conclusion follows; otherwise not. Then the conclusion is like the major, if it is not among the descriptive propositions, otherwise like the minor, with the condition of existence removed from it, and the necessity specific to the minor, and added to it the condition of existence in the major. And in the second, either permanence of the minor, or conversion of the negative of the major, and the conjunction of the possible with the necessary, or a conditioned major, and the conclusion is permanent if there is permanence, otherwise like the minor, with the condition of existence and necessity removed from it, and there is in it what is in it. And in the third, what is in the first, and the conclusion is like the major in non-descriptive propositions, otherwise the converse of the minor, with its permanence removed, and the non-permanence of the major added to it. And the rulings of the mixture of the fourth are known in the elaborations.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about modal syllogisms, specifically regarding the modality of the conclusion in mixtures of possible and necessary premises. The author presents two responses to a previous objection: first, that possibility of two things together does not imply that one can exist with the other when the other actually occurs; second, by denying that the conclusion necessarily follows even if the premises are true, because the major premise is about the middle term in actuality. Then the author gives the correct view: when 'possibility' is taken in its more specific sense (i.e., contingent possibility), it is equivalent to absoluteness (i.e., non-necessity), just as 'permanence' is to necessity in the broader sense. Thus, the conclusion follows. The conclusion takes the modality of the major if the premises are not descriptive (i.e., not about states of affairs); otherwise, it takes the modality of the minor, with the condition of existence removed from it and the necessity specific to the minor, and the condition of existence added from the major. Then the author details the three figures: in the second figure, either the minor must be permanent, or the negative major must be converted, or the major is conditioned; the conclusion is permanent if there is permanence, otherwise like the minor. In the third figure, the conclusion follows the major in non-descriptive propositions, otherwise it is the converse of the minor with its permanence removed and the non-permanence of the major added. The rules for the fourth figure are left to detailed works.

انعكاس سالبة الكبرى
كبرى مشروطة
عكس الصغرى

Composition and Conditions of Conditional Syllogisms
Author explains types of conditional syllogisms and first figure conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five types of conditional syllogisms and determine whether a conditional syllogism in the first figure is productive.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms mirror categoricals: first figure needs a shared complete part.

In this passage, the author classifies conditional syllogisms into five types based on the kinds of premises. He then explains that the first figure is most important and requires the premises to share a complete part. Understanding this structure is essential for evaluating arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh where conditional reasoning is common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five compositions
List the five possible combinations: two conjunctive conditionals, two disjunctive conditionals, a categorical with a conjunctive, a categorical with a disjunctive, or a conjunctive with a disjunctive.
These compositions cover all possible pairings of categorical (ḥamliyya), conjunctive (muttaṣila), and disjunctive (munfaṣila) premises.
2
Locate the complete part
In the first figure, the two premises must share a complete part (juzʾ tāmm). This is analogous to the middle term in categorical syllogisms. For conjunctive conditionals, the shared part could be the antecedent or consequent of one matching the antecedent or consequent of the other.
Example: 'If A then B; if B then C' shares 'B' as complete part.
3
Apply the condition to an example
Take a conditional syllogism: 'If the world is temporally originated, it has a cause; if it has a cause, the cause is eternal.' The shared complete part is 'has a cause'. This syllogism is in first figure and should yield a necessary conditional conclusion.
4
Compare with categoricals
The author states that conditions of productivity and the state of the conclusion are the same as in categorical syllogisms. This means the first figure is always productive if the premises share the complete part, just as categorical first figure is always valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الشَّرْطِيُّ يَتَرَكَّبُ مِنْ مُتَّصِلَتَيْنِ ، أَوْ مُنْفَصِلَتَيْنِ، أَوْ حَمْلِيَّةٍ وَمُتَّصِلَةٍ، أَوْ حَمْلِيَّةٍ وَمُنْفَصِلَةٍ ، أَوْ مُتَّصِلَةٍ وَمُنْفَصِلَةٍ . وَيَنْعَقِدُ فِيهِ الْأَشْكَالُ الْأَرْبَعَةُ ، وَالْعُمْدَةُ الْأَوَّلُ ، وَالْمَطْبُوعُ مِنْهُ اشْتِرَاكُ الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ فِي جُزْءٍ تَامٍ، وَشَرَائِطُ الْإِنْتَاجِ وَحَالُ النَّتِيجَةِ فِيهِ كَمَا فِي الْحَمْلِيَّاتِ ، فَإِنْتَاجُ اللُّزُومِيَّتَيْنِ لُزُومِيَّةٌ فِي الْأَوَّلِ بَيِّنٌ.
Then the conditional syllogism is composed of two conjunctive conditionals, or two disjunctive conditionals, or a categorical and a conjunctive, or a categorical and a disjunctive, or a conjunctive and a disjunctive. And the four figures occur in it, and the mainstay is the first, and its natural feature is that the two premises share a complete part, and the conditions of productivity and the state of the conclusion in it are as in the categoricals, so the productivity of two necessary conditionals is a necessary conditional in the first figure, evidently.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage classifies conditional syllogisms (qiyās shurṭī). The author lists five possible compositions: both conjunctive (muttasil), both disjunctive (munfaṣil), a categorical with a conjunctive, a categorical with a disjunctive, or a conjunctive with a disjunctive. All four figures can occur in conditional syllogisms, but the first figure is the most important. Its natural characteristic is that the two premises share a complete part (i.e., the antecedent or consequent of one matches the antecedent or consequent of the other). The conditions for productivity and the nature of the conclusion are the same as in categorical syllogisms. Specifically, if both premises are necessary conditionals (luzumiyya), then the conclusion in the first figure is also a necessary conditional, and this is evident. For example, 'If it is a man, it is an animal; if it is an animal, it is a body' yields 'If it is a man, it is a body' with necessity.

Key Terms
الشّرطيّ
المتّصلة
المنفصلة
الجزء التام

Key Takeaway
A conditional syllogism in the first figure is productive only when the two premises share a complete part, just as categorical syllogisms require a middle term.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Puzzle of the Conditional Syllogism and Its Resolution
Author presents and resolves a conditional syllogism puzzle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a conditional syllogism with a coincidental premise and resolve the puzzle of an apparently valid syllogism yielding a false conclusion.

Core Idea
Not every conditional syllogism with true premises yields a true conclusion; premises must be necessary conditionals.

In this passage, we encounter a classic puzzle that troubled logicians: how can a conditional syllogism have true premises yet a false conclusion? The author walks through several proposed solutions and offers his own resolution based on the nature of conditionals and entailment from impossible premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the puzzle
Notice the conditional syllogism: 'Whenever two is odd it is a number; whenever it is a number it is even; therefore whenever two is odd it is even.' The premises seem true, but the conclusion is false because an odd number cannot be even. This challenges the validity of conditional syllogisms.
The puzzle hinges on the second premise being only coincidentally true, not necessary.
2
Examine the first solution
The first proposed solution is to deny that the major premise ('if it is a number, it is even') is a necessary conditional (luzumiyya); it is only coincidental (ittifaqiyya). A coincidental conditional does not support entailment.
This distinction is crucial: necessary conditionals express a real dependence, coincidental ones do not.
3
Consider the counter-response
An objector replaces the major with a chain of necessary conditionals: 'if it is a number, it exists; if it exists, it is even.' But this still leads to the false conclusion, showing the solution is not straightforward.
This counter-response attempts to salvage the argument by using necessary conditionals.
4
Analyze the author's own approaches
The author suggests two ways to block the syllogism: deny the minor premise (that two is odd) because impossibilities have no cause, or deny the major premise because the general (existence) does not entail the specific (being even). These show different ways to break the inference.
These approaches highlight the importance of the causal connection in conditionals.
5
Understand the correct resolution
The author's final answer: deny that the conclusion is false. From impossible premises (like 'two is odd'), any conclusion follows logically (ex falso quodlibet). Thus the conclusion is actually true under the assumption of the premises, resolving the puzzle.
This solution preserves the validity of the conditional syllogism form by noting that impossible antecedents make conditionals vacuously true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ ، وَهُوَ أَنَّهُ يَصْدُقُ كُلَّمَا كَانَ الْإِثْنَانِ فَرْدًا كَانَ عَدَدًا ، وَكُلَّمَا كَانَ عَدَدًا كَانَ زَوْجًا، مَعَ كَذِبِ النَّتِيجَةِ، وَحَلُّهُ - كَمَا قِيلَ : مَنْعُ كَوْنِ الْكُبْرَى لُزُومِيَّةً ، وَإِنَّمَا هِيَ اتِّفَاقِيَّةٌ. وَيُجَابُ بِأَنَّ قَوْلَنَا كُلَّمَا كَانَ عَدَدًا كَانَ مَوْجُودًا» لُزُومِيَّةٌ، فَإِنَّ الْعَدَدِيَّةَ مُتَوَقِّفَةٌ عَلَى الْوُجُودِ، وَكَذَا كُلَّمَا كَانَ مَوْجُودًا كَانَ زَوْجًا ، وَهُوَ مُنْتِجٌ بِزَعْمِكُمْ لِمَا مَنَعْتُمْ. أَقُولُ : وَلَكَ أَنْ تَمْنَعَ الصُّغْرَى؛ فَإِنَّا لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ عَدَدِيَّةَ الِاثْنَيْنِ الْفَرْدِ مَعْلُولُ الْوُجُودِ؛ لِأَنَّ الْمُمْتَنِعَاتِ غَيْرُ مُعَلَّلَةٍ. وَأَنْ تَمْنَعَ الْكُبْرَى؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّ الْعَامَّ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ الْخَاصَّ ؛ لِأَنَّ وُجُودَ الِاثْنَيْنِ الْفَرْدِ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ وُجُودِ الْإِثْنَيْنِ. نَعَمْ، تَصْدُقُ اتِّفَاقِيَّةٌ ، وَلَوْ تَشَبَّثَ بِكَوْنِهَا مِنْ لَوَازِمِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ لَلَزِمَ صِدْقُ النَّتِيجَةِ الْمَفْرُوضِ كَذِبُهَا فِي هَذَا الْجَوَابِ، فَتَأَمَّلْ . وَاخْتَارَ الرَّئِيسُ فِي الْحَلِّ ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى رَأْيِهِ أَنَّ الصُّغْرَى كَاذِبَةٌ. أَقُولُ: قَوْلُنَا كُلَّمَا لَمْ يَكُنِ الْإِثْنَانِ عَدَدًا لَمْ يَكُنْ فَرْدًا يَصْدُقُ لُزُومِيَّةً ; فَإِنَّ انْتِفَاءَ الْعَامِّ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِانْتِفَاءِ الْخَاصِّ ، وَهُوَ يَنْعَكِسُ بِعَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ إِلَى تِلْكَ الصُّغْرَى ، وَمِنْهُ يَتَبَيَّنُ ضُعْفُ مَذْهَبِهِ، وَالْحَقُّ فِي الْجَوَابِ مَنْعُ كَذِبِ النَّتِيجَةِ ; بِنَاءً عَلَى تَجْوِيزِ الِاسْتِلْزَامِ بَيْنَ الْمُتَنَافِيَيْنِ. وَبَقَايَا الْبَحْثِ فِي الْمَبْسُوطَاتِ.
And here there is a doubt: that it is true that whenever two is odd it is a number, and whenever it is a number it is even, with the conclusion false. Its solution, as was said: denial that the major is a necessary conditional; rather, it is a coincidental one. And it is answered that our statement 'Whenever it is a number it exists' is necessary, for numberhood depends on existence, and likewise 'Whenever it exists it is even', and it produces according to your claim what you denied. I say: And you can deny the minor; for we do not grant that the numberhood of two being odd is an effect of existence, because impossibilities are not caused. And you can deny the major, based on the fact that the general does not entail the specific; because the existence of two being odd is part of the existence of two. Yes, a coincidental conditional is true. And if he clung to it being among the necessary concomitants of the essence, then the conclusion would have to be true, but it is assumed false in this answer, so reflect. And the Chief (Ibn Sina) chose in the solution, based on his view, that the minor is false. I say: our statement 'Whenever two is not a number it is not odd' is true as a necessary conditional; for the negation of the general entails the negation of the specific, and it converts by contraposition to that minor, and from this the weakness of his view becomes clear. And the correct answer is the denial of the falsity of the conclusion, based on allowing entailment between two contradictories. And the remaining research is in the detailed works.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents a logical puzzle. The example: 'Whenever two is odd, it is a number; whenever it is a number, it is even' — this seems to have a true conjunction of premises but a false conclusion ('Whenever two is odd, it is even'), since if two is odd, it cannot be even. The puzzle is that the syllogism appears valid but yields a falsehood. The author reports a solution: deny that the major premise ('if it is a number, it is even') is a necessary conditional; it is merely coincidental (ittifāqiyya), because not every number is even. A further objection is raised: one can replace the major with a necessary conditional chain: 'Whenever it is a number, it exists; whenever it exists, it is even' — but this is problematic. The author then offers his own analysis: one can deny the minor premise ('two is odd') because the numberhood of an odd two is an impossibility, and impossibilities have no cause. Or one can deny the major because the general (existence) does not entail the specific (being even). Ibn Sina (al-Ra'is) held that the minor is false. The author criticizes Ibn Sina's view by showing that the contrapositive of a true general conditional would support the original minor, implying its truth. The correct answer according to the author is to deny that the conclusion is false, because it is actually true under the assumption of the premises, by allowing entailment between contradictories (i.e., from impossible premises, anything follows). The detailed resolution is left to longer works.

Key Terms
الشّكّ
اللّزوميّة
الاتّفاقيّة
عكس النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The major premise is coincidental.

C) The conclusion is not false because it follows from premises that are impossible.
D) The syllogism is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The puzzle teaches that conditional syllogisms require necessary conditionals for validity; otherwise, a false conclusion may arise, which can be resolved by recognizing that from impossible premises anything follows.

The Syllogism

Valid Inferences in Conjunctive Exceptive Syllogisms
Conditions for valid exceptive syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two valid forms of exceptive syllogisms with conjunctive conditionals and distinguish them from invalid forms.

Core Idea
Only affirming the antecedent and denying the consequent yield valid conclusions in conjunctive conditionals.

In this lesson, we dive into the exceptive syllogism (qiyās istithnā'ī) as explained by the author. You already know that a syllogism combines premises to reach a conclusion. Here, the first premise is a conditional statement, and the second premise affirms or denies one part. The author lays down strict conditions and spells out which combinations produce certainty. By the end of this lesson, you will master the two valid moods and avoid common pitfalls that even some scholars debated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components
An exceptive syllogism always has two premises: a conditional (sharṭiyya) and an exception (istithnā') that either affirms (waḍ'iyya) or denies (raf'iyya) a part of the conditional. Locate these in the Arabic passage.
2
Check the conditions
The conditional must be an affirmative necessary conditional (luzūmiyya) or an adversative one (ʿinādiyya). Moreover, either the conditional itself or the exception must be universal (kulliyya). Why? Because if the conditional is not universal, the inference may not hold for all cases.
3
Apply the two valid rules
For a conjunctive conditional (muttasila), the author states: affirming the antecedent (malzūm) yields the consequent (lāzim), and denying the consequent yields denying the antecedent. These are modus ponens and modus tollens. Write an example in your own words to confirm you understand.
The author explicitly notes these are one-way, not reversible.
4
Recognize the invalid forms
Why can't we affirm the consequent or deny the antecedent? The author explains: the consequent may be more general (aʿammiyya). For instance, from 'If it is a liquid intoxicant, then it is haram', affirming 'it is haram' does not guarantee it is a liquid intoxicant—it could be another type of haram. Similarly, denying 'it is a liquid intoxicant' does not make it not haram.
This is the key insight: the consequent can be broader.
5
Examine the puzzle
The author mentions a puzzle (shall) where some scholars object that even denying the consequent may not be valid. This arises if the consequent can be absent while the antecedent is still present—i.e., the entailment is not necessary. The solution is that the conditional must be necessary (luzūmiyya) and the terms properly related. Most logicians accept modus tollens as valid under the stated conditions.
The text is cut off, but the puzzle reminds us how scholars critically examined even accepted rules.

Commentary
This passage defines the exceptive syllogism (qiyās istithnā'ī), which consists of a conditional premise and another premise that either affirms or denies part of the conditional. The conditional must be an affirmative necessary conditional (luzūmiyya) or an adversative one (ʿinādiyya). The conditional or the exception must be universal. In the conjunctive conditional (muttasila), affirming the antecedent yields the consequent, because the existence of the antecedent (malzūm) entails the existence of the consequent (lāzim), but not vice versa, as the consequent may be more general. Denying the consequent yields the denial of the antecedent, because the negation of the consequent entails the negation of the antecedent, again not vice versa. The author then mentions another puzzle: some deny that denial of the consequent entails denial of the antecedent, possibly because the consequent could have multiple antecedents. The text cuts off, but the puzzle is about the possibility of the consequent being more general, so denial of the consequent does not necessarily deny the antecedent if the consequent can be absent while the antecedent is still present? Actually, the standard rule is that denying the consequent is valid, but the puzzle argues that it might not be, and the solution is that the conditional must be necessary and the terms must be properly related. The author leaves the rest to longer works.

Resolving a False Conclusion in Conditional Syllogism
Author examines a conditional syllogism that appears to yield a false conclusion and gives multiple solutions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse a conditional syllogism with a seemingly false conclusion and evaluate the proposed solutions based on the necessity of implication.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism can be salvaged by questioning the necessity of its premises, not just their truth.

In this passage, a conditional syllogism is presented: 'If it is a number, it is even; if it is even, it exists; therefore if it is a number, it exists.' The conclusion appears false because existence is broader than number. The author walks through several proposed solutions, each challenging a different aspect of the syllogism. Your task is to follow this logical dialectic and understand how the necessity of implication, not just truth, determines validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional syllogism
Locate the two conditional premises and the conditional conclusion. The syllogism is: Major premise: 'Whenever it is a number, it is even.' Minor premise: 'Whenever it is even, it exists.' Conclusion: 'Whenever it is a number, it exists.' The conclusion is claimed to be false.
The conclusion appears false because existence (al-wujud) is a broader category than number (al-adad).
2
Understand the first solution: deny major as necessary
Some argue the major premise ('if number then even') is not necessary (luzumiyya) but merely accidental (ittifaqiyya). The author counters that 'if number then exists' is necessary, so the same reasoning should apply. This shows that the problem lies not in the major's necessity but elsewhere.
The author's counter-argument uses a parallel necessary conditional.
3
Explore denying the minor premise
The author suggests denying the minor premise: 'the numberhood of two-odd is not caused by existence, because impossible things (like two-odd) are not caused.' This challenges the connection between numberhood and existence for the problematic case.
'Two-odd' is an impossible entity (a number that is both two and odd), so it may not follow the usual causal rules.
4
Explore denying the major premise using general/specific distinction
Alternatively, deny the major premise because the general (existence) does not entail the specific (even). The existence of 'two-odd' is part of 'two's existence but does not make it even. This works only if the implication is accidental; if it were necessary, the conclusion would be true.
This distinction between general and specific is a key logical insight.
5
Consider the author's final resolution: deny the falsity of the conclusion
The author's own view is that the conclusion is not false; we should allow implication between contradictory things. That is, a conditional can be true even if its antecedent and consequent are incompatible in reality. This preserves the validity of the syllogism without denying premises.
This resolution allows the syllogism to remain valid by reinterpreting what the conclusion actually asserts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كَانَ عَدَدًا كَانَ زَوْجًا، مَعَ كَذِبِ النَّتِيجَةِ، وَحَلُهُ - كَمَا قِيلَ : مَنْعُ كَوْنِ الْكُبْرَى لُزُومِيَّةٌ ، وَإِنَّمَا هِيَ اتِّفَاقِيَّةٌ. وَيُجَابُ بِأَنَّ قَوْلَنَا كُلَّمَا كَانَ عَدَدًا كَانَ مَوْجُودًا» لُزُومِيَّةٌ، فَإِنَّ الْعَدَدِيَّةَ مُتَوَقِّفَةٌ عَلَى الْوُجُودِ، وَكَذَا كُلَّمَا كَانَ مَوْجُودًا كَانَ زَوْجًا ، وَهُوَ مُنْتِجُ بِزَعْمِكُمْ لِمَا مَنَعْتُمْ. أَقُولُ : وَلَكَ أَنْ تَمْنَعَ الصُّغْرَى؛ فَإِنَّا لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ عَدَدِيَّةَ الاِثْنَيْنِ الْفَرْدِ مَعْلُولُ الْوُجُودِ؛ لِأَنَّ الْمُمْتَنِعَاتِ غَيْرُ مُعَلَّلَةٍ. وَأَنْ تَمْنَعَ الْكُبْرَى؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّ الْعَامَّ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ الْخَاصَّ ؛ لِأَنَّ وُجُودَ الاِثْنَيْنِ الْفَرْدِ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ وُجُودِ الإثْنَيْنِ. نَعَمْ، تَصْدُقُ اتِّفَاقِيَّةٌ ، وَلَوْ تَشَبَّتَ بِكَوْنِهَا مِنْ لَوَازِمِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ لَلَزِمَ صِدْقُ النَّتِيجَةِ الْمَفْرُوض كَذِبُهَا فِي هَذَا الْجَوَابِ، فَتَأَمَّلْ . وَاخْتَارَ الرَّئِيسُ فِي الْحَلِّ ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى رَأْيِهِ أَنَّ الصُّغْرَى كَاذِبَةٌ. أَقُولُ: قَوْلُنَا كُلَّمَا لَمْ يَكُن الإِثْنَانِ عَدَدًا لَمْ يَكُنْ فَرْدًا يَصْدُقُ لُزُومِيَّةٌ ؛ فَإِنَّ انْتِفَاءَ الْعَامُ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِانْتِفَاءِ الْخَاص ، وَهُوَ يَنْعَكِسُ بِعَكْسِ النَّقِيض إِلَى تِلْكَ الصُّغْرَى ، وَمِنْهُ يَتَبَيَّنُ ضُعْفُ مَذْهَبِهِ، وَالْحَقُّ فِي الْجَوَابِ مَنْعُ كَذِبِ النَّتِيجَةِ ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى تَجْوِيزِ الاسْتِلْزَام بَيْنَ الْمُتَنَافِيْنِ. وَبَقَايَا الْبَحْثِ فِي الْمَبْسُوطَاتِ.
If it is a number, it is even, with the conclusion being false. Its solution, as was said: the major premise is denied to be necessary; rather it is accidental. And it is answered that our statement 'Whenever it is a number, it exists' is necessary, for numberhood depends on existence. And similarly 'Whenever it exists, it is even' produces [the conclusion] according to your claim, for that which you denied. I say: You may deny the minor premise, for we do not accept that the numberhood of two-odd is caused by existence, because impossible things are not caused. And you may deny the major premise, based on the fact that the general does not entail the specific, because the existence of two-odd is part of the existence of two. Yes, it is true as an accidental [implication]. And if he clings to it being from the necessary concomitants of quiddity, then the truth of the conclusion would be necessary, given its assumed falsity in this answer. So reflect. And the Chief (Ibn Sina) chose in the solution, based on his opinion, that the minor premise is false. I say: Our statement 'Whenever two is not a number, it is not odd' is true as necessary, for the negation of the general entails the negation of the specific. And that is converted by contraposition to that minor premise, and from it the weakness of his doctrine becomes clear. The truth in the answer is to deny the falsity of the conclusion, based on allowing implication between contradictory things. The remainder of the discussion is in the extensive works.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage addresses a problematic conditional syllogism that appears to yield a false conclusion. The syllogism is: 'If it is a number, it is even; if it is even, it is existent; therefore if it is a number, it is existent. But the conclusion is false because existent is wider than number.' The author presents various solutions. First, some deny that the major premise is necessary, claiming it is only accidental. But the author responds that 'everything that is a number exists' is necessary because numberhood requires existence. Then the author suggests denying the minor premise: 'the numberhood of two-odd is not caused by existence, because impossible things (like two-odd) are not caused'. Alternatively, deny the major premise because the general (existent) does not entail the specific (even); two-odd exists as part of two's existence but is not even. However, this only works if the major is taken as accidental. If one insists it is a necessary concomitant of quiddity, then the conclusion would have to be true, contradicting the assumed falsity. Ibn Sina (al-Ra'is) holds that the minor is false. The author counters by contraposition: 'if it is not a number, it is not odd' is true necessarily, and by contraposition yields the original minor, showing its truth. The author's final position is that the falsity of the conclusion should be denied, based on allowing implication between contradictory things (i.e., the conditional can hold even if the terms are incompatible in reality). This is a typical logical dialectic where each position is examined.

اتفاقية

Valid Forms of Conditional Syllogism
Defines structure and valid moods of conditional syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two valid forms of conditional syllogism (modus ponens and modus tollens) and distinguish them from the invalid forms.

Core Idea
Affirming antecedent or denying consequent yields valid inference.

The author presents the basic structure of the conditional syllogism (istiṯnāʾī), which consists of a conditional premise and an exception that affirms or denies one part. He explains that for the connected conditional (muttaṣila), affirming the antecedent entails the consequent, and denying the consequent entails denying the antecedent. The reverse forms are invalid because the consequent may be more general. This passage equips you with the foundational rules for evaluating conditional arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Locate the 'if-then' statement in the argument. The passage discusses the connected conditional (muttaṣila), where the antecedent is the sufficient condition for the consequent.
2
Identify the exception premise
Determine whether the second premise affirms or denies a part of the conditional. The author calls it 'waḍʿiyya' (affirmative) or 'rafʿiyya' (negative).
3
Apply the rule for affirming antecedent
If the exception affirms the antecedent, then conclude the consequent. This is valid because the existence of the necessary (malzūm) entails the existence of the consequent (lāzim).
4
Apply the rule for denying consequent
If the exception denies the consequent, then conclude the denial of the antecedent. This is valid because the non-existence of the consequent entails the non-existence of the necessary.
5
Distinguish invalid forms
Note that affirming the consequent or denying the antecedent does not yield a valid conclusion, because the consequent may be more general than the antecedent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالاِسْتِثْنَائِيُّ يَتَرَكَّبُ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ : شَرْطِيَّةٍ، وَوَضْعِيَّةٍ أَوْ رَفْعِيَّةٍ، وَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ كَوْنِهَا مُوجَبَةً لُزُومِيَّةَ ، أَوْ عِنَادِيَّةً ، وَمِنْ كُلِّيَّةِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ أَوِ الاِسْتِثْنَاءِ، فَفِي الْمُتَّصِلَةِ يُنْتِجُ وَضْعُ الْمُقَدَّمِ وَضْعَ التَّالِي لِأَنَّ وُجُودَ الْمَلْزُومِ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِوُجُودِ اللَّازِمِ، وَلَا عَكْسَ؛ لِجَوَازِ أَعَمِّيَّةِ اللَّازِمِ ، وَرَفْعُ التَّالِي رَفْعَ الْمُقَدَّمِ؛ فَإِنَّ انْتِفَاءَ اللَّازِمِ يَسْتَلْزِمُ انْتِفَاءَ الْمَلْزُومِ، وَلَا عَكْسَ. وَهَهُنَا شَلٌّ ، وَقِيلَ عَوِيصٌ ، وَهُوَ مَنْعُ اسْتِلْزَامِ الرَّفْعِ الرَّفْعَ؛ لِجَوَازِ اسْتِحَالَةِ انْتِفَاءِ اللَّازِمِ ، فَإِذَا وَقَعَ لَمْ يَبْقَ اللُّزُومُ مَعَهُ، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ انْتِفَاءُ الْمَلْزُومِ. أَقُولُ : حَلْهُ: إِنَّ اللُّزُومَ حَقِيقَةٌ امْتِنَاعُ الانْفِكَاكِ فِي جَمِيعِ الْأَوْقَاتِ، فَوَقْتُ الانْفِكَاكِ - وَهُوَ وَقْتُ عَدَمِ بَقَاءِ اللُّزُومِ - دَاخِل فِي الْجَمِيعِ، فَهَذَا الْمَنْعُ يَرْجِعُ إِلَى مَنْعِ اللُّزُومِ، وَقَدْ فُرِضَ وُجُودُهُ ، هَذَا خُلْفٌ.
And the conditional syllogism (istiṯnāʾī) is composed of two premises: a conditional (šarṭiyya) and an affirmative (waḍʿiyya) or negative (rafʿiyya). It is necessary that the conditional be affirmative, necessary (luzūmiyya) or contradictory (ʿinādiyya), and that either the conditional or the exception be universal. So in the connected (muttaṣila), affirming the antecedent produces affirming the consequent, because the existence of the necessary (malzūm) entails the existence of the consequent (lāzim), and not vice versa, due to the possibility of the consequent being more general. And denying the consequent produces denying the antecedent, because the non-existence of the consequent entails the non-existence of the necessary, and not vice versa. And here there is a difficulty, it is said to be a knotty problem: it is denying that the denial [of the consequent] entails the denial [of the antecedent], due to the possibility of the non-existence of the consequent being impossible. For if it occurs, the implication no longer remains with it, so the non-existence of the necessary does not follow. I say: its solution: implication (luzūm) is in reality the impossibility of separation (infikāk) at all times. So the time of separation—which is the time when the implication no longer holds—is included in 'all times'. So this denial reduces to a denial of the implication, which has been assumed to exist. This is a contradiction (ḵulf).
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage explains the structure of conditional syllogism (الاستثنائي). It consists of a conditional premise (شرطية) and either an affirmation (وضعية) or a denial (رفعية) of one part. The conditional must be affirmative and either necessary (لزومية) or contradictory (عنادية, meaning an exclusive disjunction?). Both the conditional and the exception must be universal. For the connected conditional (المتصلة), affirming the antecedent yields the consequent, because the existence of the necessary (malzūm) implies the existence of the consequent (lāzim). The reverse is invalid because the consequent might be more general. Denying the consequent yields denying the antecedent, because the non-existence of the consequent implies the non-existence of the necessary; again, the reverse is invalid. Then the author raises a famous difficulty (شلّ): denying the consequent does not necessarily entail denying the antecedent if the non-existence of the consequent is impossible. For example, if the consequent is something that must exist (like existence itself), then its denial is impossible, and when we suppose it occurs, the implication no longer holds, so the antecedent does not have to be denied. The author's solution: implication (لزوم) is defined as the impossibility of separation (انفكاك) in all times. The time when the separation occurs—i.e., when the implication fails—is itself included in 'all times'. Therefore, if the denial of the consequent occurs, it must be at a time, and since implication covers all times, the implication would be false. So denying the validity of the inference amounts to denying the implication itself, which contradicts the assumption that the implication exists. This is a reductio ad absurdum (خلف). Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
الاستثنائي
لزوم
انفكاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Affirming the consequent
B) Denying the antecedent
C) Denying the consequent
D) Both affirming the antecedent and denying the antecedent

Key Takeaway
Conditional syllogism has two valid moods: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent (modus tollens); the reverse forms are invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the conditional: 'If an act is obligatory (wājib), then performing it is rewarded. Suppose we know that the act is not obligatory. What conclusion, if any, follows? Is this a valid inference according to the rules of conditional syllogism? Explain why or why not.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Resolving the Objection Against Denial of Consequent
Resolves objection about denial of consequent with temporal definition of implication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the objection that denying the consequential may be invalid if the consequent's negation is impossible, and describe the author's solution using the definition of implication as impossibility of separation at all times.

Core Idea
Implication means no separation at any time; thus, denying the consequent is always valid.

The author presents a subtle objection to the rule of modus tollens: if the consequent is something that cannot fail to exist (like a necessary truth), then its denial is impossible. If we suppose it occurs, the implication breaks, so the antecedent need not be denied. This objection challenges the universality of modus tollens. The author resolves it by clarifying that implication (luzūm) is defined as the impossibility of separation (infikāk) in all times. Therefore, any moment of separation contradicts the very definition of implication, making the objection self-defeating. This lesson helps you understand and defend a key logical rule often used in Islamic theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection states that if the consequent is necessarily true (its non-existence is impossible), then denying the consequent is impossible. If we hypothetically suppose it occurs, the implication (luzūm) no longer holds, so the antecedent does not necessarily become false.
The objection is based on the idea that implication might not be temporally universal.
2
Examine the author's definition of implication
The author defines luzūm as 'impossibility of separation (infikāk) in all times' (imtināʿ al-infikāk fī jamīʿ al-awqāt). This means that for any time t, it is impossible that the antecedent is true while the consequent is false.
This definition emphasizes temporal universality.
3
Apply the definition to the objection
Suppose the consequent is impossible to deny at all times. If we consider a time t when the denial of the consequent supposedly occurs, then at that time there is a separation (antecedent true, consequent false). But the definition says separation is impossible at all times. Hence, the objection reduces to denying that the implication holds at all times, contradicting the initial assumption that the implication exists. This is a reductio (khulf).
The author concludes: 'this denial reduces to a denial of the implication, which has been assumed to exist. This is a contradiction.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالاِسْتِثْنَائِيُّ يَتَرَكَّبُ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ : شَرْطِيَّةٍ، وَوَضْعِيَّةٍ أَوْ رَفْعِيَّةٍ، وَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ كَوْنِهَا مُوجَبَةً لُزُومِيَّةَ ، أَوْ عِنَادِيَّةً ، وَمِنْ كُلِّيَّةِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ أَوِ الاِسْتِثْنَاءِ، فَفِي الْمُتَّصِلَةِ يُنْتِجُ وَضْعُ الْمُقَدَّمِ وَضْعَ التَّالِي لِأَنَّ وُجُودَ الْمَلْزُومِ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِوُجُودِ اللَّازِمِ، وَلَا عَكْسَ؛ لِجَوَازِ أَعَمِّيَّةِ اللَّازِمِ ، وَرَفْعُ التَّالِي رَفْعَ الْمُقَدَّمِ؛ فَإِنَّ انْتِفَاءَ اللَّازِمِ يَسْتَلْزِمُ انْتِفَاءَ الْمَلْزُومِ، وَلَا عَكْسَ. وَهَهُنَا شَلٌّ ، وَقِيلَ عَوِيصٌ ، وَهُوَ مَنْعُ اسْتِلْزَامِ الرَّفْعِ الرَّفْعَ؛ لِجَوَازِ اسْتِحَالَةِ انْتِفَاءِ اللَّازِمِ ، فَإِذَا وَقَعَ لَمْ يَبْقَ اللُّزُومُ مَعَهُ، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ انْتِفَاءُ الْمَلْزُومِ. أَقُولُ : حَلْهُ: إِنَّ اللُّزُومَ حَقِيقَةٌ امْتِنَاعُ الانْفِكَاكِ فِي جَمِيعِ الْأَوْقَاتِ، فَوَقْتُ الانْفِكَاكِ - وَهُوَ وَقْتُ عَدَمِ بَقَاءِ اللُّزُومِ - دَاخِل فِي الْجَمِيعِ، فَهَذَا الْمَنْعُ يَرْجِعُ إِلَى مَنْعِ اللُّزُومِ، وَقَدْ فُرِضَ وُجُودُهُ ، هَذَا خُلْفٌ.
And the conditional syllogism (istiṯnāʾī) is composed of two premises: a conditional (šarṭiyya) and an affirmative (waḍʿiyya) or negative (rafʿiyya). It is necessary that the conditional be affirmative, necessary (luzūmiyya) or contradictory (ʿinādiyya), and that either the conditional or the exception be universal. So in the connected (muttaṣila), affirming the antecedent produces affirming the consequent, because the existence of the necessary (malzūm) entails the existence of the consequent (lāzim), and not vice versa, due to the possibility of the consequent being more general. And denying the consequent produces denying the antecedent, because the non-existence of the consequent entails the non-existence of the necessary, and not vice versa. And here there is a difficulty, it is said to be a knotty problem: it is denying that the denial [of the consequent] entails the denial [of the antecedent], due to the possibility of the non-existence of the consequent being impossible. For if it occurs, the implication no longer remains with it, so the non-existence of the necessary does not follow. I say: its solution: implication (luzūm) is in reality the impossibility of separation (infikāk) at all times. So the time of separation—which is the time when the implication no longer holds—is included in 'all times'. So this denial reduces to a denial of the implication, which has been assumed to exist. This is a contradiction (ḵulf).
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage explains the structure of conditional syllogism (الاستثنائي). It consists of a conditional premise (شرطية) and either an affirmation (وضعية) or a denial (رفعية) of one part. The conditional must be affirmative and either necessary (لزومية) or contradictory (عنادية, meaning an exclusive disjunction?). Both the conditional and the exception must be universal. For the connected conditional (المتصلة), affirming the antecedent yields the consequent, because the existence of the necessary (malzūm) implies the existence of the consequent (lāzim). The reverse is invalid because the consequent might be more general. Denying the consequent yields denying the antecedent, because the non-existence of the consequent implies the non-existence of the necessary; again, the reverse is invalid. Then the author raises a famous difficulty (شلّ): denying the consequent does not necessarily entail denying the antecedent if the non-existence of the consequent is impossible. For example, if the consequent is something that must exist (like existence itself), then its denial is impossible, and when we suppose it occurs, the implication no longer holds, so the antecedent does not have to be denied. The author's solution: implication (لزوم) is defined as the impossibility of separation (انفكاك) in all times. The time when the separation occurs—i.e., when the implication fails—is itself included in 'all times'. Therefore, if the denial of the consequent occurs, it must be at a time, and since implication covers all times, the implication would be false. So denying the validity of the inference amounts to denying the implication itself, which contradicts the assumption that the implication exists. This is a reductio ad absurdum (خلف). Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
الاستثنائي
لزوم
انفكاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the author's key move in resolving the objection against denying the consequent?
A) He says the objection only applies to disjunctive conditionals
B) He redefines implication as impossibility of separation at all times
C) He argues that the consequent cannot be impossible
D) He says the objection is irrelevant because modus tollens is always valid

Key Takeaway
The objection to modus tollens collapses when implication is correctly defined as impossibility of separation at all times, making the inference universally valid.

Inference Rules for Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author states the inference rules for disjunctive syllogism with its three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of disjunctive propositions and determine which inferences (affirmation/denial) are valid for each type.

Core Idea
Each disjunction type has specific valid inferences: exclusive, inclusive, or both.

Disjunctive syllogisms are powerful tools in Islamic jurisprudence, used to narrow down options and deduce rulings. The classical logicians distinguished three types of disjunction: exclusive (mani'at al-jam'), inclusive (mani'at al-khuluw), and real (al-haqiqiyya). Each type permits different patterns of inference when we affirm or deny one disjunct. This lesson unpacks those rules as stated by the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of disjunction
From the passage, list the three types: exclusive (مانعة الجمع), inclusive (مانعة الخلو), and real (الحقيقية). Understand their definitions as given in the key terms.
Exclusive: both cannot be true, but can be false. Inclusive: both cannot be false, but can be true. Real: exactly one is true.
2
Extract the inference rules for each type
The passage says: 'affirmation produces denial, as in exclusive; and denial produces affirmation, as in inclusive; and the real produces the four conclusions.' Restate: For exclusive, if you affirm one disjunct, you may deny the other. For inclusive, if you deny one disjunct, you may affirm the other. For real, both directions are valid.
The 'four conclusions' refer to: affirming one → denying the other; denying one → affirming the other; and the converses (which are the same).
3
Apply the rules to a concrete example from fiqh
Consider: 'This act is either wajib or mubah.' Determine the type of disjunction. Then ask: If we know it is wajib, what follows? If we know it is not wajib, what follows? Use the rules from the passage.
If the act cannot be both wajib and mubah (exclusive), then affirming wajib leads to denying mubah. If it must be one of the two (inclusive), then denying wajib leads to affirming mubah. Real would allow both.
4
Test understanding with a non-valid inference
The passage implies that for exclusive disjunction, denying one disjunct does not validly affirm the other (since both could be false). Construct a counterexample: 'Either this liquid is wine or it is water.' If it is not wine, can we conclude it is water? Not necessarily, because it could be milk. This shows the limitation.
This matches the rule: only affirmation leads to denial in exclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَفِي الْمُنْفَصِلَةِ يُنْتِجُ الْوَضْعُ الرَّفْعَ ، كَمَانِعَةِ الْجَمْعِ، وَالرَّفْعُ الْوَضْعَ، كَمَانِعَةِ الْخُلُوِّ ، وَالْحَقِيقِيَّةُ تُنْتِجُ النَّتَائِجَ الْأَرْبَعَ.
And in the disjointed (munfaṣila), affirmation produces denial, as in manʿ al-jamʿ (exclusive); and denial produces affirmation, as in manʿ al-ḵuluw (inclusive); and the real (ḥaqīqiyya) produces the four conclusions.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage gives the rules for disjunctive syllogisms (المنفصلة). Three types are distinguished: 1. Mani'at al-jam' (exclusive disjunction): affirms one, denies the other. 'Either A or B, not both.' So if you affirm one, you deny the other; if you deny one, nothing follows about the other (because it could be both false in exclusive? Actually in exclusive, if you deny one, the other must be true, but the text says 'affirmation produces denial' only; it doesn't mention denial producing affirmation for this type. The text says: affirmation produces denial (as in mani' al-jam'), and denial produces affirmation (as in mani' al-khuluww). So for exclusive, only the rule: affirming one yields denial of the other. Denial does not yield affirmation because in exclusive, both can be false? Actually in exclusive, both are mutually exclusive but not exhaustive; so if one is denied, the other may still be false. So only one direction is valid. 2. Mani'at al-khuluww (inclusive or): at least one is true, possibly both. So denial of one yields affirmation of the other. Affirmation does not yield denial because both could be true. 3. Al-Haqiqiyya (real disjunction): both exhaustive and exclusive. This produces four conclusions: affirming one entails denying the other, denying one entails affirming the other. The author states these succinctly.

Key Terms
المنفصلة
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
الحقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Affirmation of the other disjunct
C) Nothing follows
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
The type of disjunction determines which inferences are valid: exclusive allows only affirmation-to-denial, inclusive only denial-to-affirmation, and real allows both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
