Compound Syllogism and Reductio ad Absurdum
Author defines compound syllogism and identifies reductio as composed of categorical and conditional parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a compound syllogism and distinguish reductio ad absurdum as a combination of categorical and conditional syllogisms.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms chain simple syllogisms; reductio proves by contradiction.

In earlier lessons we studied simple categorical and conditional syllogisms. Now we encounter compound syllogisms, which link multiple simple syllogisms. The author introduces two types: linked conclusions and separated conclusions. He then highlights reductio ad absurdum (الخلف) as a key compound form that combines a categorical syllogism with a conditional one to prove a conclusion by refuting its opposite.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of compound syllogism
The passage says compound syllogisms have conclusions either linked (موصول النتائج) or separated (مفصول النتائج). 'Linked' means the conclusion of one becomes a premise for the next; 'separated' means each conclusion stands alone.
2
Understand the goal of reductio
Reductio aims to prove a desired proposition by invalidating its contradictory. That is, assume the opposite, derive a contradiction, then conclude the original.
3
Break reductio into its components
The passage states reductio reduces to a categorical syllogism (اقتراني) and a conditional syllogism (استثنائي). The categorical syllogism shows that the negation leads to an absurdity; the conditional syllogism then affirms the original via modus tollens.
4
Formalize the reductio structure
Write the argument: (1) If not-P, then Q (absurd). (2) Q is false. Therefore, not-P is false, so P is true. The first step is a categorical inference from not-P to a contradiction; the second is a conditional modus tollens.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْقِيَاسُ الْمُرَكَّبُ مَوْصُولُ التَّتَائِج أَوْ مَفْصُولُهَا أَقْسَةٌ. وَمِنْهُ الْخُلْفُ، وَهُوَ: مَا يُقْصَدُ فِيهِ إِثْبَاتُ الْمَطْلُوبِ بِإِبْطَالِ نَقِيضِهِ، وَمَرْجِعُهُ إِلَى اقْتِرَانِي وَاسْتِثْنَائِي.
And the compound syllogism (al-qiyās al-murakkab) has types with its conclusions either linked or separated. And among them is the reductio (al-ḵulf), which is that in which the aim is to prove the desired conclusion by invalidating its contradictory; and its reduction is to a categorical (iqtirānī) and a conditional (istiṯnāʾī).
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage defines the compound syllogism (القياس المركب). It is a chain of simple syllogisms. The conclusions can be linked (موصول النتائج) meaning the conclusion of one becomes a premise for the next, or separated (مفصول النتائج) meaning they are independent. Then the author mentions a specific type: reductio ad absurdum (الخلف). This is a proof that establishes a proposition by showing that its negation leads to a contradiction. The structure of reductio is reduced to two parts: a categorical syllogism (اقتراني) that derives an absurdity from the negation of the desired proposition, and a conditional syllogism (استثنائي) that concludes the original proposition by modus tollens: if the negation implies falsehood, then the original is true. Thus, the reductio combines both types.

Key Terms
القياس المركب
الخلف
اقتراني
استثنائي

C) True and false
D) Categorical and conditional

Key Takeaway
A compound syllogism chains simple syllogisms; reductio ad absurdum is a compound that proves a proposition by deriving a contradiction from its negation using a categorical then a conditional syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Impossibility of Negating the Consequent
Commentator solves a doubt about the impossibility of the consequent's negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why negating the consequent in a true conditional does not entail negating the antecedent, and identify the role of the definition of luzum in resolving this logical doubt.

Core Idea
Negating the consequent attacks the conditional itself, not just the antecedent.

This passage from a commentary on Sullam al-'Ulum addresses a subtle doubt about conditional syllogisms. The matn states that if the consequent is negated, the conditional relation no longer holds, so the antecedent is not necessarily false. The commentator clarifies the meaning of luzum (concomitance) as the impossibility of separation at all times, showing that the doubt collapses into a contradiction. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying conditional arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem in the matn
The matn claims 'impossibility of negating the consequent' and explains that if the consequent is negated, the concomitance does not remain, so the antecedent is not negated. This suggests that modus tollens is not valid if the conditional is strict.
The doubt is that one might think negating the consequent always negates the antecedent, but the matn warns against this.
2
Define luzum as the impossibility of separation
The commentator states: 'al-luzum is a reality of the impossibility of separation in all times.' That means if the antecedent is true, the consequent must be true at every time.
This definition is the key to resolving the doubt.
3
Analyze the doubt in light of the definition
If we assume the consequent is negated at some time, that time is a separation between antecedent and consequent. But the definition of luzum says separation is impossible at all times. Therefore, the occurrence of a separation means the luzum does not hold at that time.
So the doubt reduces to denying the luzum itself.
4
Recognize the contradiction
The commentator argues: 'This denial reduces to denial of the concomitance, while it has been assumed to exist; this is a contradiction (khulf).' Thus the doubt is invalid.
5
Apply the rule to conditional arguments
Therefore, in a true conditional (with strict luzum), if the consequent is false, the conditional must be false. Consequently, one cannot infer the negation of the antecedent without affirming the conditional's truth. This clarifies the proper use of modus tollens in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن سلم العلوم
اسْتِحَالَةِ انْتِفَاءِ اللَّازِمِ ، فَإِذَا وَقَعَ لَمْ يَبْقَ اللُّزُومُ مَعَهُ، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ انْتِفَاءُ الْمَلْزُومِ. أَقُولُ : حَلْهُ: إِنَّ اللُّزُومَ حَقِيقَةٌ امْتِنَاعُ الانْفِكَاكِ فِي جَمِيعِ الْأَوْقَاتِ، فَوَقْتُ الانْفِكَاكِ - وَهُوَ وَقْتُ عَدَمِ بَقَاءِ اللُّزُومِ - دَاخِل فِي الْجَمِيعِ، فَهَذَا الْمَنْعُ يَرْجِعُ إِلَى مَنْعِ اللُّزُومِ، وَقَدْ فُرِضَ وُجُودُهُ ، هَذَا خُلْفٌ.
Text of Sullam al-'Ulum: Impossibility of the negation of the consequent. So if it occurs, the concomitance does not remain with it, so the negation of the antecedent is not entailed. I say: its solution: The concomitance is a reality of the impossibility of separation in all times, so the time of separation — which is the time of the non-remaining of the concomitance — is included in the 'all', so this denial reduces to denial of the concomitance, while it has been assumed to exist; this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn line from Sullam al-'Ulum concerning conditional syllogisms. The matn states that negating the consequent of a conditional is impossible, meaning that if the consequent is false, the conditional relation no longer holds, so the antecedent is not necessarily false. The commentator explains that 'concomitance' (luzum) is defined as the impossibility of separation at all times. Therefore, if a time of separation occurs, that time is still included in 'all times'? Actually, the argument is that if we deny the consequent, we are denying that the conditional holds at this time, but the conditional was supposed to hold for all times. Thus, the denial of the consequent amounts to denying the conditional relation itself, which contradicts the assumption that the conditional holds. So the objection is invalid. This shows the rigor with which logicians treat the conditional.

Key Terms
اللُّزُوم
اللازِم
الملزوم

D) The affirmation of the conditional.

Key Takeaway
The negation of the consequent in a true conditional attacks the conditional itself, not the antecedent, because luzum requires inseparable connection at all times.

Resolving a Probability Paradox: Individual vs. Conjoint Antecedents
Author presents and solves a doubt concerning conflicting probabilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical error in probabilistic reasoning when the antecedent is treated as individual rather than conjunctive.

Core Idea
A probability paradox dissolves when we realize the antecedent must be conjoint, not individual.

This passage presents a subtle epistemological doubt about probability and conditional entailment. The scenario involves three persons, two Muslims and one non-Muslim, where initial probability suggests each is likely Muslim, but certainty about two specific ones seems to imply each is likely disbeliever. The resolution hinges on a careful distinction: the antecedent of the conditional must be the conjoint belief, not the individual beliefs. Understanding this distinction is crucial for sound reasoning in legal contexts like testimony or oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the initial probability
Three persons in a house: two Muslims, one disbeliever, identities unknown. By the majority rule, each person you see is probably Muslim. Note this as a background belief.
Majority rule: 2 out of 3 are Muslim, so each individual is 66% likely Muslim.
2
Recognize the conditional certainty
If you become certain that two specific individuals are Muslims, then the third is certainly a disbeliever (given the fixed composition). This is a valid conditional: certainty about two specific persons entails certainty about the third.
This conditional is true given the assumption that exactly two are Muslim.
3
Spot the erroneous inference in the doubt
The doubt argues: 'The opinion about the antecedent (here, the belief that a given person is Muslim) entails the opinion about the consequent (the belief that he is disbeliever).' This would make each person both probably Muslim and probably disbeliever, a contradiction. The error is that the antecedent in this inference is the individual belief about each person, not the conjoint belief about two specific persons.
The confusion is between the majority-based probability and the conditionally derived certainty.
4
Understand the solution: conjoint vs. individual antecedent
The author explains that for the opinion about the antecedent to entail the opinion about the consequent, one must believe that both antecedents (the two specific persons) are realized together. The individual beliefs do not entail the problematic conclusion. In this case, we only have individual beliefs, not the conjoint belief about two identified persons.
The solution avoids the paradox by clarifying which antecedent is relevant.
5
Apply the distinction to a real Islamic legal context
Consider qasamah: if two witnesses each testify that a different person is guilty, a judge must weigh probabilities. The conjoint antecedent (both witnesses testifying to the same person) has a different probabilistic impact than individual testimonies. Misidentifying the antecedent can lead to judicial error.
This logical tool is essential for ijtihad in courts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ ، وَهُوَ: أَنَّهُ إِذَا فُرِضَ فِي بَيْتٍ ثَلَاثَةٌ، اثْنَانِ مُسْلِمَانِ وَوَاحِدٌ كَافِرُ، لَكِنْ لَمْ تَعْلَمْ بِأَعْيَانِهِمْ، فَكُلُّ مَنْ تَرَاهُ مَظْنُونُ الْإِسْلَامِ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى قَاعِدَةِ الْأَغْلَبِيَّةِ ، وَكُلَّمَا تَيَقَّنْتَ بِإِسْلَامِ اثْنَيْنِ مِنْهُمْ عَلَى التَّعَيُّنِ تَيَقَّنَتَ بِكُفْرِ الْبَاقِي ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى الْفَرْضِ ، وَالظَّنُّ بِالْمَلْزُومِ يَسْتَلْزِمُ الظَّنَّ بِاللَّازِمِ، فَيَلْزَمُ أَنْ يَكُونَ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مَظْنُونَ الْكُفْرِ ، وَذَلِكَ مُنَافٍ لِمَا ثَبَتَ أَوَّلاً . وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّ الْمَلْزُومَ إِذَا كَانَ أَمْرَيْنِ فَلَا بُدَّ فِي اسْتِلْزَامِ ظَنِّهِ الظَّنَّ بِاللَّازِمِ أَنْ يَظُنَّ بِأَنَّ كِلَيْهِمَا مَعا مُتَحَقِّقٌ، لَا أَنْ يَظُنَّ بِكُلِّ وَاحِدٍ وَاحِدٍ بِالْفِرَادِهِ، وَالثَّانِي لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ الْأَوَّلَ ، وَالْمُتَحَقِّقُ فِيمَا نَحْنُ فِيهِ هُوَ الثَّانِي ، فَلَا مَحْذُورَ، فَتَفَكَّرْ . أَقُولُ: يَرِدُ عَلَيْهِ أَنَّ وُجُودَ الثَّالِثِ لَازِمٌ لِوُجُودِ الاِثْنَيْنِ، فَالْأَوَّلُ مُتَحَقِّقٌ كَالثَّانِي . فَإِنْ قُلْتَ الْمُتَحَقِّقُ مِنَ الثَّالِثِ مَا بَيْنَ أَحَادِهِ انْتِشَارُ، بِأَنْ يُلَاحَظَ وَاحِدٌ وَاحِدٌ ، وَالْمُسْتَلْزِمُ هُوَ مُلَاحَظَةُ الْآحَادِ مَعا ؟ قُلْتُ: مَلْزُومُ الْيَقِينِ هُوَ الْيَقِينُ بِالثَّالِثِ مُطْلَقًا، فَكِلَا الْقِسْمَيْنِ مَلْزُومٌ ، إِلَّا أَنْ يُقَالَ: لَا تَفَاوُتَ فِي صُورَتَيْ مَلْزُومِ الْيَقِينِ؛ لِعَدَمِ الْمُوجِبِ لِلانْتِشَارِ، بَلْ إِنَّمَا التَّفَاوُتُ بِالِاعْتِبَارِ ، وَأَمَّا مَا نَحْنُ فِيهِ فَبِخِلَافِ ذَلِكَ ، فَتَأَمَّلْ.
And here is a doubt: That if in a house there are three persons, two Muslims and one disbeliever, but you do not know their identities, then every person you see is thought to be Muslim based on the rule of majority; and whenever you are certain about the Islam of two of them specifically, you are certain of the disbelief of the remaining one based on the assumption; and the opinion about the antecedent entails the opinion about the consequent; so it would follow that each one is thought to be a disbeliever, and that contradicts what was first established. Its solution: That if the antecedent is two things, then for the opinion about it to entail the opinion about the consequent, one must think that both together are realized, not that one thinks of each individually; and the second does not entail the first, and what is realized in our case is the second, so there is no problem. Think. I say: An objection arises: that the existence of the third is necessary for the existence of the two, so the first is realized like the second. If you say: What is realized from the third is distributed among its individuals, such that each is considered individually, and the entailing factor is the consideration of the individuals together? I say: The antecedent of certainty is the certainty about the third absolutely, so both divisions are antecedent; unless it is said: There is no difference in the two forms of the antecedent of certainty due to the absence of a cause for distribution; rather, the difference is in consideration. But our case is otherwise. So ponder.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents a sophisticated epistemological doubt (shakk) about the interplay of probability and certainty. The scenario: three people in a house, two Muslims and one non-Muslim, identities unknown. By the principle of majority, each person is likely (ẓann) a Muslim. But if you become certain that two specific ones are Muslim, then the third is certainly a non-Muslim. Now, since 'opinion about the antecedent (here, the belief that any given person is a Muslim) entails opinion about the consequent' (some logical relation), it would seem that each person becomes likely to be a non-Muslim, contradicting the initial probability. The solution distinguishes the antecedent: it must be the conjoint belief that both conditions hold simultaneously, not the individual beliefs. In this case, the individual beliefs do not entail the problematic conclusion. Then the commentator raises a further objection: the existence of the third is necessary for the existence of the two, so both antecedents are realized. The response clarifies that the antecedent of certainty (the absolute certainty about the third) works differently. Distribution and focus matter. The key takeaway is the careful handling of probabilistic inference and the need to specify the antecedent appropriately. This is relevant to fiqh when estimating probabilities, like in qasamah or testimony.

Key Terms
الظن (al-ẓann): Probable opinion, as opposed to certainty; used in the context of belief about the religion of individuals. [Note: No significant shift.]
الملزوم (al-malzūm): The antecedent in a conditional relation; here it refers to the belief about the two known Muslims. [Note: No significant shift.]
اللازم (al-lāzim): The consequent; here it refers to the belief about the third person's disbelief. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key to solving the doubt about the three-person house?
A) The majority rule is invalid.
B) The antecedent must be considered as conjoint, not individual.
C) Certainty about two negates probability about the third.
D) The third person is certainly a disbeliever from the start.

Key Takeaway
When a conditional involves multiple antecedents, the inference from individual beliefs to the consequent is invalid unless those beliefs are conjunctively held.

Burhan Limmi and Burhan Inni: Causal and Non-Causal Demonstration
Author defines burhan limmi and inni by causality of middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between burhan limmi and burhan inni by evaluating whether the middle term is the cause of the judgment in reality.

Core Idea
The difference between limmi and inni is causality of the middle term, not certainty.

In Islamic logic, demonstrations are classified by the relationship of the middle term to the judgment. If the middle term is the real cause, it's a burhan limmi (a priori); if not, it's burhan inni (a posteriori). This distinction is crucial for assessing the strength of proofs in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of demonstration
The passage names burhan limmi and burhan inni. Notice the criterion: causality of the middle term. If the middle term is the cause of the judgment in reality, it is limmi; otherwise it is inni.
2
Examine the role of the middle term
In limmi, the middle term causes the predicate to apply to the subject. In inni, the middle term is not the cause; it could be an effect (called a sign) or unrelated.
3
Analyze the example given
The author provides the syllogism: 'Every body is composed; every composed thing has a composer.' The middle term 'composed' is the cause of the judgment 'has a composer' for the subject 'body'.
4
Understand the distinction's importance
The author emphasizes that in limmi, the middle term causes the major to be accepted for the minor, not itself. This avoids circularity and ensures a causal demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الْأَوْسَطُ إِنْ كَانَ عِلَّةً لِلْحُكْمِ فِي الْوَاقِعِ فَالْبُرْهَانُ لِمِّيٌّ، وَإِلَّا فَإِنِّيٌّ سَوَاءٌ كَانَ مَعْلُولًا لِلْحُكْمِ فِي الْوَاقِعِ، وَيُسَمَّى دَلِيلًا، أَوْ لَا. وَالِاسْتِدْلَالُ بِوُجُودِ الْمَعْلُولِ لِشَيْءٍ عَلَى أَنَّ لَهُ عِلَّةً مَّا، كَقَوْلِنَا: كُلُّ جِسْمٍ مُؤَلَّفٌ، وَكُلُّ مُؤَلَّفٍ فَلَهُ مُؤَلِّفٌ لِمِّيٌّ، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ؛ فَإِنَّ الْمُعْتَبَرَ فِي بُرْهَانِ اللَّمِّ عِلِّيَّةُ الْأَوْسَطِ وَمَا فِي حُكْمِهِ لِقَبُولِ الْأَكْبَرِ لِلْأَصْغَرِ، لَا لِقَبُولِهِ فِي نَفْسِهِ، وَبَيْنَهُمَا بَوْنٌ بَيِّنٌ.
Then the middle term: if it is the cause of the judgment in reality, then the demonstration is limmi (a priori). Otherwise it is inni (a posteriori), regardless of whether it is the effect of the judgment in reality and is called a sign, or not. And inferring from the existence of an effect of something that it has some cause, as our saying: every body is composed, and every composed thing has a composer—that is limmi, and it is correct; for what is considered in limmi demonstration is the causality of the middle term and what is in its ruling for the acceptance of the major term for the minor, not for its acceptance in itself, and there is a clear distinction between them.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage defines two types of demonstration: burhan limmi (a priori) and burhan inni (a posteriori). The criterion is the relationship of the middle term to the judgment. If the middle term is the real cause of the judgment (i.e., the reason why the predicate applies to the subject), then the demonstration is limmi. For example, in 'Every body is composed, and every composed thing has a composer', the middle term 'composed' is the cause of the judgment 'has a composer' for the subject 'body'. This is considered limmi because the causality of the middle term is what makes the major term applicable to the minor. If the middle term is not the cause, the demonstration is inni. It could be an effect (called a sign or dalil) or something else with no causal relation. The author stresses that the key in limmi demonstration is that the middle term is the cause for the acceptance of the major term for the minor, not that the middle term itself accepts the major (which would be circular). There is a clear distinction between the two: in limmi, the middle term is the cause; in inni, it is not.

Key Terms
برهان لمي
برهان إني
الأوسط
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It must be an effect of the judgment.
B) It must be the cause of the judgment in reality.
C) It must be known by sense perception.
D) It must be a frequent report.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between burhan limmi and burhan inni hinges on whether the middle term is the real cause of the judgment, not on the certainty of the conclusion.

Proof of Divine Simplicity via Composition Impossibility
Author proves God's simplicity by reductio of composition cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and validate the compound reductio argument for God's simplicity by analyzing the three cases of composition and identifying the contradiction in each.

Core Idea
If God were composite, every part-case leads to contradiction; thus God must be simple.

This passage from al-Qadi Mubarak's commentary on the matn demonstrates a rigorous logical proof for tawhid (divine oneness) by reductio ad absurdum. The author systematically considers every possible scenario of composition and shows each leads to an impossibility. You will walk through each case, understand the underlying syllogisms, and see how the proof refutes any notion of parts in God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim to be proven
The target claim is that God (al-Wajib) is absolutely simple—no mental or external parts. The proof proceeds by assuming the opposite (composition) and deriving a contradiction.
The claim is stated at the beginning: 'لا يُحد بالأجزاء الذهنية ولا الخارجية'.
2
Break down the reductio into three cases
The author splits the assumption of composition into three exhaustive possibilities for the parts: all possible, one necessary one possible, or both necessary. Each branch is analyzed separately.
This is a complete case analysis (taqsīm).
3
Analyze Case 1: Both parts possible (mumkin)
If all parts are contingent, then the composite would be contingent, not necessary—contradicting God's necessity. The argument uses: 'Everything composed of possibles is itself possible; God is not possible; therefore God is not composed of possibles.'
Implicit categorical syllogism: All composites of possibles are possible. God is not possible. Therefore God is not a composite of possibles.
4
Analyze Case 2: One necessary, one possible
If one part is necessary and the other possible, then the necessary part would be the true necessary, making God dependent on that part—so God would not be necessary, but rather the part would be. Contradiction.
This shows composition implies dependence (ʿilliyya), which is impossible for God.
5
Analyze Case 3: Both parts necessary
If both are necessary, they must be distinct in themselves (as necessary beings are unique). But then they cannot unite to form a single quiddity—composition fails. Additionally, the concept of 'necessary' shared by both would require a common ground, leading to further absurdity.
The extra argument about 'common concept' (mafhūm wāḥid) strengthens the contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن جملته قوله لا يُحد بالأجزاء الذهنية ولا الخارجية؛ لأنه بسيط ذهنا وخارجا، والبراهين على بساطته وجوه:
الأول أنه لو كان مركبا، فأجزاءه سواء كانت ذهنية أو خارجية إما أن تكون ممكنات، فمن أين الوجوب، أو أحدهما واجب والآخر ممكن، فيكون تعالى معلولا للجزء الواجب، متأخرا عنه، فيكون الواجب ذلك الجزء، فلا يكون ما فرض الواجب واجبا أو واجبات، فيكونان ممتازين في أنفسهما، كما هو شأن الواجب، فلا يمكن الاتحاد، حتى يكونا جزئين ذهنيين ومستغن أحدهما عن الآخر، فلا يلتئم منهما ماهية.
وأما الاقتصار على حديث الاستغناء، والقول بأنه خرق الفرض للانتهاء إلى أجزاء بسيطة فلا ينفيان إلا التركيب الخارجي، وأيضا ذانك الواجبان لو كانا فلا بد من أن يكون أمر واحد مشترك موجود يكون منشأ لانتزاع مفهوم الواجب الواحد المشترك؛ لأن الكثرة المحضة لا ينتزع منها الواحد بالضرورة، فلا بد مما به الامتياز، فلا يكون الواجب بنفسه ممتازا.
هذا خلف.
ومن ههنا تم برهان توحيد الواجب تعالى، واندفع الشبهة الشيطانية.
From among them is His saying: 'He is not defined by mental or external parts; because He is simple mentally and externally.' Proofs for His simplicity are several: First, if He were composite, then His parts—whether mental or external—would either be all possibles, so where does necessity come from? Or one is necessary and the other possible, then the Most High would be an effect of the necessary part, posterior to it, so that part would be the necessary, and what was assumed to be the necessary would not be necessary; or both are necessary, then they would be distinct in themselves, as is the case with the necessary, so union is impossible for them to be two mental parts and each independent of the other, so a quiddity would not be composed from them. As for limiting to the discourse of self-sufficiency and the claim that it violates the assumption because it leads to simple parts, they do not negate except external composition. Also, those two necessaries, if they existed, there must be a single common existent thing that is the source for the extraction of the concept of the single common necessary; because mere multiplicity does not necessarily yield the one, so there must be something by which differentiation occurs, so the necessary would not be distinct by itself. This is a contradiction. From here the proof of the oneness of the Necessary (Exalted) is completed, and the satanic doubt is repelled.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents the logical proof for the simplicity of God (tawhid) as a commentary on the matn statement that God is not defined by parts. The author, al-Qadi Mubarak, expands on the proof by considering the hypothesis that God were composite. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum (qiyas al-khalf). The first proof analyzes three cases: if both parts are possible, the composite would be contingent, not necessary; if one is necessary and the other possible, the necessary part would be the true necessary, not the composite; if both are necessary, they would be distinct and cannot unite to form a quiddity. All cases lead to contradiction. Then a further objection is addressed: one might think the proof only rejects external composition, but a deeper analysis shows that even if parts are simple, the concept of the one necessary would require a common ground, leading to infinite regress or distinctness, again a contradiction. The conclusion is that the proof of tawhid is complete, refuting the anthropomorphic view of Ibn Taymiyya.

Key Terms
بسيط
مركب
واجب
ممكن
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) They would be finite in number.
C) They would be the same as God.
D) They would be created by God.

Key Takeaway
A compound reductio argument, exhaustively analyzing all possible cases of composition, establishes the absolute simplicity of God beyond any doubt.

Evaluating a Proof That Every Aggregate Is Finite
Author presents a proof that every aggregate is finite and responds to objections about collective vs distributive totality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the syllogistic argument for the finitude of aggregates, identify the error in its major premise, and explain the distinction between collective and distributive wholes.

Core Idea
A proof that every aggregate is finite fails because it treats all aggregates as bounded, which is not necessarily true.

In this passage, the author presents a proof attributed to Suhrawardi that every aggregate is finite, then refutes it. You will learn to dissect the categorical syllogism underlying the proof and see how the objection about collective vs. distributive wholes points to a deeper logical flaw. This is a classic example of how precision in premises is critical in Islamic logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim
The claim is that every aggregate is finite because each part is bounded. Find the statement in the Arabic that expresses this. Look for the phrase 'الجملة متناهية'.
2
Reconstruct the syllogism in the first figure
The text suggests a deduction: 'The non-finite is an aggregate, and every aggregate is finite.' Formalize this as a categorical syllogism. What is the major premise, minor premise, and conclusion? Write the syllogism in standard form.
3
Analyze the objection regarding collective vs. distributive whole
The objector says the proof confuses the collective whole (al-kull al-majmūʿī) with the individual whole (al-kull al-afrādī). Explain what this distinction means in your own words. Consider: does 'every aggregate' refer to each part individually or to the whole as a collection?
4
Apply the author's correction
The author responds that the major premise 'every aggregate is finite' is false. Instead, only bounded aggregates are finite. Show how this correction affects the syllogism's validity and why the middle term is then not repeated. Check the condition for a valid first-figure syllogism: the middle term must be identical in both premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن كل جملة منها متناهية لأجل المحصورية، فالكل متناه. قال الشيخ المقتول : هو برهان عرشي ، وإلا فيرد عليه أنه قياس للكل المجموعي على الأفرادي. فإن قلت : بل استدلال بالشكل الأول، بأن غير المتناهي جملة، وكل جملة متناهية ؟ قلت الكبرى ممنوعة، والصحيح أن كل جملة محصورة متناهية، فحينئذ الأوسط غير متكرر. وقد وقع زيادة في «العجالة النافعة».
And among them is that every aggregate of it is finite due to being bounded, so the whole is finite. The murdered Sheikh said: It is an arshi proof, otherwise it is objected to it that it is a syllogism from the collective whole to the individual whole. If you say: Rather it is a deduction by the first figure: The non-finite is an aggregate, and every aggregate is finite? I say: The major premise is denied, and the correct is that every bounded aggregate is finite, then the middle term is not repeated. And an addition occurred in 'al-'Ajalah al-Nafi'ah'.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that every aggregate is finite because each part is bounded, and then addresses objections. The proof is attributed to the 'murdered Sheikh' (likely Suhrawardi) as an 'arshī proof'. The objection is that this proof confuses the collective whole (al-kull al-majmūʿī) with the distributive whole (al-kull al-afrādī). The author then reframes the proof in the first figure syllogism: 'The non-finite is an aggregate, and every aggregate is finite.' The response is that the major premise 'every aggregate is finite' is false; only bounded aggregates are finite. The note mentions an addition in another work.

Key Terms
الجملة
المحصورية
الكل المجموعي
برهان عرشي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A valid syllogism requires an identical middle term in both premises; the distinction between bounded and unbounded aggregates refutes the proof for the finitude of every aggregate.

8 min
Proof: Contradictories of Equal Terms Are Equal
The initial proof of the theorem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio proof that the contradictories of coextensive terms are themselves coextensive.

Core Idea
The equality of contradictories follows from the impossibility of both being false or both true.

The author proves a fundamental principle: if two terms A and B are coextensive (equal in extension), then their negations not-A and not-B are also coextensive. The proof uses a reductio argument based on the laws of non-contradiction and excluded middle. Let us walk through the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
The theorem states: If A and B are equal (i.e., every A is B and every B is A), then not-A and not-B are also equal.
2
Assume the opposite
Suppose not-A and not-B are not equal. Then they differ in truth: one is true of something where the other is false.
3
Apply the laws of contradiction
If one contradictory (say not-A) is true of something, then its opposite (A) is false. But the other contradictory (not-B) is false, so its opposite (B) is true.
We use the impossibility of both contradictories being false (excluded middle) and both being true (non-contradiction).
4
Derive the contradiction
Now we have A false and B true for the same thing, so A and B are not equal, contradicting the original assumption of equality.
5
Conclude the theorem
Therefore, the assumption of inequality leads to a contradiction, so the contradictories must be equal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَنَقِيضَا الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ مُتَسَاوِيَانِ، وَإِلَّا فَتَفَارَقَا في الصدق، فيصدق أحدهما بدون الآخر، فيصدق مع عين الآخر؛ لاستحالة ارتفاع النقيضين فلا يصدق عين ذلك النقيض؛ لاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين، فيلزم صدق أحد المتساويين بدون الآخر، هذا خلف. وههنا شك قوي، وهو أن الملازمة ممنوعة؛ لأن نقيض التصادق رفعه، لا صدق التفارق، وربما يكون نقيض المتساويين مما لا فرد له أصلاً في نفس الأمر، كنقائض المفاهيم الشاملة لكل مفهوم، فيصدق الأول (أي عدم التصادق) دون الثاني (أي التفارق)؛ لأنه عدم صدق أحدهما مع صدق الآخر. وقد يجاب بأن مرجع التساوي إلى عقدين غير بينين، فنقول: لو فرض صدق أحد نقيضي المتساويين على شيء، ولم يصدق النقيض الآخر يصدق عينه، إلى آخر الدليل وفيه نظر؛ لجواز كون صدق أحد النقيضين على شيء محالا، فيجوز أن لا يصدق النقيض الآخر ولا عينه على ذلك التقدير؛ لجواز استلزام المحال محالا. وأيضا يجوز أن يصدق عين النقيض المفروض الصدق على شيء عليه معه، ولم يصدق الآخر عليه، بل عينه لذلك، فلا يلزم صدق أحد المتساويين بدون الآخر. وما قيل: إن صدق السلب على شيء لا يقتضي وجوده؛ فإن صدقه لا يستلزم إلا صدق الموجبة السالبة المحمول، التي لا تستدعي وجود الموضوع، وحينئذ رفع التصادق يستلزم التفارق، فبعد تسليمه، فيه إشارة إلى ما سيجيئ في تحقيق المحصورات، من أن الموجبة السالبة المحمول
So the two contradictories of the two equal [terms] are equal; otherwise they would differ in truth, so one would be true without the other, and then it would be true together with the other's opposite, because it is impossible for two contradictories to rise together, so that opposite contradictory would not be true; and this would entail that one of the equal terms is true without the other, which is a contradiction. And here there is a strong doubt: the implication is denied because the contradictory of co-truthfulness is its removal, not the truth of difference, and perhaps the contradictory of the equal terms has no individual at all in reality, like contradictories of comprehensive concepts that encompass every concept. So the first (i.e., non-co-truthfulness) is true without the second (i.e., difference), because it is the non-truth of one with the truth of the other. And it may be answered that the reference of equality is to two non-evident terms; so we say: if we suppose that one of the two contradictories of the equal terms is true of something, and the other contradictory is not true, then its opposite would be true, etc. But there is reflection on this: because it is possible that the truth of one of the two contradictories about something is impossible, and then it is possible that neither the other contradictory nor its opposite is true in that estimation, because an impossibility may imply an impossibility. Also, it is possible that the opposite of the supposed contradictory is true of that thing together with it, and the other is not true, but its opposite, so it does not entail that one of the equal terms is true without the other. And what was said: that the truth of a negation about something does not require its existence; for its truth only entails the truth of the affirmative-with-negative-predicate, which does not require the existence of the subject. Then the removal of co-truthfulness entails difference. After accepting this, there is an indication to what will come later in the investigation of quantified propositions, that the affirmative-with-negative-predicate...
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage presents a complex logical argument about the contradictories of equal terms, followed by a strong doubt and several attempted resolutions. The author first proves that if two terms (A and B) are equal (coextensive), then their contradictories (not-A and not-B) must also be equal. The proof is by reductio: assume not-A and not-B differ in truth (one true where the other false). Then, due to the laws of contradiction and excluded middle, a contradiction arises regarding the original equal terms. This proof relies on the impossibility of both contradictories being false together (no truth gap) and both being true together (no overlap). However, a strong doubt is raised: the link between the difference in truth and the conclusion is denied. The contradictory of 'co-truthfulness' (tasāduq) is simply the absence of co-truthfulness (i.e., not both true), not the stronger 'difference' (tafāruq) where one is true and the other false. Moreover, if the contradictories themselves are concepts with no actual instances (e.g., 'non-concept' or 'non-thing'), then the logical relations may not hold. The first attempted answer tries to salvage the proof by assuming non-evident (non-bayyin) terms, but this is criticized because if one contradictory is impossible, the other may also be impossible, breaking the reasoning. Another point brings in the distinction between negative propositions that do not imply existence (sāliba maḥmūla) versus those that do. The discussion is inconclusive as the text breaks off, pointing ahead to a later section on quantified propositions. This passage shows the depth of critique within Islamic logic, where even basic principles are examined for subtle assumptions about existence and negation.

Key Terms
نقيض المتساويين
التصادق
التفارق
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why must the contradictories of equal terms be equal?
A) Because of the law of non-contradiction alone
B) Because assuming inequality leads to a contradiction with the original equality
C) Because the terms are always positive
D) Because they are defined as equal

Key Takeaway
The theorem that contradictories of equal terms are equal is proven by reducing the opposite assumption to a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a reductio proof for the claim: 'The contradictories of 'wajib' (obligatory) and 'fard' (mandatory) are equal' using the steps from the lesson.
?
Identify the steps of the proof in the following kalam argument: 'If 'existent' and 'thing' are equal, then 'non-existent' and 'non-thing' are equal.'

10 min
Strong Doubt: When Contradictories Have No Instances
The strong doubt and its critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical gap in the proof when contradictories have no instances and evaluate the attempted responses.

Core Idea
The proof fails if the contradictories are empty terms, because the link between co-truthfulness and difference breaks down.

A sharp doubt is raised against the proof. The link between the contradictory of co-truthfulness and actual difference is denied. Moreover, if the contradictories themselves are terms with no real instances, the logical relations may not hold. This lesson examines the doubt and the attempted solutions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt's claim
The doubt says the implication in the proof is not necessary: the contradictory of 'co-truthfulness' (tasāduq) is simply its negation (non-co-truthfulness), not the stronger 'difference' (tafāruq) where one is true and the other false.
2
Consider empty terms
If the contradictories have no individuals (e.g., the contradictory of a universal concept like 'concept' itself might be 'non-concept' which has no instances), then the inference from non-co-truth to difference may not hold.
Example: 'existent' vs. 'non-existent' – the latter may have no instances in some views.
3
Examine the first response
The answer attempts to salvage the proof by saying the equality refers to non-evident terms, but this is criticized because if one contradictory is impossible, the other may also be impossible.
4
Analyze the second response
The text mentions a distinction: the truth of a negation like 'not-A is true of x' does not require x to exist, which affects the reasoning. This ties to the concept of the affirmative-with-negative-predicate.
5
See the incomplete resolution
The passage ends pointing to a later discussion. Recognize that the doubt exposes a subtle assumption about existential import.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَنَقِيضَا الْمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ مُتَسَاوِيَانِ، وَإِلَّا فَتَفَارَقَا في الصدق، فيصدق أحدهما بدون الآخر، فيصدق مع عين الآخر؛ لاستحالة ارتفاع النقيضين فلا يصدق عين ذلك النقيض؛ لاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين، فيلزم صدق أحد المتساويين بدون الآخر، هذا خلف. وههنا شك قوي، وهو أن الملازمة ممنوعة؛ لأن نقيض التصادق رفعه، لا صدق التفارق، وربما يكون نقيض المتساويين مما لا فرد له أصلاً في نفس الأمر، كنقائض المفاهيم الشاملة لكل مفهوم، فيصدق الأول (أي عدم التصادق) دون الثاني (أي التفارق)؛ لأنه عدم صدق أحدهما مع صدق الآخر. وقد يجاب بأن مرجع التساوي إلى عقدين غير بينين، فنقول: لو فرض صدق أحد نقيضي المتساويين على شيء، ولم يصدق النقيض الآخر يصدق عينه، إلى آخر الدليل وفيه نظر؛ لجواز كون صدق أحد النقيضين على شيء محالا، فيجوز أن لا يصدق النقيض الآخر ولا عينه على ذلك التقدير؛ لجواز استلزام المحال محالا. وأيضا يجوز أن يصدق عين النقيض المفروض الصدق على شيء عليه معه، ولم يصدق الآخر عليه، بل عينه لذلك، فلا يلزم صدق أحد المتساويين بدون الآخر. وما قيل: إن صدق السلب على شيء لا يقتضي وجوده؛ فإن صدقه لا يستلزم إلا صدق الموجبة السالبة المحمول، التي لا تستدعي وجود الموضوع، وحينئذ رفع التصادق يستلزم التفارق، فبعد تسليمه، فيه إشارة إلى ما سيجيئ في تحقيق المحصورات، من أن الموجبة السالبة المحمول

Resolving the Objection: One Accident in Two Subjects?
Author addresses objection about a common point and defends the position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical exchange on the problem of a common point and evaluate the solution involving interpenetration (tadakhul) to defend the impossibility of one accident inhering in two subjects.

Core Idea
Two points can interpenetrate so that no single accident inheres in two distinct subjects.

This passage presents a dialectical objection: if a point is common to two lines, it would have two existences, implying one accident in two subjects—a logical impossibility. The commentator defends the position by introducing the concept of interpenetration (tadakhul), arguing that what appears as a single point is actually two distinct but coincident points. This refinement preserves the principle that an accident cannot inhere in two subjects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim
The commentator is defending the view that a common point does not entail one accident inhering in two subjects. Recognize this as the claim under attack.
The claim is implicit in the Shaykh's position and the majority view.
2
Locate the objection
The objector argues that if the point is common, it must exist with two distinct existences (one for each line), which is impossible for a single accident. Find this in the passage.
The objection is: 'يلزم أن يكون النقطة المشتركة بين الخطين موجودة بوجودين'.
3
Examine the escape via interpenetration
The commentator suggests that attachment to both lines occurs from the aspect of their union at start/end, not from being a single accident inhering in two subjects. This is the initial escape.
The term تداخل (tadakhul) is central here.
4
Unpack the final solution
The definitive answer is that there are two points (end of one line, start of another) that interpenetrate and are indistinguishable to the senses. Thus, no single accident inheres in two subjects.
The passage states: 'إن هناك نقطتين: أحدهما منتهى لخط، والآخر مبدأ لآخر، لكنهما تداخلا'.
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider a fiqh parallel: if an act is both mubah (permissible) and haram (forbidden) from different perspectives, does that mean one ruling inheres in one act? Use the concept of tadakhul to argue there are two distinct considerations.
This shows the tool's utility across disciplines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في «الحاشية»: يرد عليه أنه يلزم أن يكون النقطة المشتركة بين الخطين موجودة بوجودين ؛ فإن وجودها بهذا الخط غير وجودها لذلك الخط ، وقد عرفت معنى كلام الشيخ ، فلا يرد عليه شيء ، ثم قال: ويرد على الجمهور قیام عرض واحد بمحلين، وغاية التفصي عن الفريقين أن بطلان التالي ممنوع على تقدير التداخل ، فالنقطة الواحدة إنما تعرض الخطين من حيث اتحادهما في المبدأ والمنتهى، وتلك الحيثية مصححة ، وإن لم يعلم كيفية تصحيحه. والحق في الجواب أن يقال: إن هناك نقطتين: أحدهما منتهى لخط، والآخر مبدأ لآخر، لكنهما تداخلا، ولا تتميزان عند الحس، وأما النقطة المفروضة في وسط خط واحد فلا وجود لها فلا قيام، فليس هناك قيام عرض واحد بمحلين . فإن قلت: فحينئذ يلزم انتزاع عرض واحد من محلين ؟ قلت : ذلك باعتبارين ، ولا مضايقة فيه، والله أعلم بالصواب.
He said in the gloss: There is an objection against it that it would require that the point common to two lines exist with two existences; for its existence with this line is not its existence with that line. And you have known the meaning of the Shaykh's speech, so nothing is objected against him. Then he said: And there is an objection against the majority that one accident inheres in two subjects. The ultimate escape from the two groups is that the falsity of the consequent is not conceded on the assumption of interpenetration. For the single point only attaches to the two lines from the aspect of their union in the beginning and the end, and that aspect is a corrective, even if the manner of its correction is not known. And the truth in the answer is that there are two points: one is the end of a line, and the other is the beginning of another, but they interpenetrate and are not distinguished by sensation. As for a point supposed in the middle of a single line, it has no existence, so no inherence. So there is no case of one accident inhering in two subjects. If you say: Then it would require extracting one accident from two subjects? I say: That is under two considerations, and there is no constraint in it. And Allah knows best the truth.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response regarding the problem of a point common to two lines. The objection is that if an accident (the point) is common, it would have two existences (one for each line), which is impossible for a single thing. The author first notes that this does not refute the Shaykh's (Ibn Sina) view because his meaning was already understood. Then, for the majority view, another objection arises: one accident inhering in two subjects. The escape is to deny the falsity of the consequent if we allow interpenetration (تداخل) — the point is attached to both lines because they coincide at the start and end. The correct answer is that there are actually two distinct points (one endpoint of the first line, one start of the second) that interpenetrate and are not distinguishable to the senses. A point in the middle of a single line has no real existence, so there is no inherence of one accident in two subjects. A further question: what about extracting one accident from two subjects? The answer is that it is extracted under two different considerations (e.g., as endpoint and as start), so no contradiction arises. This refines the earlier position and defends the principle that an accident does not inhere in two distinct subjects as one.

Key Terms
تداخل
قيام عرض واحد بمحلين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the author's final answer to the objection about one point common to two lines?
A) The point is a single accident inhering in two subjects
B) There are two points that interpenetrate and are indistinguishable
C) The point has no existence at all
D) The point is only mental and not real

Key Takeaway
The problem of one accident inhering in two subjects is resolved by recognizing that what appears as one is actually two interpenetrating points, preserving the logical principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Reconstruct the dialectic: In your own words, state the original claim, the objection, the initial escape, and the final solution from the passage. Then, evaluate whether the final solution successfully defends the principle that one accident cannot inhere in two subjects.
?
2. Apply the same reasoning to a Fiqh example: The act of reciting Qur'an during sujud is sometimes considered permissible (mubah) from one perspective and recommended (mandub) from another. Does this mean one ruling (accident) inheres in one act? Use the concept of tadakhul to argue that there are two distinct considerations, so no contradiction.

The Principle that a Possible Cannot Entail an Impossible: Standard Proof and Objection
Author introduces and defends the famous premise

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the standard proof that a possible cannot entail an impossible and identify the objection based on the relativity of possibility.

Core Idea
A possible cannot entail an impossible because it could be separated from it — unless impossibility is relative.

The principle that a possible cannot entail an impossible is a foundation of modal logic. Its standard proof argues that if a possible thing entailed an impossible, the possible could exist without the impossible, which is a contradiction. However, a subtle objection arises: impossibility might be relative to the entailer. This lesson unpacks the proof and the objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle and its standard proof
Find where the author says: 'if the possible entailed the impossible by essence, then the entailer could be separated from the entailed, because the possible is receptive to existence while the impossible is not.' This is the core argument.
2
Understand the objection's key distinction
The objection points out that separation would only be necessary if the impossible is impossible with respect to the entailer (i.e., relative to it). But the impossibility might be intrinsic (by essence) without affecting the possibility of the entailer.
The author mentions: 'impossibility by essence does not contradict possibility with respect to another.'
3
Reformulate the principle with nuance
The principle holds only when the entailed is impossible both in itself and with respect to the entailer. If the impossibility is only relative (e.g., an effect is possible in itself but necessary relative to its cause), the possible can entail it without contradiction.
This nuance is crucial for later theological applications.

اعلم أنه قد اشتهر هذه المقدمة، واستدل عليها بأنه لو استلزم الممكن محالا بالذات لأمكن انفكاك الملزوم عن اللازم؛ لأن الممكن الملزوم غير آب عن قبول الوجود لإمكانه، والمحال اللازم أباه. ويرد عليه ورودا ظاهرا أن الانفكاك إنما يلزم لو كان اللازم محالا بالنظر إلى الملزوم، وهو ممنوع، واستحالة الوجود بالذات لا تنافي الإمكان بالنظر إلى الغير، بل الوجوب بالنظر إليه. وأيضا لو كان الأمر كما هو المشهور، لما جاز كون كل ممكن معلولا للواجب، وإلا فعدمه الممكن يستلزم محالا بالذات، وهو عدم الواجب. وأجيب تارة بأن عدم المعلول إنما يستلزم عدم علية الواجب، لا عدم ذاته إلا بالعرض، ولا يخفى ما فيه؛ فإن جاعليته تعالى ضرورية له، وعدمها محال؛ لأن الواجب واجب مع جميع جهاته، على أن عدم معلوله تعالى يستلزم عدم العلية، وهو يستلزم عدم ذاته تعالى عنه؛ لأن انتفاء اللازم يستلزم انتفاء الملزوم. وأيضا يستلزم عدم العلية عدم العلة فهو مستلزم له، وإنكاره مكابرة. وتارة بأن عدم المعلول إنما يستلزم عدم العلة؛ لامتناعه بالواجب، أما بالنظر إلى ذاته فكلا، وإنما يحكم باستحالة المستلزم للمحال إن كان بالنظر إلى ذاته. ولا يخفى أن عدم المعلول ملزوم لوصف المعلولية، وهي ملزوم جاعلية عدم الواجب، وهي ملزومة لنفس العدم؛ لأن وصف الجاعلية ضروري لما هو جاعل، كما نص عليه الشيخ، فلزم أن يكون عدم المعلول الممكن ملزوما لعدم الواجب المحال. فإن قلت: فعلى هذا لا يكون المحال لازما للممكن بالذات، بل بالعرض، ولا ضير فيه؟ قلت: لا، بل بالذات إنما الوسائط وسائط في الثبوت؛ على ما يشهد به الضرورة، وأيضا شيء من الوسائط محال بالذات، أو كلها ممكنات، وعلى كلا التقديرين استلزام الممكن المحال. أما على الأول: فلأن الممكن يستلزم الوسائط المحالة، وأما على الثاني: فلأن الواسطة الممكنة استلزمت محالا.
Know that this premise has become famous, and it was argued for by [the claim that] if the possible entailed the impossible by essence, then the entailed could be separated from the entailer; because the possible entailer is not averse to receiving existence due to its possibility, while the impossible entailer refuses it. But it is objected to in an apparent way that separation would only be necessary if the impossible were [impossible] with respect to the entailer, which is denied; and the impossibility of existence by essence does not contradict possibility with respect to another, rather it necessitates [existence] with respect to that other. Also, if the matter were as it is famous, then it would not be permissible for every possible to be an effect of the Necessary, otherwise its possible non-existence would entail an impossible by essence, namely the non-existence of the Necessary. And it was answered sometimes that the non-existence of the effect only entails the non-existence of the causality of the Necessary, not His essence except accidentally, and it is not hidden what is in this; for His making [things] is necessary for Him, and its non-existence is impossible; because the Necessary is necessary in all His aspects. Moreover, the non-existence of His effect entails the non-existence of causality, which entails the non-existence of His essence, may He be exalted; because the negation of the necessary entails the negation of the necessitated. Also, the non-existence of causality entails the non-existence of the cause, so it is entailed by it, and denying it is obstinacy. And [it was answered] other times that the non-existence of the effect only entails the non-existence of the cause due to its impossibility by the Necessary; but with respect to itself, not at all; and the judgment of the impossibility of that which entails an impossible is only if it is with respect to itself. And it is not hidden that the non-existence of the effect is necessitator of the attribute of effect-hood, which is necessitator of the making [causality] of the non-existence of the Necessary, which is necessitator of non-existence itself; because the attribute of making is necessary for that which is a maker, as the Shaykh stated. So it follows that the non-existence of the possible effect is necessitator of the non-existence of the Necessary, which is impossible. If you say: then according to this, the impossible is not necessary for the possible by essence, but accidentally, and there is no harm in that? I say: No, rather by essence. The intermediaries are only intermediaries in subsistence, as necessity testifies. Also, some intermediaries are impossible by essence, or all are possible. On both assumptions, the possible entails the impossible. As for the first: because the possible entails the impossible intermediaries. As for the second: because the possible intermediary entailed an impossible.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a defence and refinement of a well-known logical principle: 'If a possible thing entailed an impossible thing by essence, then it would itself be impossible.' The author begins by presenting the famous argument: if a possible (e.g., the world) entailed an impossible (e.g., a self-contradictory state), then it would be possible to separate the entailer from the entailed, because the possible is receptive to existence while the impossible is not. But an objection is raised: separation only follows if the entailed is impossible relative to the entailer, which is denied. Impossibility by essence does not negate possibility with respect to something else; rather it entails necessity with respect to that something else. Then the author adds a theological case: if every possible were an effect of the Necessary, then the possible non-existence of an effect would entail the impossible non-existence of the Necessary. Two responses are given. The first response says the non-existence of the effect only entails the non-existence of God's causality, not His essence, except accidentally. The author criticizes this: God's attribute of causality is necessary for Him, so its non-existence is impossible. Moreover, the absence of causality entails the absence of the cause, which would be the non-existence of God. The second response says the non-existence of the effect only entails the non-existence of the cause due to the impossibility relative to the Necessary; but with respect to itself, it does not. The author then argues that the non-existence of the effect is the necessitator of the attribute of effect-hood, which necessitates the attribute of making (causality) of the non-existence of the Necessary, which itself necessitates non-existence. Thus the non-existence of the possible effect necessitates the impossible non-existence of the Necessary. The objection that the impossible is only entailed accidentally is rebutted: the intermediaries are only intermediaries in subsistence, and they are either impossible or possible. In both cases, the possible entails the impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the central objection raised against the standard proof that a possible cannot entail an impossible?
A) The possible might be necessary.
B) Impossibility might be relative to the entailer.
C) The impossible might be possible.
D) The proof is circular.

Key Takeaway
The principle that a possible cannot entail an impossible holds only when the impossibility is both in itself and with respect to the entailer; relativity of possibility/complexity is crucial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a simple argument to show why a possible (like the contingent world) cannot entail an impossible (like a contradiction). Use the standard proof: if it did, the possible could be separated from the impossible.
?
Identify the objection in the following claim: 'An effect is possible in itself but necessary relative to its cause; therefore, a possible can entail a necessary, not an impossible.' How does this objection relate to the principle?
